iThab particular bundle of appetites, moods,
and yearnings known as Sam Walter Foss com-
menoed independent reaction to the Uni versa!
stimuli in 1858 ; so it has now been hard at wor c
litmus-papering for forty years. But it didn’i
take Foss forty years to get out of the wilderne
of why and whether into the land of a promisi
workaday philosophy. At about 25, or thei
abouts one imagines, he was settled in the ere
that —for himself and others —the wav
make the best of this-only-world-so-far-as-th
evidence-goes is to ‘keep a-goin,’ and ‘keep e
j yin yourself, and lift your horn an’ cry hullo
cheerfully to an out-of-luck comrade in the ranks
at Feggers-Away. Foss’s three books of vers
Back Country Poems,’ ‘Whiffs from Wil

xankee B.ade,’which (according to last vague

a its.
* * » *
Foss is no remarkable poet in the ordinar
ense; and his serious essays in verse read jus'i
ke any others of the millions of flat American
shoes of the Masters. But Foss wants to reach
nd touch the inarticulate, half-educated masses—
-16 crowds of honest, healthy people who liave to
ork so hard with their muscles that their brain
imam unfed, dormant, but who nevertheless
ave a good, instinctive comprehension of the
irtue.of effort, the shame of shirk, the tickle or
amor, and the thrill of pathos. Subtleties,
tiler of form or matter, lie beyond the ken of
osss audience; and he doesn’t bother with per-
ct rhymes or regular cadences : he is not eompe-
nc to interpret the loftiest messages of Thought
e sweetest visions of Beauty. Down oil the
oadest plane of humanity he deals with
imanity’s simplest affairs, most primitive
lotions. Naturally his fiddle has few strings
it they vibrate strongly. Withal Foss is
mething_ of a ‘faker.’ Apparently lie left the
ternal New-Hampshire farm too young to
quire the most intimate acquaintance with
ral life : he is not so keen and capable a
untry man as Whitcomb Riley, for example:
t he utilises his acquaintance to the farthest it
H go. It is an easy way of verse-building to
te, as Foss takes, some familiar farmyard
m and educe a general application. Foss
ines specially in the quaint and pithy homeli-
-38 with which he states and illustrates
i theme. An amalgam of W. T. Goodge and
snry Lawson would make something very
e a Foss. Singly Lawson is too serious; Goodge
lot serious enough. Foss's picture prefixed to
3 of his books shows an energetic Yankee with
iroad head and what looks like a, streak of
tch in it: stoutish, merryish, alert and alive—
>d fellow, apparently. And, in response to
ders who have been inspirited by fragments of
is’s meat to ask for more, here is a whole page

A MODERN MALTHUSIAN.
I can’t git no job ;
’Tain't no good to try.
There is too many born,
An’ there ain’t enough die )
There’s too big a crowd
Fer a man to wedge in.
I can’t find no job,
An’ I sha’n’t try ag’in ;
You oan’t git no job
In the kentry or town.
There is too many folks in the worl’,
An’ there ain’t enough jobs to go roun*.
Wen the worl’ wuz cut out,
’Twas out out too small;
'Twarn’t made big enough
Fer its purpose at all.
The crowd is jammed in,
In a terrible cram ;
Be*t thing you can do
Is git out er the jam.
So I’ve crawled from the crowd,
An’ I’ve jest settled down.
There is too many folks in the worl’,
An’ there ain’t enough jobs to go roun’.
My talents is large,
But they’ve no room to grow ;
The worl’ is too small.
An’ they don’t get no show.
‘An’,’ sez I to myself,
* You. Sempronius Lang,
Clear out er this mob,
An’git out er this gang ;
For the mob’ll jest crowd,
An’jest trample ye down.
There is too many f >lks in the worl’,
An’ there ain’t enough jobs to go roun’/
An’ it don’t do no good,
An’ I ain’t goin’ to look,
Fer all places is filled,
An’ the jobs is all took.
The worl’ it wuz built
On a too narrer plan ;
So I’m a shut-out,
An’ a left-over man.
So what is the good
For to rush up an’ down ?
There is too many folks in the worl',
An there ain’t enough johs to go roun'.
There wuz jest one job left
In Bill Green’B cotton-mill}
All the one I could find
In the hull worl’ to fill.
But I’ve sieh a big heart,
This one job of my life,
I jest give it up,
Gen’rous-like, to my wife;
An' there ain’t no more jobs,
■So I’ve jest settled down.
There is too many folks in the worl’,
An’ there ain’t enough jobs to go roun*.

THU SPARE ROOM.
Our front room, it was furnished fair,
But closed to all the life of home j
A reservoir of mouldy air,
A corpseless catacomb.
A stern domestic quarantine
Scared childish footsteps from its door,
As if a powder magazine
Were kept beneath the floor.
But when our folks had company
The unused doors were opened wide,
And on the lavish luxury
We feasted open-eyed.
But we were strangers there, and henoe
A nervous terror flushed each cheek;
Before the grand magnificence
We dared not move or speak.
And so we sat in vague alarms,
And sighed for some supporting peg*
For our unnecessary arms
And our superfluous legs ;
We smiled our india-rubber smile,—
A long, perfunctory, muscular grin,
Which advertised to all outside
How bad we felt within.
Our hearts were in the barn at play,
Or played at tag about the shed ; ‘
Our bodies, statuettes of clay,
Sat in the parlor—dead.
In moveless suffering we sat on
And wept for back-yard haunts to roam.
As, by the brooks of Babylon,
The Hebrews wept for home.
In intellectual kitchens dole
Strong men their ohoioest life away,
And keep the front rooms of the soul
Unopened to the day.
They keep the pantry well-equipped.
The cellar they will never scant,—
The parlor is a darkened crypt
Without an occupant.
Henoe, blest is he who quits the quest
For wealth, or fame’s receding goal,
And every day returns for rest
To the front room of the soul.
Who lets the tempest rave and roll
Around him ; in his glad release,
Within the front room of the soul
He findeth perfect peace.

verges on the anti—but not for Foss’s audience
the melodrama-audience. He has made it, may

IS LITTLE 808 TUCKED INI
‘l’ve gotter go,’ she said, ‘an’ see
If little Bob’s tucked in ;
He II git his death if he’s uncovered
In this col’ storm an’ win’.’
'Oh, little Bob’s all right,’ said I,
‘ You’ve bin to tuok him in
Four times this evenin’, an’ I wouldn’
Run ’way upstairs ag’in,’
But Cynthy’d worry, fret, an’ stew,
An’ raise a dreflle din ;
‘W'y, I mus’ go ag’in,’ says she,
‘An’ see if Bob’s tucked in.'
‘ W’y, Cynthy, jest set down,’ I said,
‘ An’ git some good er life.
A feller wants a chance to talk
Some evenin’s with his wife.’
Then she would take her knittin’ out,
Or work upon her spread,
An’ make b’lieve lissen, though she didn't
Hear quarter what I said.
She wouldn’t much more than git sit down
Than jump right up ag’in,
An’ say, ‘ I mus’ run up an’ see
If little Bob’s tucked in.’
Young Bob was alius on the jump,
An’ filled the house with din,
An’ kicked his quilts off ev’ry night
Fast as she tucked him in.
His Inigs they went so fast all day,
As long as it was light,
An’ got up speed so they couldn’ stop,
An’ kep’ a-goin’ all night.
So Cynthy'd keep a-gittin’ up
An’ gittin’ up ag’iu ;
‘l’ve gotter look an’ see,' says she,
‘ If little Bob’s tucked In.’
She stood above the coffin there,
She bent to kiss his face,
To pat a stragglin' ourl of hair,
Or fix a bit of lace.
Her heart was breakin’ with the thought
That Bob, so round an’ fat,
So full of pranks and fun, should sleep
Within a crib like that;
But still she’d fix his little robe,
An’ then come back ag’in,
An take a long, last look, an’ see
Her little Bob tucked in.
That night a storm er snow came on,
An’ how the winds did rave !
The snow fell, like a coverlid,
On little Bob’s new grave.
‘ I m glad it snows,’ his mother said,
‘ It looked so hard an’ bare,
So hard, so cruel, an’ so bleak,
I cried to leave him there.
But God has sent the blessed snow,
I think—an’ 'tis no sin—
That he has sent His snow to see
That little Bob’s tucked in.’

cular-instance : half Foss’s virtue lies in this
deductive knack.

* XJNCLE SE'L'K ON KINGS,
Them kings in Europe over there are settin’ on their
thrones,
Their thrones built on the necks of men for their founda-
tion-stones ;
But trod-on men, I’m ;>lad to say, have learned to squirm
an’ creep,
They re willin' ; soon you’ll see them thrones come
tumblin’ in a heap.
Support my soldiers,’ says them kings, ‘my men who
shoot an’ hack ; ’
Till now each peasant carries roun’ a soldier on his back.
But that poor peasant’s growin’wise; there’s fire in his
blood.
Just wait a bit; you’ll see him dump that soldier in the
mud.
Theie s men across that bound’ry line that you must go
an’ kill ;
Go shoot em for us,’ says them kings, ‘go stab ’em ; ’tis
our will.’
all, kings, bimeby them men will say, ‘we don’t ob-
serve no sign
Thet men are vipers to be killed across that bound’ry
line.
If you want butchers to kill beeves, a bargain might be
made ;
If you want butchers to kill men, w’y, that ar ain’t our
trade.
If you want blood by hogsheadsful, don’t seek it at our
store ;
bor we ain’t killin' feller-men an’ brothers any more.’
Hall, kings, this ain’t the kind er talk to soothe a royal
ear,
But jest erbout the kin’ er stuff thet you hev got ter
hear;
lor we ve about made up our minds to lay you on the
shelf,
lor each man now hez come to know thet he’s a king
himself.
The kin er king that Europe wants won’t wear no jewel
crown.
An’ he is coinin’ w’en your thrones hev all been rattled
down.
He 11 wear a hat like other men, an’ set on a plain chair ;
But he will be a king er men, an’ rule ’em everywhere.
Notw’at he wears outside his head will be his kingly
pride;
Not w’at he wears outside his head, but w’at he wears
inside.
He’ll want no throne; a king er men can alius rule his
own
If he sets upon a nail-kaig, jest as well as on a throne.
He’ll say, this king that’s coinin’, to his soldiers old an'
new,
Break ranks, my frien’s; disband; go home; ain’t no
more work for you.
I legalize no more the art of takin’ people’s lives.
Your job hez gone ; break ranks; disband ; go home an*
see your wives.
‘ An’ beat your sword-blades into scythes. Go home an’
cut your grain ;
Make green with corn and w'ite with wheat the blood-red
battle plain. •
Though mowin’oats an’mowin’ grass is tiresome work,
but then,
Tis more respectable an’ clean than mowin’ feller-men.’
This is the kin’ er king we want; the ol’ style breed of
kings,
W’y, they hev bungled long enough, an’ made a botch of
things.
This king is cornin’ by an’ by, an’ let the roun' sky ring,
‘Hip, hip, hooray I hip, hip, hooray! God save, God
save the king I ’

In Melbourne, recently, Robert Brown,
carpenter, fell 40ft. off a ladder, through a gal-
vanised-iron verandah, on to an ex-M.P., and then
rolled down the steps of an adjacent public-house
cellar. But when people went down to pick up
Bob Brown’s pieces they found him dusting his
jacket, and swearing because he’d torn his trousers !
A lunatic from Bairnsdale (Vic.) was re-
cently sent to the asylum. He had attacked nurses
and doctors, and played up generally, but his
sanity was first really doubted when he was found
engaged in washing the Bible in a bucket of water.
Yet there are a good many things in the Scripture
which badly want washing.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An Easter Hymn.
The long-neck spurs are idle, and the shoes are off
the brown ;
The saddle-hacks are spelling at the back of
Boona Range;
The engine’s fired and waiting, and it’s hey for
Sydney Town !
The sheep can go to blazes, for the shepherds
want a change.
A twelve-month we’ve been toiling, as the best of
sheep-men must,
Playing games of woolly billiards, with a pine-
stick for a cue,
And wearing down the rollers of our long-necks in
the dust,
And saying little prayers to the gentle full-
mouth ewe!
Now —the roaring of the traffic and the flashing of
the lights !
Now—the ships upon the harbor and the crowds
upon the quay !
Now —the revel of the mornings and the riot of
the nights !
The long-worn fetters broken and—the freedom
of the free !
The crowds are keeping Easter and the shrines
are overflowed :
We shall trouble not the churches or the chapels,
comrades mine,
There are other altars hold us on the broad and
flowery road,
And the gods we go to worship are the Gods of
Love and Wine!
In a fortnight you’ll be weary of the streets that
slack and fill,
Of the nights of restless revel and the noons of
languid ease,
And you’ll wish that you were riding at the back
of Boona Hill,
With the miljee at your stirrups and the gooma
at your knees.
You can ride the bucking harbor when the tide
rips thro’ the Heads,
You can mount the moving tram-cars in a smoke
too thick to see,
You can mash the merry barmaids, you can “ do ”
the show-yard sheds—
A gallop down the Yarran flats is good enough
for me.
N.S.W.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
On the Sands.
All the air was tranced and the sea was stilled,
And we stood and dreamed of a world to be ;
When it seemed to me that our souls were thrilled
With a sudden sympathy :
My life’s long riddle at last I read,
And the spirit-face I had sought I knew ;
All the past’s far years to this hour had led
On the sands alone with you :
And you —you thought that the skies were fair,
And such twilight peace you had seldom known ;
And you never guessed that a soul was there
That hungered for your own.
You never knew —there was just the lack
Of a passioned look that would thrill me
through ;
But the night swept down, with its shroud of
black—
And you never, never knew !
M.L.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
Prometheus Proletaire.
If tears at times unbidden rise
Ye need not ask me why they come ;
I never weep o’er woes of mine,
On private griefs my voice is dumb.
But when I see the forms I love
In bitter need around me stand,
And see myself without the power
To aid them strain my fettered hand ;
’Tis this that slowly breaks my heart,
’Tis this that rends my soul in twain—
To gaze from out my gloomy night,
For Freedom pine—and pine in vain.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Conscience-Stricken.
It pleases me that you are not more wise,
For now you think me greater than I am,
And scan my life with simple, loving eyes,
Which may not see its hollowness and sham.
My loving tliee such great delight doth give
That nought can harm the trust in which you live.
I pray that death will be the end of all,
That you may never know in after life
How poor a thing had held your soul in thrall,
And dared to claim your love as slave and wife.
Thy loving me doth give me such delight,
I fear to be made smaller in thy sight.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing u'ith Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But ive
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, APRIL 30, 1898.
The Reasons Why.
The Bulletin offers the following as a few
of the reasons why every Australian should
vote “ Yes ” at the Federal poll next June :
(1) It is agreed by all political parties that Federation
is desirable. Even the bitterest of Provincialists declare
this. The unanimity on this point is so unanimous as to
be almost incredible.
(2) There is no possible guarantee that by delay the
provinces will get a better Federal scheme than the
present one. They may get a better one, or a worse
one, or none at all. If the present scheme is rejected,
events may happen to so rouse up provincial animosities
that it will be 100 years before there is another serious
attempt at union.
(3) It is claimed that it is a small matter if the present
dratt Constitution is rejected, because the natural growth
of the Federal sentiment Will bring union to pass all the
same. In that case the Bill should he accepted. If the
Federal and brotherly sentiment is growing, as alleged,
that is a guarantee that whatever defects there are in the
Constitution can be easily amended later on.
(4) The community which waits for a perfect Consti-
tution before it starts never will get started at all.
(5) The community which waits to unite till it gets a
Constitution which will please everybody—ultra-Free-
traders, ultra-Protectionists, hard-shell Tories, advanced
Democrats and Socialists, will never unite.
(6) The opposition to the Constitution Bill includes
Tories who claim that it is too Democratic ; Democrats
who claim that it is too Tory ; Protectionists who claim
that it commits the country to Foreign-trade ; Foreign-
traders who claim that it commits the country to Protec-
tion ; small, mangy politicians who have no'prospect of
getting into the Federal Parliament, and who fear that
their local Parliaments will be reduced to insignificance ;
property-owners who want an assurance that the Federal
capital will be located in their city, in the hope that the
consequent increase of population will enhance the value
of dirt and bricks and mortar; and the bang-crash
variety of politicians who can’t construct anything, and
who have to pull down what others have constructed in
order to keep themselves befofe the public. And when
so many miscellaneous characters are against the Bill,
they furnish at least a negative probability that it is a
reasonable measure, which holds the balance as fairly as
possible among all parties.
• * * •
(7) The proposed Constitution provides for the amalga-
tion of the five separate military departments, which will
make the defences of Australia cheaper as well as more
efficient.
(8) It will combine the posts and telegraphs of five pro-
vinces, thus making a great reduction in expenses, saving
four out of five Postmasters-General, and making rates
uniform throughout the Commonwealth.
(9) It will create a uniform tariff outside the Common-
wealth, and absolute Freetrade inside. All the costly and
troublesome paraphernalia of Border-customs and cus-
toms-officers will be swept away. By making trade abso-
lutely free over an entire continent (for the ultimate
adhesion of Queensland is inevitable, and the special
arrangement with Westralia lasts only five years), it will
be the greatest Freetrade movement which the world has
seen this century. The Freetrader who is against Fede-
ration under such circumstances is an anomaly.
(10) The Commonwealth Bill gives power to the Fede-
ration to take over the State debts. And as the Federal
Government’s credit will be better than that of any in-
dividual State the Commonwealth will be able to con-
solidate the debts at reduced interest, and thus save,
probably, £1,000,000 a year. This will be an immense
reduction of the public burdens. All the land and in-
come taxes of Australia are required solely to make up
this one sum which is annually fooled away under the
provincial system.
* o * *
(11) The Commonwealth Bill does not provide an
ideal Democratic Constitution. BUT it provides a more
Democratic Constitution than any now existing in the five
federating provinces, South Australia possibly excepted.
There is no nominee Council as in New South Wales ; no
property qualification for Councillors as is the case with
the elective Councils in Victoria, Tasmania and Westralia.
There is no plural vote-it is expressly stipulated that
in the elections for both Federal Houses each elector
shall have one vote and one only. And the Federal
Senate is liable to dissolution when it comes in collision
with the popular will, w hereas no Upper House in Aus-
tralasia is dissolvable. This gives the people for the first
time a firm control of the Upper House.
(12) The Federal franchise will be almost certainly the
most liberal in the world. The first Federal Parliament
will be elected on the provincial suffrages now existing,
with the reservation that no plural vote is allowed.
Therefore New South Wales, Victoria and South Australia
will elect their representatives on the principle of abso-
lute manhood (or, in South Australia, manhood and
womanhood) suffrage. These three Democratic provinces
will command an immense majority in both Houses—and
(hey will fix the Federal suffrage. Their people have
plus a chance to start afresfc themselves and to start Aus-
tralia afresh under absolutely Democratic conditions—
s uch a chance as they never had before and as few
Countries ever have.
(13) If New South Wales stands out. and leaves the
ot her four provinces to federate by themselves there will
he no reliable Democratic majority in the Federal Senate
to make a liberal suffrage absolutely certain.
(14) If New South Wales stands out and the present
movement fails it may not start again at all. Or it may
start again with all six provinces in it. And if Queens-
land is still under the present regime it will probably
make another Tory vote, and, again, there will be no sure.
Democratic majority in both Federal Houses to fix the
Federal suffrage. By a wonderful combination of cir-
cumstances the Democratic party has an absolutely sure
hold on both Federal Houses, if it only grasps the oppor-
tunity with both hands, and federates now. It can make
the suffrage the most liberal in the world. It can elect
the second Federal Parliament on the basis of an absolute
manhood vote, and then add the Referendum to the Con-
stitution, and thus make it wider and more Democratic
than it was originally. In fact, it can do anything if it
only seizes the opportunity while the combination of
three Liberal, as against only two Tory, provinces leaves
the power in its hands.
(15) The proposed Federal Constitution gives the
States, large and small, equal representation in the Senate.
But in the House of Representatives the States have
votes practically according to population. In theory
the equal vote in the Senate is an injury to the principle
of majority rule. In practice it is not—at least, not to
any extent worth mentioning -for a number of reasons.
(a) If any question should arise in which the three
small States, with their majority in the Senate, are
on one side while the two large ones, with their
majority in the House of Representatives, are on the
other, the House which speaks for the majority of
people has the advantage because the Ministry is
responsible to it only.
(b) It has this further advantage : If the Senate passes
a bill which the more popular House rejects, the
. Senate has no redress. If the popular House passes
a bill which the Senate rejects, it can obtain a dis-
solution of both Houses and an appeal to the people.
If the deadlock still continues, the popular House can
get the matter referred to a joint sitting of the two
Chambers, where a three fifths majority will carry
the bill in spite of the Senate’s veto. And New South
Wales and Victoria—the provinces which represent
the majority of people—have the three-fifths majority
and something over.
(c) Thus in the end the power rests with the more
populous States. The Federal Senate has no
absolute veto, whereas in New South Wales the
House of Nominees has an absolute veto, and so have
the Councils of the other provinces elected on a pro-
perty qualification. Therefore (despite the unfor-
tunate three-fifths majority provision) the Common-
wealth Parliament will be the only one Australia has
ever possessed where the people have a real control
over both Houses.
**x * *
(16) The Federal Constitution can be altered at any
time and in any way by the vote of a majority of both
Houses, a majority of the people, and a majority of the
States. The three Democratic provinces (New South
Wales, Victoria, and South Australia) command a majority
in all three respects, so fhe future of the Constitution
rests with the Democracy alone.
(17) If, by any possibility, the Federal union is a
failure, the way out is easy—easier than the way in. All
that is required is to amend the one word “ indissoluble ”
out of the preamble of the Constitution and to pass an
amendment declaring the union dissolved. To make the
union requires the consent of ten Houses of Parliament,
the assent of a majority of the total number of people
voting, the assent of five separate 'majorities in the indi-
vidual provinces, and it is also requisite that these
majorities shall comprise 142,000 votes. A vote of two
Houses of Parliament and the assent of New South Wales,
Victoria, and one of the small provinces will be sufficient
to dissolve it again. The allegation that the provinces
are taking a leap in the dark, which leap can never be
retraced, is mere foolishness and ignorance and premedi-
tated bunkum.
(18) The Federal Constitution leaves the fiscal question
absolutely open and the question of Freetrade or Protec-
tion is left to the people to decide—all the allegations
about “ Braddon’s Blot” to the contrary notwithstand-
ing. Braiiuon’s famous amendment provides that one-
fourth of the customs and excise revenue may be re-
tained by the Federal Government towards its own
expenses, and three-fourths must be returned to the
States. This leaves the Federal Government absolutely
free to pay its expenses out of direct taxation, or out of
customs and excise, or partly by one and partly by the
other. The meaningless Braddon amendment leaves
things, so far as concerns the fiscal question, exactly
where they are now. J
(19) The-Constitution does not, unfortunately, provide
definitely for the taking over of the railways by the
Commonwealth. But it gives the Commonwealth power
to take them over with the States’ consent. It is pro-
posed in some quarters that Federation should be post-
poned until the federalising of the railways is arranged
That also means waiting till the States‘consent And
there is no reason for waiting. The federated States will
consent just as soon and just as easily as the unfede-
rated States would.
* * * *
(20) The Constitution Bill prov ides Australia, for the
first tune, with what is practically a final Court of
Appeal of its own. Hitherto the final Court of Appwil
has been m England, 16,000 miles away, and justice has
been hideously slow and remote in consequence.
(21) It leaves the fixing of the Federal capital to the
federal Parliament the Parliament from which the pro-
perty vote is absolutely excluded.
(22) It introduces the principle of liberal payment of
members in both Houses, and abolishes all property
qualification for members. Unpaid Upper Houses with
propeity qualifications have hitherto been the stronghold
of Australian Toryism. The Federal scheme builds both
Houses on a Democratic foundation.
(23) Whatever is bad or deficient in the Constitution
it leaves the power absolutely with the people, and thev
can repair these faults or deficiencies as they please.
Equal representation in the Senate gives rather more
than a fair share of authority to the Tory provinces of
Tasmania and Westralia. It also gives rather more than
a fair share to South Australia-the most ultra-democratic
ATJhnw ;^i UStra^ a - whlch is a fair counterbalance.
Anyhow, the three Democratic States-South Australia,
Victoria, and New South Wales—have the balance of
power every time, and all the time, and if they can’t make
the Federal Constitution all that a Democratic Constitu-
tion should be they have only themselves to blame.
These are a few of the reasons why The
Bulletin gives its vote for the acceptance of
the proposed Constitution. It isn’t perfect,
but it is about the most liberal Constitution
in the world, and it is about as near perfec-
tion as any gathering of five separate States
is likely to get. There are two ways of get-
ting a better Constitution—one is by accept-
ing the present one and repairing it in the
Federal Parliament ; the other is by rejecting
it and doing the repairs in another Conven-
tion. The difference lies in the fact that in
the Federal Parliament the minority will be
governed by the vote of the majority, there-
fore it is possible to vote down the Tory
minority; in a purely voluntary Convention,
theminority, if it objects to thedecision of the
majority, can simply gather up its bag and
leave. Consequently, The Bulletin believes
in taking the Constitution as it is, and doing
needful repairs afterwards. To reject it for
the sake of remaining indefinitely under the
domination of the New South Wales House
of Nominees or the Victorian House of 48
Rich Landlords isn’t a cheerful prospect,
anyhow.
N.S.W. Free Newspaper Postage Swindle'
“ Under Federation there will be postage
on newspapers in New South Wales.” Now,
in N.S.W. newspapers go through the post
free. A grateful country carries the wares of
the newspaper proprietor gratis, while whisky,
and fat swine, and ironmonger}', and hair-re-
storers, and tonics, and Pale Beans for Boiled
People, and boots, and large iron boilers, which
are also articles of merchandise, have to pay
their way. The Government deliberately taxes
the people to pay the cost of carrying and de-
livering free the goods of the rich newspaper
owner by train and steamboat and coach, and
other conveyance. It doesn’t, however, extend
this privilege to papers published outside
N.S.W. The penny paper from any adjacent
province has to pay postage, therefore the
local Freetrade paper is protected up to 50 per
cent, against its beastly imported competitor.
The 50 per cent, protection on the Freetrade
paper is the only protective item which the
Freetrade press has never once denounced
as sinful and iniquitous and demoralising
and other like adjectives. And the 50 per cent.
Protection granted to the Herald and Tele-
graph and Evening News —which all support
the Reid Government—is the only kind of Pro-
tection that the Reid Government has never
cursed, or interfered with, or even alluded to.
The spectacle of the press which denounces Pro-
tection as an artificial coddling of weak, exotic
industries, and a robbery of the people for the
benefit of a few capitalists and monopolists,
being itself a protected and coddled monopolist
and robber of the people, is a trifle pitiful—but
things like that will happen.
* * * •
Newspapers are carried free in N.S. Wales
on the excuse that to charge postage on them
would be to “ lay a tax on knowledge.” But,
in the first place, it is not a question of taxing
knowledge, but of making it pay for services
rendered. In the second place, the N.S.W.
Government doesn’t care a straw whether
knowledge is taxed or not; if it did, it would
make education free, and extend the free-post-
age privilege to books, magazines, and news-
papers in general, instead of confining it to purely
local publications. The bald and obvious purpose
of the Government which granted the concession
was to buy the support of the local press. And
the bald and obvious purpose of succeeding
Governments, in continuing it, was to avoid
rousing the wrath of the press more than was
absolutely necessary.
* * * *
By a curious combination of circumstances,
however, the great scheme has gone very far
askew and a time is evidently coming when
even the daily papers will have to agitate for
the abolition of the big State subsidy they now
enjoy. In N.S.W., anything which is published
daily or weekly or fortnightly or monthly, is a
newspaper, and has to be carried free at the
public charge. Therefore, almost everybody in
a large way of business, and many who are in a
very small way, now find it cheaper to publish
their own advertising circulars, and circulate
them through the post at the public expense,
than to put their little advt. in the newspapers
as they used to do. N.S. Wales is full of
alleged newspapers, fully one-third of which
have no possible claim to the name, for they
supply no news of real public interest. About
half the churches now run a little organ apiece,
which gives a report of the Rev. Shem Howler’s

meeting and collection-plate items, and appeals
for better attendances, and reports of the open-
air meeting that was held in the school-room to
consider what color the church should be white-
washed. The following are a few sample names :

* * * V
The Bulletin wonders whether the cornstalk
Protestant knows that he is being heavily
taxed every year to hump great masses of Holy
Roman literature all over the country like this,
and to introduce transubstantiation and the
cult of St. Peter into every household. Also,
if the cornstalk Holy Roman knows that he
pays for the dissemination of all these tons of
heretic literature. And if the cornstalk agnos-
tic has dropped to the fact that he is paying to
subsidise the publications of all the sects whose
little snuffle-tracts appear in the above list.
* * • *
And even this is only the beginning of the
commencement of the trouble “and robbery.
There are in New South Wales jewellers,
butchers, tea-dealers, bakers, ironmongers,
bad debt-collectors, stock-agents, dealers ill
stamps, wholesale publicans, life-insurance
offices, and other traders who publish purely
advertising circulars for the purpose of putting
their private businesses and giving the prices of
their wares, and these are registered as news-
papers and carried at the expense of the nation
over 310,000 square miles of territory. One
individual who runs a marriage-bureau circu-
lates his little paper in the same fashion. So
does a person who feels bumps for a modest
consideration, his little advertising sheet being
called a “phrenological journal.” About a
dozen or twenty Cold-Tea publications, not in-
cluded in the above list of pious papers, are
carried free along with the public-house
organs with the latest whisky-quotations
in them. At recent elections a number of
candidates registered little ephemeral journals
containing reports of their meetings, and ac-
counts of the vast crow'd that wasn’t there, and
the applause which didn’t happen, and the
beautiful speech which was carefully revised
afterwards, and circulated them also at the
damage and injury of the national pocket.
And the time is evidently coming when the
push which aims bottles at the casual stranger
will issue little papers called Stoush orBLANKY,
and when the house of ill-fame will publish
a gazette called Flossie, or something of
the kind, with the portrait of the new girl
who has just come to stay there thrown in for
public information. In fact, there is absolutely
no limit to the great free-newspaper-postage
joke in New South Wales.
• * * *
It is the proud boast of the N.S. W. Joe Cook
Department that it carries 45,000,000 news-
papers annually, against only 30,000,000 carried
by the Postal Department of that miserable,
ignorant, Protectionist province, Victoria. But
when the 80 or more little church rags, and the
Matrimonial Gazette, and the bump-feeler’s
paper, and the stock-agents’ circulars, and the
dozen or twenty Cold-Tea organs, and the ad-
vertising sheets of three biglife-assuranceoffices,
and the circular of the man who collects bad
debts, and the political speeches, and the iron-
mongery and grocery and meat and sugar and
jewellery pamphlets, and the stamp-collector’s
price-list, and all the dozens and dozens of
similar literature which go through the post
free at the public cost, are reckoned in, it is
only wonderful that the alleged newspapers
mailed annually in N.S.W. don’t run to 400 mil-
lions instead of only 45. The people of N.S.W.
are apparently only just waking up to the vast
possibilities of their free newspaper-postage
system. Last year 65 new alleged journals
were registered in N.S.W., the great bulk of
them being trade-circulars, or parsonical sheets
calling for more liberality in the matter of the
Plate. And wdien printing and paper are cheap,
and labor is very cheap indeed, and the State
will deliver the beer or furniture or grocery
price-list as a new'spaper to every soul in
N.S.W. for absolutely nothing, there are great
prospects ahead for somebody or something.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Why, Cert’nly.
A provincialist person writes to The Bulle-
tin under the erroneous impression that he has
got a great idea :
Dear Bulletin.—Allow me to remind you that you have
visited me regularly onee a week for many years. We
have, as it were, grown up together, from youthful
Radicalism to middle-aged Respectability, strengthened
by Toryism. Presuming on our lengthy acquaintance-
ship, I venture to submit the following proposal, in the
sure and certain hope it will meet with your enthusiastic
approval. That The Bulletin and I should federate is
the one great ambition of my life. We are brother Aus-
tralians, with one destiny ; therefore let us federate. It
will add materially to my worldly prosperity, and also
satisfy the aspirations of my higher nature ; therefore let
us federate.
Tiik Bulletin is prosperous, with every prospect of
increased prosperity in the future. I am as free from
wealth as a frog is from feathers, and my future
prospects are in the keeping of the bailiff, but,
as a set-off to these drawbacks, I may mention
that I am The Bulletin’s Australian brother, and
also add that my grandparent did snare a rabbit in Hing-
land, and consequently spent the rest of his life in Lag-
land. From this lamented ancestor I have inherited a
profound respect for authority, also a not ungraceful
peculiarity in my walk. For these reasons The Bulletin
will readily admit I am entitled to eight times the voting
power of itself.
The terms of our Federation to be—
1. Both partners to reside in the same house, said
house to be erected and run at Bulletin expense.
2. Tiie Bulletin to chop all wood, I agreeing to
supply the grunting. ,
3. The Bulletin to do the washing-up, all broken
crockery to be replaced at Bulletin expense.
4. In the event of the bloated ruffian next door stealing
our wood, or fowls, or becoming insulting, it shall be
my stern duty to declare war against said bloated
ruffian, and it shall be the proud privilege of Thb
Bulletin’s seven sons to do the fighting.
5. Those points settled, all other matters can be very
well left to adjust themselves.
6. Mvbloominoath.
Sordid-minded persons, men with cart-horse souls, may
sneer at my pretensions in this matter, but you and I,
dear Bulletin, can regard their sneers with calm indif-
ference, for are we not brother Australians, pigs of the
same snout, so to speak, with one great grunt—l mean
one glorious destiny ; and has not an all-wise Providence
blessed us with souls to soar to the lofty and magnificent
heights of Federal sublimity ? —Junee.
The Bulletin receives this communication
with gladness, and hereby offers to federate
with “Junee” on exactly the same terms as
those on which N.S. W. is federating with
Tasmania: (1) Each party keeps its own
property, just as N.S.W. keeps its rail-
ways, public lands, and all its other assets.
If the combined firm wants any of the
property it has to pay for it. (2) Ih®
Bulletin, representing N.S.W., has 31
votes in the directorate, while “Junee,” re-
presenting Tasmania, has 11. (3) Both parties
are taxed on the same scale (according to the
Federal tariff) to pay the working expenses of
the partnership. Or, if “ Junee ” prefers it.
The Bulletin, representing Victoria and
N.S.W., will federate with him, “ J unee ” re-
presenting the three small provinces. Terms are


(1) Each party keeps its own property,
as provided in the Commonwealth Bill.
(2) The joint business is run by two boards of
directors, “Junee” having a majority vote
on one board and The Bulletin on the
other, but in case of disagreement the two
sit as one. The Bulletin, representing N. S. W.
and Vic., has 59 votes on the joint board while
“Junee,” representing the other three pro-
vinces, has 35. The Bulletin is quite willing
to agree that a two-thirds vote shall be neces-
sary to carry anything at a sitting of the joint
hoard, as it will have two-thirds of the votes
and two over. (3) The Federation shall have
power to take over the debts of both parties—
The Bulletin lias more debts than “Junee,”
so it doesn’t mind. (4) The Federation shall
have power to take over the assets at a valua-
tion—The Bulletin has sufficient voting power
to see that it doesn’t get left at the valuation.
(5) Both parties shall be taxed on the same
scale. Will “Junee” kindly come along and
federate with this paper at once ?

Rip Van Winkle.
~Y
N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan has been per-
suaded to curse Federation on financial grounds
in the columns of Sydney Daily Telegraph,
and his curse is a sick-aud-sorry spectacle.
Coghlan goes on the dry and desolate figures
of the case thus : The Federal tariff will yield,
say, .£6,000,000. It may yield any other sum,
but £6,000,000 will do as well as anything else
for an example. Of this £4,500,000 is to be
returned to the States. And if the relative
trade of the provinces remains the same—which
it certainly will not —New South Wales will
contribute 41 per cent, of this revenue, and
will be entitled to something like £1,842,000
of the surplus. This calculation is, of
course, absurd, because if a tariff which is
calculated to produce £6,000,000 a year is im-
posed the heavy duties will reduce imports in
N.S. W., and it won’t contribute 41 per cent.,
or be entitled to £1,842,000 of the surplus.
Fhe whole estimate is based on the theory that
if a man is 6ft. high at age 25 he will neces-
sarily be 18ft. high at age 75. Then if things
remain as they are, the next item is that the
other provinces require, to keep them from
insolvency, the following sums out of the
Federal surplus :
Victoria £1,680,000
S.A 663,000
W.A 890,000
Tasmania 295,000
And as they can’t get these sums out of the
£4,500,000 which, it is assumed, will be divided
among the States out of the assumed £6,000,000,
therefore Coghlan assumes that they must
annex part of N.S. W.’s fair share of the Federal
surplus. Of course it would be equally rele-
vant to assume that N.S. W.,S.A.,and Westralia
would conspire together to annex Victoria’s
share of the cash, which is one awkward dis-
crepancy to begin with. Also there is no
shadow of evidence whatever that there won’t
be £5,500,000 to divide instead of £4,500,000;
Coghlan’s figures are based on the assumption
that the Federal Government will choose to pay
all its expenses out of customs and excise, and
it isn’t bound to pay all or any of them from
that source. Also the Federal Government is
bound, for five years after the Federal tariff
starts, to divide the surplus according to the
amount of each province’s contribution, so
Coghlan’s whole theory is based on the further
ridiculous assumption that the provincial
finances will be in exactly the same condition
about seven years hence as they are now. It is
only when the five years expire that the pro-
vinces can appeal to the Federal Legislature to
readjust the division of the surplus. Also, it
is necessary to include the wild inference that
the debts and railways can’t possibly be taken
over by the Commonwealth in the seven years ;
that the debts can’t be converted so as to make
the necessary saving by the reduction of
interest; that the provinces can’t possibly find
any new sources of revenue; that railway,
postal, and telegraph receipts won’t improve ;
in short, that Rip Van Winkle won’t wake,
and that Moses, having been in a particular
spot when the light went out in, say, 1899,
will stay in exactly the same spot till 1906.
Starting on the assumption that everything in
1906 will be in exactly the same position as it
was in 1896, Coghlan’s figures are all
right. But everyone knows perfectly well that
they won’t be ; in all probability by 1906 the
present conditions will be as obsolete as an
armour-clad crusader. It is actually proposed
by the anti-Federationist push to appoint an
“ independent board of financial experts ” to
settle the Federal question for good by over-
hauling Coghlan’s figures and testing their
accuracy. Apart from the fact that there are
no independent experts available (barring, per-
haps, the Auditor-General, whose report on
Reid’s finance is being carefully kept dark),
the real point is that the mere mechanical
accuracy of Coghlan’s figures doesn’t matter a
solitary rap. They are necessarily based on
the theory that the world is going to stand still
—and it never does stand still.
A University God.
Sydney University Commemoration elicited the
usual babyish display from the undergraduates,
and from Chancellor MacLaurin and Gov.
Hampden some curious remarks. The intelli-
gent Chancellor devoted a paragraph of his
curtailed speech to the late Judge Windeyer,
and invited the audience to “strive to imitate
the patient industry, the unflinching devotion to
justice and truth, and the noble scorn of detrac-
tion, for which Windeyer’s memory will long
be cherished. (Applause.)” Dr. MacLaurin
might have added that Judge Windeyer’s
“ patient industry ” and “ unflinching devotion
to justice and truth ” were conspicuously shown
in the Mt. Rennie trial, at which he patiently
and industriously sat for 77 hours in six days ;
and particularly during the defence at that trial,
when he unflinchingly devoted himself (and
sleeping jurymen, and the sleeping prisoners,
and the worn-out counsel) to justice and
truth for 48J hours with 5£ hours intermission;
thereby earning the leading-article remarks of
Sydney Morning Herald that—
Whatever the facilities afforded to prisoners on their
trial may Lie, they can he of little avail when the sittings
of the court are protracted from day to day to such an
extent as to exhaust the physical and mental energies of
counsel and jurymen alike. . . . No case has been heard
of in modern times in which English Judges have com-
pelled jurors and counsel in criminal cases to sit all
through the night, in spite of repeated protests and
requests for adjournment—
and winning from W. B. Dalley, Q.C., the
comment that similar instances of devotion to
justice and truth
“ could only he discovered in some of the darkest pages
of a very dark period of Irish history, when counsel, on
whose hands were committed the lives and liberties of
human beings, appealed in vain to the tiibunal for some
consideration, and for some regard for human weakness.”
Chancellor MacLaurin mentioned that
We are to receive a portrait of our late colleague |Win-
DBYKii aforesaid], which will occupy a worthy place in this
hall, sanctified to him by the recollections of a lifetime.
The Bulletin has pleasure in suggesting that
instead of a portrait, .Sydney University should
sanctify its hall by a gr eat Australian historical
painting representing “Botany Bay Justice”
as seen at 11 o’clock on the Saturday night of
the Mount Rennie trial, with the jurymen
passing round their smelling-salts to keep them-
selves awake, and counsel jabbing prisoners to
wake them up for sentence, and the Judge
fresh and lively after the final 14-hours’ sitting
and “ wiring in ” with the zest of a University
scholar to a penny-whistle. Such a picture
would unquestionably “ take well and give
pleasure to all.” The Bulletin is so charmed
with this improvement on the MacLaurin
programme that it can barely note Governor
Hampden’s counsel to avoid reading bad,
ephemeral literature, as conjoined with his
“ patronage ”of the local trash-novel; also his
advice “ to avoid as a leprosy the reading of
scandalous things about one’s neighbors ”
which is reminiscent of “ Shure Maginnis the
mimber’s a liar, and a thief, and a dirty dog
intirely ; hut indade I niver mind that. Didn’t
he make me a bailiff?” Gov. Hampden
should surely have added his definition of
scandal, and declared that to omit to pay
attention to the scandalous deeds of public
persons is to scandalise oneself as a citizen.
The Three Remarkable Politicians.
The Bulletin is still waiting, in its own
patient, harmless fashion, to hear that Messrs.
Hughes and Lyne and Want have offered an
apology and an explanation to the people of
New South Wales. These gentlemen are sup-
posed to be prominent and honorable politicians
who can be trusted to instruct and guide the
people. And when they inadvertently tell the
people (or a newspaper wickedly misrepresents
them as doing so) things that are grossly un-
true, and which amount to mean slanders about
the acts of their political opponents, and the
utter untruthfulness of these statements is fully
demonstrated, it is natural to expect that they
will retract the statements if they made them,
or, if they didn’t make them, that they will
cause the daily organ which said they did to
consume its wicked assertions. Hughes, M. L. A.,
was reported by Sydney Daily Telegraph as
instructing the public that, in cases of dead-
lock, the federal Senate (the House where the
small provinces have more than their share of
representation) was exempt from dissolution till
after the House of Representatives had suffered
the painful experience of a dissolution and re-
election. There is nothing even dimly resemb-
ling this in the Constitution, bull clause 57 dis-
tinctly provides for both Houses being dissolved
at the same time. He also gave his hearers, or
was reported as doing so, the horrifying infor-
mation that S. A., Tasmania, and W.A. could
alter the Constitution as they pleased, whereas
clause 127 distinctly provides that a majority
of people, as well as a majority of .States, must
be in favor of an alteration before it can be
effected. The Bulletin is waiting for Hughes
to get on a platform and honestly retract or ex-
plain these sinful misstatements. It is waiting
for Lyne to say something manly and respect-
able about his ridiculous fiction (or the fiction
which some paper invented on his behalf) that
because the Constitution says (in clause 87)
that three-fourths of the Customs and Excise
revenue are to be returned to the States and
the Commonwealth is only to retain one-
fourth, therefore it is to pay all its expenses
out of that fourth, thus necessitating a tariff
of £9,000,000 a year. As there is no such in-
ference in the clause, The Bulletin is waiting
to hear William John explain that he is an
honorable political ass and made his ridiculous
assertion through ignorance. And, whereas
ex-Attorney-General . Want is reported as
having endeavored to inflame the provincial
fury of his Cornstalk audience by telling
it that Victoria had “so rigged the
coal contracts and bounties and duties that
hardly an ounce from N.S.W. could get in
there to compete with them,” and whereas this
statement or alleged statement is absolutely
false, inasmuch as there is no Vic. coal duty at
all, and Victoria is N.S. W.’s biggest coal cus-
tomer, taking nearly 600,000 tons a year, The
Bulletin naturally expects King Cabbage-
tree either to deny having made the Ananias
in question, or to acknowledge that he was in-
flaming his bearers against Federation by telling
them things which hadn’t a shred of fact behind
them. Likewise it expects him, as a man of
honor, to apologise for his statement (or to deny
that he made the alleged statement) that the
other provinces “ kicked ” the Referendum out
of the Commonwealth Bill, seeing that it
was never in the Bill and they didn’t kick
it out. There are a good many other points
which call for explanation, but The Bulletin
selects these few examples at random. And it
asks the three gentlemen who talked such in-
sane rubbish through their hats to state
honestly, either that they were misreported, or
that they were not wilfully telling what wasn’t
true and merely made an awful exhibition of
themselves because they were even more ignor-
ant than the people they professed to instruct.
Because if they don’t the public will begin to
assume that they don’t care whether they are
decently accurate or not, so long as they whoop
up their own shabby little gospel of Separatism.
“ My God ! ”
According to Premier John Forrest’s own
account, the only real assault committed on
him at the famous Kalgoorlie “ riot ” consisted
in the small circumstance that he was prodded
with an umbrella by some person unknown
wielding a gamp that has hitherto escaped
identification. The crowd was loud and pro-
fane and hot and blanky in its language, but
the prod with the umbrella was the only actual
assault. No alluvial digger that The Bulletin
ever saw or heard of possessed anything so
ordinary as an umbrella, and it is now solemnly
alleged that the bruise arose through the mis-
directed energy of Forrest’s own private secre-
tary, who was hysterically threatening the mob
with bis silk brolly, ami bit his chief by mis-
take. All the same, the papers publish four
columns of shriekful telegrams in which several
hundred people wail about the “villainous
brutality ” of the mob. The prize shriek of
the collection is contributed by one Brown —
As Government Resident of the Victoria district I beg
respectfully to assure you that our people one and all
scorn and deplore the cowardly, dastardly, and ungrate-
ful treatment which you, the worthy head of our Govern-
ment, have received at the hands of the enemies of law
and order, the enemies of Western Australia, indeed, the
enemies of British civilisation. We are with you to a
man, not merely in sympathy, but if needs be with our
might. My God, I am proud of you ! Our civilisation
requires a Government that will insist upon and establish
the maintenance of law and order. “ What better death
to die than facing fearful odds for the ashes of our fathers
and the temples of our gods.” My kindest regards to you
and yours.
Poor Brown probably meant well, but he got
the first line of his poetic quotation dreadfully
mixed. Also the connection between the
Roman who was prepared to die in his coat of
mail with an iron tail, and the man who was
struck from behind with an umbrella while
making the quickest possible retreat to the
railway-station, isn’t so apparent as it might
be. The idea that Forrest went to Kalgoorlie
to hustle for the ash of his parent looks a little
out of place too. In these days, when
people believe in hell, only a dreadfully clumsy
man talks about the ashes of any other man’s
father.
Premier Byrnes, of Queensland, unburthened
his soul last week at Warwick—and left things as
he found them. Only one portion of his lengthy
speech evoked the least symptom of enthusiasm
—it was his- reference to the Q.N.B. business
and the “ damnosa hereditas ” bequeathed
therewith to the Govt. As to the question of
constitutional reform, T.J.B. is of opinion that
some people want one thing and some another,
and T.J.B. is going to wait till they all want
the same way at the same time—and tell him so.
As to Japanese immigration, Byrnes asseverates
Q. is on excellent terms with Japan, and hopes
she will remain so without insulting the little
brown men by too restrictive legislation. Tom-
joe asserts further he is strongly in favor of a
white Queensland —and is also fully cognisant
of the advantages of alien labor in proper ad-
mixture. Also, the Q. squatters are an admir-
able race of men whose interests must be duly
conserved—and closer settlement at the same
time duly encouraged and promoted. Alto-
gether it was a tame, lame, limping oration
without one spark of fire to set the Democratic
bosom in a glow—an utterance worthy of the
worst days of poor old Nevermind Nelson.
John Norton, in his tremendous anti-Federal
speech last week, explained (according to Syd-
ney Daily Telegraph report) that N.S.W.
shouldn’t Federate because—
The resources and population of N.S.W. were develop-
ing by leaps and bounds, while the populations of the
other provinces were either standing still or decreasing.
The public in general is woefully ignorant, and
will believe almost any kind of yarn if it is told

The Bulletin respectfully asks the Provin-
cialist crowd if it can’t conduct its campaign
with a semblance of truthfulness? The con-
stant effort to arouse the passions of the old
Cabbage-tree mob by mean untruths concerning
the Constitution Bill, and its supporters, and
the other provinces, and everything outside the
little Cornstalk circle, is a very poor and
hog-like proceeding.
The following are quotations from three anti-
Federal utterances in N.S. Wales last week :
Federation under any circumstances would mean the
virtual surrender of Riverina to Victoria, and the Barrier
district to South Australia.— Griffith, M.L.A., at Wel-
lington.
The principal function of the proposed commission (the
Federal Inter-State Commission) is to ensure the distribu-
tion of our South-western trade between Victoria and
S.A. — Edward VV. Knox, in Daily Telegraph.
Mr. Ashton then proceeded to examine the Bill from
the point of view—first, of Riverina ; next of New South
Wales ; and then of Australia. Without railway com-
munication to Victoria from the points where the New
South Wales railways terminated—such as Hay and Jeril-
derie—intercolonial Freetrade was a farce. That com-
munication could not be .constructed by the Federal
Parliament without the consent of the State, and New
South Wales was not more likely to construct a line
from Hay to Deniliquin under Federation than she
had been hitherto. Not only would the producers
of the Hay district be unable to get a direct railway to
the natural port, but the existing’ rates would be
materially increased. The clauses absolutely prohibited
the Victorian Commissioners charging a less rate for
goods from Melbourne to Riverina than they charged the
Victorians themselves, and this would mean that the ex-
isting rates on goods to be river-borne from Echuca
would be increased to an enormous extent. Instances of
the different increases were quoted. In the case of wool
river borne to Echuca for dispatch to Melbourne, the Vic-
torian railway rates would be increased from 75 to 122
per cent. This enormous increase would enable the New-
South Wales Commissioners to increase the rates to
Riverina materially, and still retain the traffic. Those
increased rates the people of Riverina would have to pay.
—Ashton, M.L.A., at Kay, Riverina.
This is a skilful distribution of forces.
In and near Sydney the Knox and Griffith
end of the anti-Federal crowd is pointing
out that the inevitable result of Federation is to
send the border trade to Melbourne. And
down on the border Ashton is pointing out
that the inevitable result of Federation will be
to send the border trade to Sydney, whereas
the people want an outlet at Melbourne.
Therefore the border people are asked by
Ashton to vote against Federation because it
will force the Riverina trade to Sydney, while
the Sydney people are invited by Knox and
Griffith to vote against it because it will
drive the Riverina trade to Melbourne. Why
doesn’t Griffith try his little yarn at Hay,
and let Ashton fire off his particular tale in
Sydney ? The result should be interesting—
or bricks.
♦
One of the awful crimes charged against the
Federation Bill in N.S. W. is that, just as the
Labor-party had put old-age pensions into its
“ fighting platform,” -the Convention takes the
business out of the hands of the State. In the
first place, though, it doesn’t. The State can
go ahead with its old-age pension scheme until
the Federation takes up the work. And the
sooner the Federation does take it up the
better ; for any system adopted by one State,
or any system adopted by all the States,
individually and separately, would stand a
good chance of giving a lot of trouble.
For instance, Westralia has during the
last couple of years taken over several tens of
thousands of men from Victoria, N.S. W., and
South Australia ; and if any one of these States
had a system of pensions “ on its own,” its
emigrants would, presumably, lose the contri-
butions made on their behalf. Likewise, what
of the men who follow the shearing from north
to south, or those who go backwards and for-
wards between South Australia and the Broken
Hill silverfield or the West Darling shearing-
sheds ? Working men—the men who want
these pensions most —can’t pay rnt'ch attention
these times to boundary lines ; and if they can’t
do that, no system of pensions or insurance run
by individual States can fill the bill.
Dear Bulletin. —Apropos the Salvation Army
corrobboree. It is noticeable that all their
converts are of low mental type ; so-called
“ saving ” being rare amongst the brainy and
educated, except where the emotional is
stronger than the rational. All progressive re-
ligions appeal to sentiment, the most successful
preachers having bull necks, meaty lips and
jowly cheeks. Also, the religion that spreads
must appeal to the senses with ritual, formula
and show. With the Anglicans High Church-
ism makes the pace with candles, millinery and
incense ; the Broad Church appeals to reason
and empty benches. The Unitarians, strongest
of all sects in intellectual men, make no pro-
gress in numbers. Brains seek no proselytes.
You can back flags and a big drum all the time.
Thus the Free-Thought cult is moribund. The
spectacle of the black-coated agnostic, going to
his hall or chapel every Sabbath to listen to a
paid expounder, was absurd on the face of it—
the expiring flicker of the old creed» Re-
ligions rank with fairy tales as pap for
baby minds ; conveying with their Mumbo-
Jumbo a certain ballast of moral truth, and
sometimes, as with Judaism, valuable sanitary
and physiological regulations. Therefore does
the missionary appeal vainly to educated
Hindoos or Chinamen ; whereas, when a blood-
and-fire enthusiast gets amongst savages he
“ converts ” them by the score. It is a lallacy,
however, to think that by reason we can over-
throw prejudices and sentiment. People have
to (jrow out of religion, as they grow out of
fairy stories ; the devil and angels follow the
giants and bogies ; the miracles fade aw ay with
the fairy godmother, and the sane sceptic can
no more be “ converted ” than a grown man can
give credit to “ Jack and the Beanstalk.” No
effort to liberalise a church can succeed, while
every added ceremony booms the plate. There
is a Klondyke waiting the cleric who can work
in a circus or a ballet with the Wurrd. If
living (Scriptural) pictures were presented even
the bald-heads from the stalls would roll up to
behold the freaks of the patriarchs.—H.F.
The sufferers by the Brunner colliery disaster
brought actions against the co., and have been
awarded compensation amounting to <£7275.
The section of the M.L. Coal Mines Act on
which the Chief Justice had to stand, although
he does not seem to have liked the job, is
almost identical with one of the N.S.W. mining
regulations-—-that the occurrence of an accident
in a mine shall be prima facie evidence of the
owner’s neglect or default. The owners in this
case could not show that the accident was not
due to their neglect or default; and as the
injured men and the dead men’s representatives
could show that they had not been negligent,
they got the verdict. On this side of the water,
though, things have panned out differently for
the miner’s widow. In N. S. Wales, relying
upon the same provision contained in a
regulation, she lias been told that she
has been fooled by the authority of
his Excellency the Governor, and that the
regulation is ultra vires. Moreover, judges
have usually improved the shining hour 'by
scoffing at it as an absurdity, anyw'ay. Also,
Judge Murray, sitting as a Royal Commission,
found and reported last year that not only this
regulation, but a great many others as well, are
not soundly based in law; and still the wretched,
treacherous things continue to be posted con-
spicuously (according to law, which, in the
circumstances, some employers’ agents seem to
take a malicious delight in obeying) “ for the
guidance and protection of the workmen ! ”
Further, there is no hope of any satisfactory
regulations superseding these, because the law
- the act is 25 years old—does not give any-
body power to make them. And, as the party
which was supposed to specially look
after the interests of the miners’ widows is
prominent in fighting against a scheme under
which the Parliament of N.S.W. mi<dit
have a chance to get along with some domestic
legislation, it is apparently quite content to let
an amendment of this law happen any time
during the next 25 years. Possibly, however
the miner’s widow will get tired in the mean-
time, and seek another champion—if, that is
the supposed followers of theparty don’t save
the supposed leaders from the results of their
folly.

Wilson Barrett’s version of “ Hamlet,”
which is doing a season of one week at Her
Majesty’s, is probably as much of a success
as anyone can make “ Hamlet ” in Sydney these
times. Except under extraordinary circumstances
the demand for the immortal Shakesbacon is
limited. The present version is very picturesquely
fixed up in those scenes which lend themselves to
spectacular display, and the co. keeps on a high
level throughout. The main character is rather
disappointing at first, but when his view of
Hamlet had time to soak into the writer’s intelli-
gence he was inclined to vote him a Dane of a de-
cidedly high order. Said Dane is obviouslyaged 30,
or possibly 35 ; Barrett’s idea may be that Hamlet
is 20, but he doesn’t even begin to look like that.
Instead, he is a heavily-built prince with a middle-
aged voice as well as a middle-aged aspect; but it
is a very fine mellow voice, and the elocution is
good after the slow, majestic fashion of the
Shakespearian tradition, and the acting is excel-
lent most of the time, though Barrett’s exits are
often spoiled by a desire to linger too long and
rend the last possible ounce of fat out of
the situation. But the final verdict is that
Barrett s Hamlet is a very fine affair and
well worth seeing, and anyone who disagrees with
this view may call personally at The Bulletin
office after 7 p.m. and lay his grievance before the
watchman. The mere fact that Barrett departs
from many of the old traditions of the part is in his
favor ;-old tradition grows just as objectionable as
old fish. Miss Maud Jeffries’ Ophelia is good all
through, and without any reservation. So also is
the Polonius of Horace Hodges, who plays the old
Danish bore to the satisfaction of all the audience
there is; and the always reliable Ambrose
Manning makes one of the very best grave-diggers
within the writer’s recollection. The rest of the'
co. is lumped together with a general benediction.
Its good features are much more numerous than
its bad ones, anyhow.
* * * *
Speaking strictly as a private literary
Goth, the present writer desires to comment on
one aspect, anyhow, of the Shakespearian tradi-
tion. Said tradition is very slow in its walk and
conversation, very majestic, and very gradual in
its aspect and tremendous in its deportment. It
was born in the land of Lots-of-Time and in the
age of Pomp and Sleep. It lives in a mist of
stupendous elocution with one arm extended in the
air and the other ready to be extended when thefirst
one is tired —and these are probably the reasons
why the community takes its Baconspearein small
quantities and without enthusiasm. This isn’t a
reflection on Barrett, who is rather better than
the average, but on the Shakespearian tradition
in general. Speaking again as a private Goth, the
writer has an idea that if Shakespeare were played
a little faster than he has ever been played yet it
would either burst up the Bard altogether, or else
improve him a good deal, or else leave him where
he is now. He wants to see Claudius or Lear step
out with the ordinary velocity of an ordinary
live-meat individual. This idea is thrown out
quite casually ; if it chances to hit any person in
the eye, the necessary steak to put on the
damaged optic must be found at the injured
party’s expense. All letters of indignant re-
monstrance from Shakespearian authorities will
be consigned to the waste - paper basket un-
answered.
** * *
At Sydney Lyceum, the “White Squad-
ron,” a cry of red wrath and devastation all the
way from Brazil, has succeeded “Phantoms,”
rather to this paper’s regret. As a spectacle, the '
“ Squadron ”is very fine and extensive. It is full
of military Brazilians in uniforms of loud dazzle,
and gaudy soldiers tramp through it, and the view
of Rio harbor is a picturesque piece of blue
water, and there are all manner of .bright clothes,
loud music, row, bombardment, and sudden riot!
And in matters of literary detail the author fails
shockingly. He represents slavery as still existing
in Brazil in the ’9o’s, and the drama hinges on the
gathering at Rio of five fleets, solely in order that
the admirals may hand in a very small piece of
paper on which they allege that brigandage is bad
in these parts, and must be put down with a loud
whack. The arrival of such a large number of
ironclads carrying such a very small observation is
rather incongruous. The boss brigands are really
the wicked general who is employed to put down
brigandage, and his nephew, the wicked colonel;
and these two futile people steal despatches for
no plausible reason, and abduct girls for no
good cause, and do unprofitable sins that any
sane man would avoid. When they find a dan-
gerous enemy asleep with his tail stretched out
comfortably on the carpet, they always take a
running jump right on to the tail and wake him
up for their own destruction. In fact, all their
troubles arise through getting on to their enemy’s
tail when there is absolutely no money in it.
Most of their time is spent in darkly conspiring
against a British officer whose death would be no
visible use to them, and the rest in the continuous
horrid murder of a Brazilian captain who is still
alive and flourishing when all is over. If any one
would furnish the pair with a respectable motive ;
and it was explained why the white squadron was
a white squadron instead of green or shell-pink or
crushed strawberry ; and if the squadron brought
along a bit of paper nearer its own size ; and if
the slave was accounted for; and if he didn’t
disappear in the last act just when he is most
wanted ; and if a lot of other things were straight-
ened out, things would be better. The “Squadron”
is a live play and keeps going all the time, but its
beginning is mixed, and the middle is chaos, and
the end is a painted delirium.
* » * *
The Lyceum co. has been further strength-
ened by the addition of Miss Fanny Liddiard,
whose representation of a tomboy is a very mode-
rate success. When the depraved general, who
seems to abduct everybody on general principles,
steals Miss Liddiard and carries her off to the
interior, she departs not much regretted. A
burlesque artist of the impossible ’Arry descrip-
tion, played by Harry Shine, is a galvanic kind of
joke which seems to take well with the audience.
Diver makes a fine military figure as the Brazilian
general, and drags that gorgeously-dressed im-
probability through with credit; and Miss Ben-
susan, as his daughter, is a bright-eyed donna who
does her little part well, and casts off her bowelless
parent eloquently, Stanford’s colonel is one of
the best things he has yet done, and Miss Wil-
liamson is reasonably good, though by ill-luck she
is fitted into a part where she has to shriek
lamentably at a window, and again her some-
what damaged top-note is painfully evident.
Miss Williamson would be a really valuable
actress if her lines had fallen in places where she
didn’t require to yell for help.
* * # *
Haddon Chambers’ beautiful play, “ The
Idler,” is due at Sydney Criterion on Saturday.
With any semblance of luck, it ought to turn the
fortunes of the Cartwright co., which has so far
only met with a moderate amount of success—
certainly much less than it has deserved. “The
Idler ” is a pretty large contract for any co. to
attempt. It is associated with recollections of the
Brough-Boucicault combination when it was far
and away the best in the Seven Provinces. This
was before Boucicault’s defection, and before
these latter days when discipline began to relax
and an aspect of Letterslide commenced to per-
vade the co., and the mould of neglect showed
on its surface. The co. which runs, against the
old 8.-B. tradition of the “ Idler ” lias a
lot to do—but The Bulletin wishes it luck
for the sake of its own merits and those
of the play. Meanwhile, the caterpillar of com-
parative failure looms large on top of the “ Tree
of Knowledge.” The troubles of that too-artistic
play begin right in the first act, when Cartwright
tells how his life has become dust and ashes—
merely because he and a siren lived and loved
together, and then parted. The audience wants
a bigger Tree than this. The idea of any man’s
conscience being seriously damaged because he and
a girl whose morals were cut low had once sinned
pleasantly among the lotuses and lilies is too much
for any average audience. After all, it doesn’t
want a speck of refined Sin on a plate, but a
large hunk of red, underdone Iniquity sawn off
the joint—something it can get its big shop-teeth
into.

Miss Fanny Wiseman, the bright-eyed
little woman whose Jo and Topsy and other quaint
characters used to bring joy to the hearts of play-
goers, is to have a benefit on sth May at the Regi-
mental Hall, Victoria Barracks, Sydney. Major-
General French, and other elevated personages
lend their patronage, and the programme is

her old part of Topsy. Fanny Wiseman is an
antediluvian star —that is to say, she was best re-
membered before the financial deluge in ’93—but
she is still young and bright enough to make a
dashing Topsy, and this paper wishes her ben. all
manner of iuck.

The “ Cotton King ” is in its third week
at Sydney Royal, and the success with which
Bland Holt keeps this decidedly unpromising play
afloat is a mystery. Even allowing for all the
good points about the company, and the staging,
and the mechanism, the cotton potentate looked,
like a drama which was going. to die young and
have disrespectful things said above its grave.
And at time of writing it looks a little more robust
than it did a week back.

The Kellinos and the Haytors are leaving
the Tivoli shortly, but the versatile Rickards has>
new novelties on hand with which to fill the gap,
and the show goeth on for ever. The star of
’Enery Rickards, which wore a somewhat faded
aspect in the days of the Palace spec., now shines
with all its old lustre. Apparently Rickards, so
long as he sticks to the lucky Tivoli, and thus lays
to his book, can hold his own indefinitely against
all comers.

Harmston’s circus leaves Sydney on Mon-
day, so the last public appearance is fixed for
Saturday. Considering the horse and the Japanese
fiend on the wire, and the small brown Jap, and
the two visions of loveliness on the trapeze, and
the baby elephant, and the smellful tiger, and all
the rest, it has been a deserving entertainment,,
and, but for the fact that it struck Sydney at a
time of red-hot competition, it would have done
better. _ On Friday there will be a speoial bill and
a big vice-regal night.
*# * *
The only change in Melbourne bills this
week is at the wild untutored Alexandra, where
Uncle Tom has made room for “The Roll of the
Drum.” On the word of Dan Barry, this ram-
sha.ckle outrage is an object lesson in history pro-
duced for the benefit of the rising generation, and
the small boys in the gallery appear grateful
for it. The lesson has some sort of bearing upon
Hingland’s glory, wherefore the objects are
mostly understood to be soldiers engaged in a
desperate assault upon Johannesburg. A noble
drummer-boy gets stabbed by a wealthy Boer
quite early in the campaign, but the army rallies
from this disaster, and after much charging of the
foe ii: single file and many repetitions of the same
old file, the British troops capture Johannesburg
and the drummer’s death is avenged. As a
military drama “ The Roll of the Drum ”
seems unusually preposterous ; also its plot
exceeds the limit of toleration due to shows
at the low - priced Haleck. Three or four
people make creditable attempts to take them-
selves seriously, the best of these devoted
mummers being Walter Vincent and H. Stone-
ham. Manager Dan Barry walks around in a
comic-relief capacity, and the shouts of his patrons
bear witness that he fills a want, but the eternal
Haleckian demand for an irrelevant Irish char-
acter in an unconvincing wig is a rather deplor-
able matter. If Barry were to take the trouble to
be more humorous they wouldn’t like him any the
less.

“ When London Sleeps ” has arrived at
the last nights period of a long and cheerful
career. Melb. Royal was full again on Saturday,
and Miss Duggan escaped from her burning bed-
room in a frilled nightgown with unabated suc-
cess. Likewise the dear little child escaped after
her. The infant in question is growing more
vociferous than ever, and it wouldn’t be a bad
idea to postpone her next rescue until she had got
her hair singed. Probably the unnatural strength
of her pronunciation lies in the hair.

** * *
At last annual dinner of the London Play-
goers’ Club a protest was raised by Henry Irving
against the modern newspaper tendency to print
“ columns of rubbish ” about actors. “Carados,”
in the Referee, replied with the simple truth
concerning this little matter. “ Actors them-
selves are surely to blame even more than the
newspapers. Every right-minded person will
agree with Henry Irving that there is
too much rubbish written about actors. If
I do not misapprehend his meaning, how-
ever, he would limit the gossip about
theatres to such announcements as the actors
themselves are ready to supply, unasked, to
the press. That, perhaps, is one-half of the rub-
bish. and the worst part of it, too.” “ Carados ”
might very well have stated also that managers
are industrious circulators of self-glorifying
balderdash. Nothing in journalism is more
pathetic than the faith of verdant theatrical para-
graphists in “information received from the
manager,” whose business policy is persistent
exaggeration of facts and bold invention ot
fictions.
