Dear Bulletin, —I know about ten more or less enthu*
siastio Shakespearian worshippers in Sydney. I didn’t
see any of them at “ Hamlet” on Saturday night—nor on
Monday. On the latter evening’ I got sufficiently in-
terested to look for them round the other shows. I found
two at the Lyceum doing the “ White Squadron,” and
discovered another at the “Tree of Knowledge” at the
Criterion. Hadn’t time to go to the Circus, but I after-
wards learned that one of them was there admiring the
baby elephant. Also I heard of a fifth as having been at
the Tivoli on Saturday. Do Shakespearian enthusiasts
go to see Shakespeare, or don't they?—T.S.
A Sydney man and his niece were sitting in the parlor
of a Paris hotel, discoursing on voices which they had
heard since going abroad. “There isn’t one in the same
street as Ada Crossley,” declared the Australian, ener-
getically. An elegant young woman, exquisitely gowned,
glided gracefully up to him with “Thank you so much ;
lam Ada Crossley.” Which was true ; but she has im-
proved so much in dress, manner, and appearance that
the Sydney man didn’t recognise her. After which, she
took the niece into her room, and dazzled her with a
Paris-gown exhibition.
An alleged portrait of the late H. R. Harwood, pub-
lished in Melb. ’Erald, was a ghastly outrage on
the dead. Portraits in evenin’ journals ought to be
forbidden. Not one artist in a thousand can draw for th 6
rotary press. There is a field indeed for the really able
black-and-white man.
“ Sydneyite Abroad ” : George Darrell, disguised as an
over-fed brigand, is walking round London as only a
melodramatic hero can, and starting paving-stones with
the weight of his mighty tread. His first date is August,
at the Surrey Royal blood-and-cordite shop, with George
Conquest, in “ The Sunny South.” Ha-ha !
“ Earl-actor Erskine-Rosslyn has done himself and his
aristocratic friends a ‘ cruel treat,’ ” writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent. “To celebrate his 50th day
of mummerhood, he gave forth invitations for a dinner
at a Piccadilly hotel, where the management never asks
its patrons for marriage certificates. Two-and-forty of
the blue-blooded mingled with the ‘ Trelawny of the
Wells’ co., and dined and wined on the very best. In the
prevailing mode the ‘ jests ’—for the sequel proved they
were —were split up into small parties with separate
tables, and Hilda Spong interfered considerably with the
thirst-soothing of a noble vis-a-vis by fascinating his
attention from the Perrier-Joudt, whilst Pat 6 Browne
increased the irrigation of her immediates by spurring
them to imitate her natural sparkle. All signed their
names on the menus, and it was a great success for the
actors, until a daily paper cruelly asked the earl why a
£166,000 bankrupt should give such an expensive banquet
—and not to his creditors. Then this footlight fin of
condensed simplicity explained it was not ‘really his
dinner,’but the hotel people’s, who desired to advertise
their establishment. And now Lady Flo Sturt, Lady
Essex, Lady de Trafford, &c., are suffering from an indi-
gestible insult that only a second fulminating indictment
by Clement Scott of the stage and stageites can hope to
assuage.”

to have been sacred from hotel-barberism, had his coat-
pocket robbed of four £5 notes whilst he was away in the
bath-room at the Black Swan Hotel, Numurkah. The
soulless miscreant was seemingly disturbed in his depre-
dation, for he left 1 55 in another packet. Phil was
running his show at the local Easter Fair —a Waxworks,
Gramophone, General Mite, and a little concert co. If
the notes-“ shaker ’ ’ gets captured he will receive a double
punishment, no doubt. Phil is bound to put him in the

H. R. Harwood’s funeral was very poorly attended—for
that of one who had done the world so much good. Can
it be that the old man was nearly' forgotten ? Some of
the old school —of which Grattan Riggs may be taken as a
type—were there to pay last respects, but the rest!—Per-
haps it was because so many co’s were away from Melb.
at the time.
Paradis : “ When Montgomery acted Lavater, in ‘ Not
a Bad Judge,’ at Melb. Royal, manager Harwood took
Treval. Not long after, Jim Bartlett’s Lavater came
along- there, but Harwood put Ireland into Treval. Dur-
ing the performance I was lounging with Harwood over
the back of the dress-circle, and asked why he didn’t act
the part, having made a hit in it. “ Oh,” says he, ‘ what
is the use of acting yourself when y'ou can afford to pay
another man for doing it?’ Such was his view of Art.

The Bulletin’s dear old friend had amassed by pouring

A week before a certain operatic society produced
“ Maritana ” there came the usual hitch in the arrange-
ments. The amateur contralto cast for the tights of
Lazarillo wrote to say that her brother wouldn’t let her
wear ’em, but, as a compromise, she was prepared to play
in velvet knickers. A Lazarillo in “ rationals ” sounded
painfully suggestive of a bike, and the management,
being now rather doubtful re the extremities of “ my
brother's” sister, decided to engage a professional for the
part. The latter’s legs, although not impressive, have
learned to take the stage without any scruples.
Miss Stewart, in “The Tree of Knowledge,” is one of
the very best silver-haired mothers presented on the Aus-
tralian stage these many years. Her success in this part
came at the end of the season, and was a surprise to
Melb. people who had seen nearly every member of the
Cartwright co. to disadvantage in the earlier productions.
The Vic. Operatic Society’s deal with “Maritana” will
yield a profit of £l5O or so for the benefit of the Melb.
Children’s Hospital, after meeting the exes, of an or-
chestra, stage-manager (George Leopold) and two profes-
sionals in the co. Tom Kennedy, who undertook the
business management, had predicted a £lOO profit as the
most they could expect.
A genuinely'-charitable and useful body of lady-workers
is that known as the London Ladies Theatrical Guild. On
Friday afternoons from 50 to 100 ladies belonging to the
profession (writes an Australienne in London) meet and
spend three or four hours stitching babies’ clothes, under-
linen and even dresses for their less-fortunate acting
sisters. Many young actresses, through the kindly help
of the guild in providing them with decent clothing, have
been enabled to get engagements, who would otherwise
have been passed over by managers on account of their
poverty-stricken appearance. The members deplore the
temporary loss of the assistance of the beauteous Maud
Jeffries, by far and away the most exquisite worker
amongst them. Her stitches are so delicate and the gar-
ments she makes so daintily fashioned that they are
always bought up eagerly by the various members of the
guild at a fancy price.
The Bulletin doesn’t want to hear any more mention
of girjs who insisted upon warbling to Albani and were
advised by that lady to go 'Ome to pursue their studies.
The same old yarn was told many times and oft when
Belle Cole was out here, and Patey, and Santley, and
others, and it was always the same old sort of self-adver-
tising girl who got exhorted to go ’Ome. And not one of
those girls has ever been of the least consequence as a
vocalist.

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
It is now reckoned in Westralia that the year
will probably end with a tremendous deiicm w something
like £560,000. For the nine months to 31st March, ex-
penditure was £2,310,820, and revenue only £2,048,004 ;
shortage on three-quarters of the year £262,816, and
heavy items of expenditure still to be met. On present
appearances the receipts will be £90,000 less than last
year, and the cold shades of slump are over almost every
separate item of the revenue.
• * * •
Victoria’s list of unprofitable boom railways
showed a loss of £299,985 for 1897 as compared with
£318,793 loss in 1896. The process of straightening out
tbe tangle left behind by Gillies and Speight is very slow,
but things do improve a little. Last year five lines which
formerly didn’t even pay working expenses, let alone
interest on cost, began to cover ex.’s and contri-
bute something to the interest bill. Also one
line—Eaglehawk to Swan Hill—which never before
managed to pay full expenses and interest, showed a clear
profit of l 2470. But for these small blessings Commis-
sioner Mathieson would probably end his official troubles
by poisoning himself with the new axle-grease.
* * * »
Silverton (N.S.W.) Railway Co. for 1897 made
a profit of over £BB,OO0 —equal to about 45 per cent, on
the paid-up capital of £197,806. And, not being satisfied
even with this magnificent return, it paid 471 per cent,
dividend—equal to £93,957. In 1896, the Silverton rail-
way paid 50 per cent., and 42£ per cent, in 1895. The
dividends are made by the simple process of charging the
miserable public rates that no State-controlled line in
Australia even dreams of. These charges are a heavy tax
on Broken Hill industries, and the Government which
handed over the entire Barrier railway-traffic to this
private syndicate deserves to be remembered as the prize
ass Ministry of N.S.W. But the N.S.W. Government
found it inconvenient to make an isolated scrap of line in
the far West. The S.A. Government would have gladly
extended its line to Broken Hill, and carried goods and
passengers at half the rates charged by the private
monopoly, and even then it would have made 10 per cent,
profit; but the artificial boundary stood in the way, and
there was no Federal Government or Federal railway de-
partment which could do the work, irrespective of pro-
vincial boundaries. About £65,000 a-year is the price
which Broken Hill pays for the want of Federation.
* * • *
The New Zealand Mail points out sadly that
fire-insurance profits are very low just now, and therefore
State fire insurance can’t pay. Which is an utterly illogi-
cal way of jumping at a decision. The profits of private
fire-insurance enterprises are low, because the multiplicity
of small co’s necessitates far too many people living on
the game, and the trouble is not that premiums are too
low, but that expenses are far too high. A State office
could combine a far larger business under one manage-
ment, and thus make a great saving of expense, and a
State office with compulsory insurance would make a
further very large saving, as it would not need to pay the
present heavy commissions to obtain business. Such an
office should be able to take 10 per cent, off the present
premiums, and still make handsome profits. Most of the
small mutual life insurance offices have to charge far
higher premiums than the Maoriland State Life Depart-
ment, and yet pay smaller bonuses, the saving in expenses
under State management accounting for the difference.,
Yet the Maoriland Life Insurance Department pays com-
mission for its business; if life insurance was made com-
pulsory, and this item of expense was saved, it should be
possible to reduce the expenses to 5 per cent., and to pay
the largest bonuses on earth out of the saving thus
effected.
* * * •
The Commercial Bank of Tasmania, for the half-
year to 28th Feb., made a profit of £3463. As the 5 per
cent. div. absorbed £3537 and the dividend tax took
another £177, or £3714 in all, things go backwards to the
extpnt of £251. The Commercial has £141,492 capital and
£19(1,000 reserve, so on the whole of the shareholders’
funds it only makes a profit at the rate of £2 2s per cent,
per annum. Considering that the money would yield 3
per cent, if invested in Vic. State Land Bank securities or
N.S.W. Govt, stock, there is very little joy in running a
bank under these conditions. How things have been
going with the Commercial of Tasmania of late years is
shown below, as nearly as a rather confused system of
accountancy will permit:—

There have been no additions to the reserve-
fund for a long time, and since the end of ’92 the bank
has paid away some £1387 more than its earnings. The
balance at credit of profit and loss is now reduced to
£637, so unless things improve the dividend may yet
have to drop to a humble 4 per cent. Twelve years' ago,
in the grand old times of 20 per cent, dividends, the earn-
ings of the Commercial of Tasmania were over £35,000 a
year, and the drop from that to less than £BOOO is very
violent—for an institution which didn’t reconstruct or
make any special provision for losses. Still the old bank
(it is now aged 66 years) has certainly a splendid reserve-
fund, a very solid reserve of cash and State securities,
equal to over 33 per cent, of its liabilities to the public,
and on paper, at all events, its position looks remarkably
sound. One notable feature is that, since 1892, its hold-
ing of Government securities has dropped from £203,000
to £89,000. It would be interesting to know how much,
if any, of the profits arose through the sale of these at a
premium.

The, report of the National Bank of Australasia
for the half-year ended 31st March, 1898, shows a quite
unlooked-for falling-off in the profits, which only amount
to £15,471. This is a wholly unexpected stoppage of the
upward movement. . During the last nine half-years the
National’s profits have been :
March, ’94 .. £31,312 September, ’96 .. £11,706
September, ’94 .. 25,231 March, ’97 .. 14,712
March, ’95 .. 15,126 September, ’97 .. 18,275
September, ’95 ~ 13,763 March, ’9B .. 15,471
March, ’96 .. 10,523
A dividend of 5 per cent, on preference capital amounts
to £7644, and there is a balance of £7827 to carry forward.
This half-year the National has forfeited 10,531 shares for
unpaid calls, and the £45,456 paid on them, with £4544
added from profits, make a new reserve-fund of £50,000.
There is also a balance of £45,124 accumulated
profit. _ This is a good beginning; still £50,000 re-
serve isn’t much considering the kind of bank the
National used to be, and The Bulletin again urges
the advisability of a cast-iron regulation by which
a certain percentage of the profits will be reserved every
half-year until the fund reaches at least half a million.
Also, the fund should be separately invested in State
securities, and not buried in the general business.
The National’s paid-up capital is now £1,973,078,
so the profit only amounts to the lamentably small figure
of £1 11s. 4d. per cent, per annum, as comp: 1 with
about £1 17s. per cent, last half. This is a very disap-
pointing result. Some good points are that the National
has now released £5,187,044 out of the £6,844,884 of re-
constructed 4J per cent, deposits. Its holding of State
and municipal securities is £645,810 against only £358,437
last half-year. Cash and liquid securities amount to
£2,534,108 (£337,000 increase on last year) which
equals 36 per cent, of the total liabilities to the
public—a figure which is much above the average.
Deposits have increased by over £300,000 in the past
six months. In fact, the only cheerless feature
about the National Bank is that it earns £llls. 4d. per
cent, for its shareholders, when they could get 3 per cent,
for their money in State securities. Considering the
strength of its cash reserve, it is rather surprising that
the institution doesn’t release the remainder of its per
cent, deposits in one act. Such a proceeding ought to
sn.vo eiq t oQO a y ear j n interest, which is a very large con-
sideration.
* * * *
The Colonial Bank (Melbourne) shows a further
small improvement, the half-year’s profits up to 31st
March being £3398. Since the reduction of deposit-in-
terest to 2J per cent., the profits have been as follows
September, 1895.. £7BO I March, 1897 .. .. £3043
March, 1896 .. 2564 September, 1897 .. 3312
September, 1896.. 2004 | March, 1898 .. 3398
Even with this improvement, however, the profits equal
less than eighteen shillings per cent, per annum on
the paid-up capital of £794,605. And when the pro-
posed writing-off of £460,682 is effected, and things are
supposed to he put absolutely straight, the profits will
only equal a few coppers over 2 per cent, per annum.
This certainly suggests that even the proposed writing-
down is none too liberal —possibly not liberal enough. A
bank which gets the bulk of its deposits at 2J per cent,
should be able to show much better returns.
The writing-down of lost capital has hitherto
been postponed for the sufficient reason that £558,000 of
losses couldn’t come out of only £507,000 of ordinary
capital. This was how things stood a year ago, but since
then, by vigorously forfeiting shares in arrear, and mak-
ing calls, and applying profits in reduction of the old debit
balance, things have been so far adjusted that
there is now £490,561 of ordinary capital available
to cover the £460,681 of old losses still undisposed
of. Under these circumstances, the cleaning-up pro-
cess should come off shortly. And when it does come
off it will leave the Colonial a very small institution with
£304,000 preference capital and an ordinary capital almost
too small to he worth mentioning. Even with the in-
creased profits, dividends still look very far off ; it will
take £7600 to pay a half-year’s dividend at 5 per cent, per
annum on preference capital alone, and the profits don’t
reach near half that figure. During the last 12 months
the Colonial has reduced its liability to the reconstructed
depositors by £90,000, leaving £1,396,381 still owing.
An instalment of £93,345 is to be paid shortly, which will
further reduce the total. In the Colonial, new deposits
have preference over old ones ; and the reconstructed
person doesn’t even get his 2J per cent, interest until all
other charges have been paid, which is good for the new
depositor anyhow. Apparently even this least fortunate
among the Melbourne banks is on the up-grade now, but
it is too soon to be violently sanguine. The repayment of
old deposits doesn’t strengthen its position, as is the case
with the National and Victoria. They are paying off obli-
gations which cost them 4.1 per cent., and reducing their
interest hill; the Colonial is paying back money which
only costs it 2.) per cent., and even in these times money
is very cheap at 2A per cent.

Tooth & Co (Limited), the big Sydney brewers,

* * * *
At one N.S.W. branch bank—not a large one,
either—the unclaimed balances amount to over £4OO. The
owners are either vanished or dead, and the bank doesn’t
look for the late depositor’s widow. What do these un-
claimed balances amount to for a whole province, and why
doesn’t the Government require them to be paid into the
Treasury, and advertise names and amounts, in order to
give the depositors’ heirs a chance to hear of them ?
• * * *
The last of the B.H. Prop, smelting-furnaces at
Broken Hill have been run down, and for the future all
the Barrier’s smelting will be done on the S.A. sea-
shore —barring the little that may go to the works at
Illawarra. The showers of molten slag, white and red
and dull crimson, pouring down the black, rugged sides
of 100 ft. precipices, scattering into spray and lighting up
the background of the Hill with its towering chimney-
stacks —this was all that Broken Hill had to give in the
way of the spectacular ; so that the visitor with the artis-
tic eye usually spent most of his evening-time look-
ing hard that way. And now that is gone, the great
silver centre has little to offer but smoke and grime and
dust—and, happily, long drinks.
* * » *
The fate of the Broken Hill smelting works
ought to be an awful warning to Australian silver-mine
owners—and to those people who think they own silver-
mines. Shortly after their first winding-engines were
bedded, Block 14, the South, the Central, the British, the
Junction, the Pinnacles, the Terrible Dick, and the Day-
Dream provided themselves with smelters. All but Block
14 abandoned smelting long ago—most of them well
within two years of starting. And even 14 abandoned it
for years. Not only did the plants cost a pot of money,
but in the effort to keep them going the mines were most
unfairly handled, the “eyes” being picked out and only
the poor ore—in some cases insufficiently timbered up—
left. The B.H. Prop., with its big area and phenomenal
variety of ores, was an exception to the rule ; yet, now
that the mine furnaces have been run down, not a single
ore-extracting plant that was first adopted has been con-
tinued in use. It has taken all these years to learn the
lesson that ’Murka taught at the beginning—that, save in
exceptional cases, silver-raising is one business and silver-
smelting quite another.
* * * *
British Broken Hill has in its last year made a
profit of £19,285, of which it carries forward £13,881, and
sets aside the balance to meet the verdict given against
the co. and in favor of the Dry Creek Smelting co. There
should be a div. at the annual meeting.
* * * *
Junction North expects to have its milling plant
going in June ; and to carry things on till returns come
from that a contract has been made for tbe sale of 2000
tons first-grade ore to Block 14. For a couple of months,
therefore, Block 14 production returns will be a little
more misleading than usual, unless it is kept in mind that
this co is an ore-purchaser as well as an ore-raiser.

B.H. South shares rise steadily. With very
probable quarterly divs., the return is fair. There is
plenty of ore, but all low-grade; and, even at present
metal prices, it will take the co. all its time to maintain
the quarterlies. And it seems to he accumulating a
tremendous quantity of dressed ore, which suggests a
difficulty in quitting satisfactorily.

The veteran John Howell has resigned the
general management of the Illawarra smelting works,
N.S.W., but holds a seat on the Australian board. It is
understood that he will now devote most of his time to
his own extensive mining interests.
* • * *
Coramba, N.S.W. : “Dennings are carting stone
to the Beacon battery.” This prompts the query—What
has become of the Beacon mine, for which the mighty
Exploration co. gave an enormous sum in cash and shares
last year? It has paid one or two small divs., represent-
ing a very small part of the amount paid for the mine.
And now one doesn’t hear over-much about the Beacon.
* » * *
General-manager Stewart, of Broken Hill, for-
merly manager of Rio Tinto, the world’s second copper-
mine, is making an examination of the Chillagoe group of
copper-mines ; also the railway and water-power schemes.
Some persons are enthusiastic enough to declare that if
his report is favorable the syndicate shares will go to
£5OOO. Certainly they will go below £5OO if it is con-
demnatory. Queensland looks like being the locale of the
next copper-boom.
a * * *
Twenty-six inches of rain fell at Croydon during
first quarter current year. Bull is going to have a big
innings there. Waratah Co., a British concern, has about
130 acres of mining holdings at Tabletop, besides the old
Waratah leases near town. Federation and Bobby
Dazzler, containing quarries of low-grade ores, are to be
worked systematically. Splendid dam constructed and
battery now going up.
La Reine d’Or, another Bull co., has bought the
Iguana 3 and 4 West Block, and has 2oz. stone in sink.
The local management of the Elmslie Ltd. is going to
work its recent purchase for all it is worth —which,
judging by present appearances, is pretty considerable.
For past three months of year Rogers' No. 1
North mine at Golden Gate, Croydon, returned IOOOoz. a
month from 2400 tons crushed, not including cyanide re-
turns. The No. lis under offer to an English co. with a
prospect of sale at a high figure. Owner Jinnny Rogers
is Mayor, likewise King of Croydon, also prospective
opponent to Labor-member Bill Brown at next Parlia-
mentary election. His platform mostly consists of some-
thing to eat and drink.
Croydon March mill yield 5454 tons for
64430 z. Rain regarded returns. So far, Croydon has
only been hen-scratched. Early-day wild-cats gave the
field a had reputation, from which it is only now suffering
a recovery. Latest strike in deep ground in No. 7. N.
Golden Gate is giving the field a big lift. Expected
Croydon Consols, a Bull concern, will shortly hit the rich
shoot of gold being worked in No. 7. If so, there will be
great joy both for the Britisher and the Croydonite.
• * • *
Mining forges ahead in Clermont district.
Shallow ground on the Wild Cat has recently been turn-
ing out some wonderful gold. In one claim only 12ft.
down claim-holders bottomed on a 4ft. wash which ran
3dwt. to the dish—7ozs. of beautiful little nuggets being
picked out of the face. The recent revival in the copper
industry bids fair to set poor little Copperfield on its legs
again—for the old works are being re-fitted, population is
gathering again, and a small boom is initiating.
* * » V «
How they clutch at straws. Already applica-
tions are coming in for suspension of labor conditions on
Westralian leases on the ground that, owing to the dispute
with regard to the right to the alluvial, heavy expenses
have been incurred and calls have also been poorly
responded to. And, officially, these applications get a
good deal of sympathy, because they show (or can be
twisted into showing) how “ capital has been frightened
out of the country.”
* * * •
Millions only will measure the depreciation
which has taken place within the last three months in
Kalgoorlie mines. Lake Views, at time of writing,
are down to £7 10s. That represents a fall of
over a million in value. Associated some months
ago were about 90s. ; they are now 505., which
means another drop of a million. Great Boulders-
have had a fall of nearly half-a-million. Frightful falls
have also occurred in Ivanhoes, Kalgurlis, and other
mines. Many of the local brokers are in Queer-street;
Adelaide is in a very crippled condition, and as long as
this state of things continues in London large parcels are
bound to be forced into the market, on account of buyers
unable to cover. This is the bears’ great opportunity, "and
they will make the most of it. As stated last week, the
mines never looked better. Probably they never looked
so well. The Boulder has bored into the lode at the 400 ft.,
and so far it maintains an average of over 3oz. That is an
important development, and will help to send othersha ts
down with greater expedition than hitherto displayed.
The Lake View has apparently got over the slimes trouble
by the adoption of the filter-press process. The output is
increasing, and there is enough ore in sight to run the
present plant for years. The Associated production has
fallen off simply because the practice of sending sulphide
ores to Dry Creek has been discontinued ; but 50b tons
lately sent to Illawarra went 4Joz., and writer is well
informed that it was taken as it came from a body some
ISft. thick. Other big producers, notably Kalgurli and
Brown Hill, have come into line. If booms are made
by yields, Kalgoorlie should have one. Why is it the
other way about? The same old game, with the same
result. When these mines reached their full market value,
or more than full value, newspapers and other playthings of
the boomsters exaggerated their richness ; tipsters, Sab-
bath and otherwise, declared that £5 shares were going to
£lO, and hundreds of fools went in because of the boom
that .was coming, never dreaming that it was already pre-
sent. In London, thousands of people with £lOOO are
allowed to do t'lo,ooo worth of stock-buying business.
The bears wade in ; a war-scare helps to break the market;
for the want of cover cart-loads of scrip are forced
on sale ; and we get the back swing of the pendulum.
At this juncture, the writer is almost persuaded to become
a mining tipster. P.S.—The market hasturned upwards.
* • » *
Sons of Gwalia mine, Mt. Leonora, W. A., con-
tinues to turn out a lot of gold with a ten-head mill, the
ore running ljoz. per ton, leaving another ounce in the
tailings. Already calculations are made in London re the
production with the 60-head mill ordered. The annual
yield is put down at 129,3750 z., and the profit £323,000 —
“ in fact the mine ought to produce, if the tailings are
economically treated, £600,000 sterling a year.” Other
great things are anticipated from “ the able engineership
of Messrs. Bewick, Moreing and co.” But not a word
about the result of their engineership in many other
directions in W.A.
* * * *
Lake View Consols (W.A.) has declared a second
div. of 10s. a share - £125,000. Why, in Heaven’s name,
don’t the shareholders protest against such a form of dis-
tribution ? Much more satisfactory if the divs. came in
half-crowns, and four times as often. There is little
trouble or expense in paying divs., and to pay them at
the rate of £125,000 a time places too much power and
temptation in the hands of the directors in London ; and
there have been cases, somewhere in history, where even
London directors have worked things, when they got a
chance, for their own good!
* * * *
Golden Casket, an alleged gold-mine in W.A.,
largely owned by Sydneyites, has belied its name : “The
ten tons forwarded to Northam works yielded 2dwt. per
ton ; ten tons forwarded to Wallaroo works yielded ldwt.
per ton ; and the two tons of ore from the 150 ft. level
yielded nothing.” Many persons swore by this mine—
they are now swearing at it. It was reported that there
were at least 10,000 tons of payable ore above the 50ft.
level. Somebody must have walked off with it. A
searching investigation should be made. The chairman
of the co. declares that he and others based their faith on
reports, and disclaim any wrong-doing. In its inception,
a cheap mine—and poor Sydney loveth the cheap, nasty
mine, and has only repeated its usual luck.
While the bottom has been falling outof the mar-
ket of the big gold-producing mines of Kalgoorlie, those of
the Northern Territories, comparatively non-producing, are
booming far beyond legitimate value. Horatio Bottomley
is manipulating the latter, and some people say he is
breaking down one market to boom the other.-
Horatio Bottomley, the London Ranji, who is
for ever booming the mysterious Northern Territories
goldfields, made a dazzlingly impudent speech at the
annual meeting last month. Horatio calmly prophesied
that ere the year was out N.T.’s would be “ far and away
the leading gold producer of the world.” Further : “If I
had the choice given me to-day to accept the whole of the
properties of the Associated Gold Mines of W.A. or the
properties of this co., I should not hesitate to take the
whole of the properties of this co. or one group alone.
. . . My own advices are to the effect that in one of our
properties alone we have upwards of £3,030,000 in value of
ore in sight and ready to be crushed. . . . My information
is that in the Howley group we have over £2,000,000 of ore
in siqht.” Then Bottomley went on naively to remark that
other information of an equally startling character Tjad
been received ! He further remarked that there was an
amount of £lO,OOO to come from S.A. Govt., for certain
deep sinking. “ Whether we have received that Ido not
know, hut I am certain we shall receive it, if we have not
already done so.” As a matter of fact, the little Cheque
hasn’t been paid over yet. Bottomley prefaced his speech
by relating that a London mining expert had remarked to
him, “ I have read every paper you have given me and
the conclusion I have come to is this : That either you
have the biggest thing yet struck in gold-mining, or the
damndest swindle ever perpetrated on the British public.”
Poor public!

In 47 years Bendigo has produced one-fourth of
the total gold-yield of Victoria, which, to date, amounts
to 70 million ounces.
* * * •
Each chapter of the Crown Lyell story is more
amusing than the previous one. The shareholders’
special meeting in Melbourne last week broke up amidst
a storm of hoots and groans of dissatisfaction with things
in general. Nobody could say for certain whether he was
hooting the directors’ mysterious attempt to “place” the
spec, in London, or the London and Glasgow alleged
“purchasers” who didn’t purchase, or the chairman’s
recommendation that the ownership of the property had
better remain with the “free and unencumbered” co.
which was singingits praises whilst the shares rose from
lOd. to 28s. What* the trouble with the Crown Lyellers,
anyhow? The mine stands where it did, and they are
there as before. If it’s a “ fine property,” let them
rejoice in their full possession of it; and if, on the other
hand, their property is nothing much to rejoice over, let
them gather Kerristian satisfaction from the fact that J.
Bull and Sandy didn’t fall in.
* * * *
Crotty’s death has caused much consternation
amongst the’ Mt. Lyell boomsters, and stocks have slumped
even in the face of the copper outlook. “ Shareholders of the
various co.’s in which he was interested need be under no
misapprehension that his shares would be placed on the
market. The executors had full discretionary powers as
to the disposal of the shares.” But if the mines are as
great as the public have been led to think, a chance to
buy Crotty’s interests should be hailed with delight.

Now the Tasmanian West Coast unions send
a warning that that prosperous part is over-stocked
with miners and other laborers. And wages are not high,
either. The hands on the Emu Bay railway works are. not
averaging 85s. per week.

The greatest sympathy of a certain trading
community is expressed for the two little shipping agents
who, in the most innocent way possible, guaranteed the
bona-fides of. the draw back claims on which a Govern-
ment has, it is alleged, been defrauded of many thou-
sands. These little men are liable for an absolutely enor-
mous sum should a conviction he obtained against the
shippers now being proceeded against.
» * * *
What are the prospects of a boom in copper dur-
ing the Spanish-American war? The boss copper-mine of
the world, Anaconda, is in America ; the next, Rio Tinto,
is in Spain ; the third, Calumet and Hecla, in America; and
the fourth, Tharsis, in Spain. These four mines produce
more copper than all the others of the world. As the
fighting is not to be in either of the countries, they are
not likely to shut-down, unless the thousands of men
working in the two great Spanish mines are withdrawn
for military purposes. The Crimean war did not shut-
down any great copper-mines, though while it was raging
copper reached £l3O a ton. The Franco-Prussian war did
not materially reduce the copper production, and again
copper went to £126 a ton. Within the last few weeks it
has gone up £3 a ton, and the chances are the next few
weeks will see a bigger advance. Metal-buyers are begin-
ning to bestir themselves, and are now ready to make
twelve months’ contracts at the ruling market price.
With the war on one hand, and the enormous consumption
on the other, writer is prepared to see copper at £6O
before the end of next month, and at £7O before the end
of the year. Should it so happen that the two Spanish
mines close down, and the production of the big mines of
America be in any way limited, this year will see copper
at £lOO a ton, but at the moment the chances are against
it. The two big mines in Spain are owned by English
co’s. Within the last two years, the Exploration co. placed
a large interest in the Anaconda. The Calumet and
Hecla is a French co., so every effort will, no doubt, be
made to keep up the supply, and the advance in value
will be measured by the increased consumption which the
war will largely help to bring about.
* * * •
The strike among the officers of the British India
Steamship Co., extending over the whole Indian and
Burmese littoral, has ended badly for the strikers.
Sir J. Mackay, who specially visited India to inquire into
the officers’ grievances, demanded, as a precedent con-
dition, that the officers’ union should be dissolved. The
officers declined, and the co. thereupon preferred the
chances of a strike to what it was pleased to term “ coer-
cion.” Native certificated officers and others manned the
steamers, with the result that the officers had to cave in,
abolish their union, and apply for employment indi-
vidually.

Society
N.S.W. police authorities might as well
straightway and straightforwardly employ a
regular staff of female detectives. At present a
number of Sydney women are earning their living
in that line, but are paid by plain clothes or
uniformed men out of their official wages, appar-
ently without open official sanction. The fame
of a “clever capture,” or the successful run-
ning down of a “clue,” no doubt compensates the
“ copper ” for money out of pocket, but it leaves
the woman practically at his mercy. Most cap-
tures in “ difficult ” cases are made by the agency
of women. An absconder has a wife or sweet-
heart : she is shadowed and her correspondence
overhauled at the Post-office—probably ‘ ‘ steamed, ”
opened, read, and closed again. Or she is
“pumped ” by a woman in the pay of the police
and, woman-like, becomes confidential. That is
the whole secret of the “ clever ” capture.

* * • *
One barrister who wins many victories in
an Adelaide court is married to a niece of the pre-
siding magistrate, and is a partner of that same
magistrate’s brother. The ideal Judge has no
relations and no special friendships. Pie owns no
shares, is not interested in business, and never
poses as a light in society. Above all, he is never
a secret society boss.
* * * *
The “ unconsecrated-ground ” barbarity
turned-up again in the case of the recently-
suicided Sydney constable, Moran. It would be
hard to imagine a more atrocious refinement of
cruelty than the manner in which Christian
charity contrives to add to the grief of the shock-
ingly-bereaved widow the conviction tliatthe unfor-
tunate self-slayer is also booked for everlasting
damnation.
* * * *
Hermes : “ Asked a medical friend :
‘ Why do doctors put folks under chloroform for
trifling dental matters without impressing upon
them that all chloroform-operations are a life and
death lottery ? ’ ‘Look here,’ he said, ‘person
comes to dentist to get a dozen or more teeth out
in one act. Says the dentist: “Must put you
under chloroform for this, sir.” Patient: “All
right ! ” Dentist (to doctor) : “Is this gentleman
fit?” And, as far as we can tell, he is fit. But
if there is a flaw in him the chloroform finds it,
and out he goes ! Of course, if there is any
apparent risk no doctor would think of concealing
it from the patient, but it’s not etiquette to remind
him that there is ahvays a risk, and that the best
plan would be to have only a couple out at a time
without the anaesthetic. That would smash up the
other fellow’s biz, and he’d soon get a more com-
plaisant doctor.”

Dear Bulletin. —The fact that Albury
Federal Capital Association’s three prizes fell all
to girls makes it look as if the New Female, even
in her teens, is treading on incipient man. But
incipient man—which is to say, the Boy—has the
profoundest contempt for the Girl and all her
works. It should be consoling to a lot of us to
remember that we were once boys, and had our
revenge on the sex in advance. — Buffer.
* * * *
A fiasco was inevitable when the Melb.
Tram Commission decided to hear evidence in
public. The co’s undertaking that no employes
should suffer by reason of any evidence was worth-
less in the circumstances. Nobody ever is dis-
missed by any co. for any such cause, but he is so
very apt to lose his billet very shortly after for
some other cause, that he gets there just the same.
* - * * *
Melb. Tram-conductor : ‘‘ What we want
is, present wages (385.) for 48 hours work, and
time-and-a-half for overtime; one day off in
seven ; and the chance of confronting the maker
of any accusations against us. At present a charge
is simply recorded, and we only know of it when
it suits the co. to bring it up against us. Our
present hours are over 60 per week ; and, as we
are paid by the trip, a slack-day deprives us of
whatever little extra we may gain by a busy one.
And, to sum up—if we give evidence before this
Commission now sitting, we will be marked men
and lose our livelihood.”

A Melb. shop-girl last week obtained £49
damages for slander against a bogus Don Juan for
having represented her as his “victim” to a couple
of her girl friends. Plaintiff had previously
broken her indignant brolly across the'young man’s
carcase. A rare instance of righteous retribution.
The dirty cads who “ give away”
A damsel when she’s gone astray—
We often meet them, sad to say,
Wherever we meander ;
And yet their number is but small
Beside the dawgs that tell the “ fall”
Of maids who haven’t sinned at all,
And never hear the slander.

Extract from one of the defendant’s letters
in a Melb. breach-of-promise case : “ Why doubt
me? If I am the cause of your trouble,"tell me
to go to hell, and not call me your Porkey—a name
I detest. My own Christian name I think a more
endearing one.” His own Christian name was
Bertie, and before the awful crash came it had
been arranged that the plaintiff was to .be
“ Bertie’s good girl ” for ever and ever. The evi-
dence didn’t disclose what she calls him now.
Probably she has reverted to “Porkey.”

Ben Tillett, if you please, is not the only
man who says nasty things about the Mayor of
Melbourne. Rev. Prof. Gosman, after deliver-
ing a lecture on the “Ethics of Competition ” at
Rev. Chas. Strong’s church (Melb.), the other
Sunday, remarked that “it was rather shabby for
the Mayor to refuse them the use of the Town
Hall. If the Town Hall was to be for the benefit
of the citizens, he did not know any more worthy
object that it could be put to than for a meeting
advocating the suppression of the sweating sys-
tem.” If Mayor M'Eacharn intends to identify
himself in public with the Employers’ Union, and
Eatinan in general, he will get himself disliked at
a very early date.

Queensland is possessed altogether of 450
artesian wells. These yield 193,000,000gal. or
862,220 tons a day. Water has been tapped right
from the Gulf country down to the N.S.W.
border. All this has been done within
some 10 years, for the first supply
was struck at Back Creek (Cen-
tralia) nearly 11 years ago.
Now there are Western
towns reticulated
with artesian water-
pipes, and poor,
little God-forgotten
Thargomindah ac-
tually boasts an elec-
tric-lighting plant
driven by the water-
power of the local
bore.

* * * *
Melb. City Council has had its usual
wrangle over the supply of men, horses, and drays
by tender. The works-committee recommended
the acceptance of a tender at 7s. per day for man,
horse and dray—this in face of the fact that the
council by-law fixed the minimum-wage for
drivers at ss. 6d. per day. A few of the more
liberal-minded councillors pointed out that this
left the contractor Is. 6d. a day for horse-food,
wear-and-tear, and his little bit of profit; but Fat
Man prevailed, and the tender was accepted, the
inference being that the contractor is a philan-
thropic crank who employs men for natural love
and affection, and keeps horses and drays and
mops the public gutters as an expensive amuse-
ment. Everything considered, this tender is
pretty tough.
The Melb. philanthropist who lately pre-
sented one of the city cab-ranks with a picturesque
shelter-kiosk also presented the men with a
list of regulations, among which was the
prohibition of bad language and card-play-
ing within the sacred precincts. The
ghastly absurdity of saddling a “ gift ”
with such snufflesome conditions will
probably induce cabby to prefer the
accustomed kerb-stone with its
perennial euchre and unlimited
blanky.

A big Melb. bicycle estab-
lishment has been in the habit
of printing tickets for tradesmen
gratis on the understanding that the
tradespeople allow them to conspicu-
ou.sly advertise their wares on said tickets.
One day a man dressed in seedy black
rushed into the cycle-shop and asked that
some few thousands of cards might be printed \
for him. Then he began to write out hisown “ad.,”
but when he handed it over the counter was in-
formed, with picturesque firmness, that they
weren’t taking any of him. He was an under-
taker. It would, they thought, be too suggestive :
“ Ride our tyres.” “Go to Deals for cheap
coffins.” Who says an undertaker has no humor ?
* * * *
S.A. Govt, has decided to close West Ade-
laide cemetery, the big necropolis containing the
deceased citizens of 60 years. The cemetery is in
a very thickly populated part, and for years people
have complained that with the west wind came
strange odors, believed to be pathetic messages
from the versatile remains. .A. large soap-factory
was some time ago eagerly welcomed as an anti-
dote, but the original and horrible perfume from
this only seemed to encourage the “ much re-
gretted,” and with better organisation they be-
came irresistible. As their ranks contain hundreds
of local-preachers who have always been in evil
odor, Govt, gave up the unequal fight and decided
to close the cemetery.

In one Maoriland town recently, inside of
a fortnight: One married policeman had an order
made against him for the maintenance of his
illegitimate child (this was the second or third
time such a disaster had happened to him) ;
one policeman had to be arrested for being glori-
ously drunk “on jooty ; ” one policeman left
hurriedly to avoid paying for his illegitimate
child —he tried to dodge responsibility by exhibit-
ing a photo, of the unmarried mother taken with
very little on, but it was held to be irrelevant;
also, there was a certain amount of disturbance
about two bobbies tickling a lady of loose char-
acter in the main street one afternoon. The
general reputation of the M.L. police force is that
it is the most drunken and demoralised affair of
its kind outside Hades.
A Melb. paper goes much out of its way
to inform its readers that donkeys will not bray
if a weight is tied to their tails. The value of this
information is minimised by the fact that local
politicians have no tails.

“If it should ever fall to my lot,” said
Judge Rogers, at Sydney Metrop. Quarter Ses-
sions, the other day, to try a dangerous
murderer, I should insist that, if he wished to give
evidence, he should do so from the dock. Why a
dangerous criminal, like Butler, for instance,
could, if he were let out of the dock, kill a few
counsel and the judge and one or two jurymen. It
wouldn’t matter to him ; he could only be hanged,
and he might consider it as well to be hanged for
a sheep as for a lamb.” Which recalls that once
a man was being tried before Judge Windeyer for
killing a fellow-prisoner in Bathurst gaol. R. D.
Meagher, the accused’s counsel, tendered his client
as a witness just before lunch-time, and applied
that the latter should be allowed into the witness-
box to testify. But Windeyer—as timorous at
bottom as are mostly men of his truculently over-
bearing type —promptly refused to let the
man out of the dock. “ Very well, your
Honor,” said Meagher—“ in that case I shall
feel bound to take a certain course ” —presumably
hinting an intention to make capital, on his
client’s behalf, with the jury (or with the Full
Court, upon appeal) of the disadvantage to the
accused of having to give his evidence from behind
the spikes. This aspect of the matter was doubt-
less well-chewed over by Windeyer along with his
mid-day quarter of beef ; for, when proceedings
were resumed, he announced that, upon re-con-
sideration, he had decided to allow accused into
the box And all those who could put 2-and-2
together winked the inside eye, as they observed
that all inkstands, rulers, books everything
which could possibly have been used as a weapon
or a missile —had been removed far from the
prisoner’s reach, and that between him and the
Judge’s precious person was posted fat detective
Hinds, as an “ outer line of defence.”

Benalla (Vic.) has inspired the local laureate to
warble thus:—
Vic. authorities wanted 40 mounted-police
recruits the other day ; 640 strapping young men
applied. There was no advt.; the powers com-
municated with 50 or 60 previous applicants by
letter, and the intelligence got about. Police
officials say a well-distributed advt, for half-a-
dozen recruits for the mounted-constabulary would
result in the invasion of the metropolis by two-
thirds of the- hale young men of the province.
1 he height of young Australia’s ambition is about
15 hands.

Remarked in legal circles in a certain pro-
vince that a certain barrister almost invariably
holds a brief, for one or other side, in criminal
cases which have been worked up by a cer-
tain detective. Considering that the learned
gentleman’s services are not in all-round demand,
it would seein that the d. recommends him
specially to the prisoner or the prosecutor— when
it isn’t a Crown job. If, perchance, a captive gets
the whisper that his only chance of escape lies in
employing Mr. Blank —wel l , Blank is very likely
to get instructions for the defence. Also a private
prosecutor’s selection of counsel is likely to be
influenced by the active and intelligent officer
who has. executed the warrant. But wherefore
the D.’s interest in the B.’s welfare ?

The inexplicable aberrations of lovely
woman ! Bundaberg (Q.) boasts a female delin-
quent occasionally hauled up before the P.M. and
fined £5 for “langwidge,” who is the wife (Cau-
casian, of course) of a kanaka. The lady is in
receipt of regular remittances from the old
country ; has iIIOOO to her credit in a local bank,
and pays her fines with charming nonchalance.
The buck Polynesian is almost as attractive to
certain European women as the male Chow.
* * * »
A good many cases of lead poisoning have
occurred in Brisbane in the last 12 months, causing
much perplexity to local doctors. Some attribute
the trouble to the lead-headed nails used in nail-
ing galvanised-iron ; others put it down to the use
°f galvanised-iron tanks for drinking-water ; some
agrin attribute the sickness to the defective sol-

have the foulest city water supply in Australasia.
According to an English paper, the
lady cabwoman is going to be an institution
in London. One cab co., it is alleged,
has engaged 25 nice girls, who are to
wear a natty uniform and plaintively
urge the haggard horse through the
Strand and down Piccadilly. It is
a good idea to hear about at a long
distance, but anybody in Australia
who tries to start any female cab-
girl aged under 65 should be hanged
for the safety of his species. Man
has enough risks to run as it is. And
when the cab-girl comes along and that hys-
terical maid is feeling lonesome and scared on
her perch at 2 a.m., and a somewhat obfuscated
citizen asks her to drive him to the lonesome
wilds of Five Dock, she will assume at once
that he is planning the usual “assault.” And
halfway there she will collapse, and let her
hair down, and proceed to gallop every way
at once and scream for the police, and the
gentleman who is doubled up inside,
dreaming the peaceful dreams of the
baby-drunk, will either have his neck
broken or get seven years and three lash

The bachelor's paradise is undoubtedly Canterbury.
M.lj. In that province there are 4000 spinsters who can
never be married, for they exceed the bachelors by that
number. In Otago 2066 last roses of summer must be
left blooming alone unless they go to Klondyke, for the
simple reason that in Otago if all the bachelors were
yarded and forcibly Kinsman’d, there would be still
2066 waists left over unsqueezed, and 4132 lips un-
kissed. Down Winton and Riverston way the cigarette
johnny with the downy lip, receding chin and straw hat,
is simply made a little tin god of. One girl said plain-
tively that the “ boys ” were spoiled by the way the girls
petted and worshipped even tenth-rate specimens of the
forked radish. Fellows who now are welcome in the
drawing-room would, in less favored places, be glad to
take night-about with the policeman in the kitchen.
With woman-suffrage polygamy looms.

In drawing plans for a projected new
Children’s Hospital the other day, one architect
placed the morgue-room as opening off the operat-
ing-theatre.
“ It needs no mathematician to prove that
24,000 pennies make <£200,” said Rev. M'Laren at
the recent Melb. Presbyterian Assembly, before
proceeding to show how all world’s missions could
be maintained by a levy of a “ deener ” a head on
everybody. Nobody laughed—nobody ever does
laugh in the Presbyterian Assembly—and nobody
ventured to question the arithmetic of this cock-
sure lightning calculator. That is the one weak
point of the usually hard-headed Scotchmen, as it
is with the wily Jew. In pishness, nothing is
accepted until proved ; in the kirk or the syna-
gogue the most ridiculous assertion is swallowed
with childlike simplicity.

The Salvation Army has most depressing
ideas on the subject of heaven. It has now taken
to displaying at its meetings a series of lime-
light pictures which profess to represent the
career of the blood-and-fire warrior after
death. In the first picture he is shown,
still in his red wardrobe with his
metal fixings and sacred iron-
mongery on, wandering in the
twilight through a bumpy
country where there is no
vegetation whatsoever.
This place looks like a re-
gion of extinct volcanoes
in the moon. Suddenly he
is struck with a large white
plank—presumably intend-
ed to represent a blaze of
glory. The next picture
shows him in his red clothes
talking with two
partial] y-clothed
figures stand-
ing outside a
large gate,
said gate be-
ing easily sup-
ported by a simple
white cloud. And
the series ends fight
there, so the ultimate
fate of the dead red warrior
is a mystery.

Mofussil. Anglo-
Indians wonder at the
sending of Australian
missionaries to India.
The English are material-
ists, generally without any
really fixed belief in a
future life, and the intensely
spiritual Indian population,
both Hindu and Mahome-
tans, well knows that not
5 per cent, of the Eng-
lish are of the accepted
religion. Get up in an
English or Australian
street, to talk religion,
and you are sneered at,
but try it in any city of
India, and immediately
there is a reverent crowd.
Railways and bridges make
no impression on these
people, who have an absolute realisation of a
future eternal life, of which the bullocky,
tyrannous, brute-powerful, atheistic English 'only
hold the portico.”
Bishop Goe complains that the Australian
natives are preternaturally solemn, and incapable
of hearty laughter. We sympathise with the old
man of the See, although he is himself the dead
centre of gravity
Good reason to express surprise
Has Dr. Goe that rapturous cries
Don’t shake each rafter;
For when he takes his Sunday stand,
All present have good cause at hand
For hearty laughter!

The new Maoriland scenery postage-stamps
are out, and on the 2£d. one, —the one with the
view of Lake Wakatipu—that uncouth name has
been wrongly spelt. Stamp-drunkards from all
parts of the globe are rushing the curio, and the
ill-spelt stamp commands fancy prices. Why
doesn’t some one invent a bi-chloride of gold cure
for the person who has stamps crawling over him
in his dreams, as well as for the one who has
snakes ?

Hideous snuffle from Vic. Bible Echo :
Last spring a family devoted the increase of a. flock of
four turkeys to foreign mission-work. The Lord blessed
the turkeys, and something like £9 has been received as
the result of consecrating these fowls to God. . . .
Those turkeys were holy unto the Lord. During the six
months those turkeys walked about every member of the
family was constantly reminded of Christ’s charge, “Go
ye into all the world,” &c.
Those turkeys!
Greece, they say, was rather risky
In the times of pretty Lais,
But it wasn’t half as frisky
As the township of to day is.
And, though scarcely free from scandal
Were the heroes of Walhalla,
Lord ! they couldn’t hold a candle
To the sinners of Benalla !
The parson has been candid,
To the point of rudeness even,
And Benalla isn’t branded
As a city ripe for heaven.
For, of all earth’s naughty places,
From Japan to Guatemala,
There is none, it seems, whose pace is
Quite as rapid as Benalla.
Now, of course, we cannot credit
All the clersryman’s assertions ;
If it were as he has said it,
We’d be running cheap excursions.
But, unless he s quite betrayed us,
I should reckon that “Tra-lala”
Is a word to suit the status
Of the morals of Benalla.

HER LETTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are days in every week that seem to drag,
While. I watch for telegraphic news of ships,
But the day that brings her letter to my hand,
The nectar of the gods my soul then sips.
And, ere opening it, I press it oft to mine,
For I know that she has sealed it with her lips.
Though many a mile of wave and woodland rolls
Between my girl—God bless her—and my camp;
Her letter ever brings her near to me,
As I read it o’er again by my dim lamp,
And ere I lay me down to dream of her
I take one little kiss from ’neath the stamp.
And in the right top corner of each sheet,
That in answering I send across the sea,
I make a little mark for her to know
The very spot to find a kiss from me;
So, though separated far by space and time,
We still exchange Love’s little courtesy.
W.A.

All Drapers Everywhere.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 26, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
Of course you know the fascination of putting
on a big hat and a long train, and playing you’re
the most magnificently-lovely duchess that ever
lived out of a picture. The charm of this game
attacked most of us in the nursery, when we
dressed up the cat as well as ourselves, and, as
Heine says—
After her health we asked,
Our care and regard to evince.
(We have made the very same speeches
To many an old cat since.)
I notice that men don’t succumb to this charm so
easily. At Major and Mrs. Ferguson’s fancy-dress
ball last Thursday one brave soldier dressed up as
a monk, and kept his face cloaked all the time, and
the only other being present who hadn’t the
courage to reveal his features was a Man. The
gentleman who represented a bobby had borrowed
a suit from the genuine article, but couldn’t get a
correct belt that would meet and fasten, the avoir-
dupois of the Force being limited by the Law.
This particular fancy-dress ball made four hun-
dred society people young and great and happy
again. The same flower-girls, housemaids,
Romeos, and Spanish ladies that made their debut
in the year One were present, but there were
plenty of people who were quite new to me.
Semiramis, for instance —I never met her at a
fancy-dress ball before, and rather imagined that
she was not asked everywhere. A fair-haired
naval lady, just from England, played Will-o’-the-
Wisp effectively with black flounces and an elec-
tric-light in her hair. This electric-lighting is
remarkably pretty while it lasts, but creates a
melancholy impression when fading out, and the
first waltz sees the beginning of its end.
Mrs. C. B. Fairfax put her whole soul into her
Japanese costume, and captured every detail. She
even sacrificed her good looks, and wore a false
forehead, and severe hair, and loaded her head
with a Japanese temple turned upside-down.
Miss Clara Manning pointed a moral lesson to
young men with a champagne-bottle on her head,
race-horses painted round her skirts, cards, dice,
and the inevitable pistol scattered about her, and
an unnecessary placard over her shoulders an-
nouncing that these ways Ruination lies. •
If you are any sort of a Christian you will be
glad to hear that flash-light photographs may
now go out of fashion. The flash that was to light
up these gay fancy-dressers caught some light
draperies on the walls, and smoke and flames
sprang out suddenly. The lancers came to a dead
stop, and a scare began that lasted about half a
minute, but was a genuine scare for that length of
time. And death by burning is now the latest
terror that has attached ’tseif £o all the other
terrors of photography.
By the way, one of the photographer’s most
willing victims has been caught at last in another
snare. I speak of Julius Knight, who is to marry
Miss Elliot Page in Sydney, some time soon.
With Hamlet, Wilson Barrett enters into the
best partnership he has yet contrived. The legs
bring memories of Claudian, and the wig is as
wiggy as ever, but, otherwise, the partnership is a
closer bond than Barrett, Claudian and co., or
Barrett, Denver and co. In fact, the new regime
tempts one to discourse on Hamlet only, just
mentioning Barrett as junior partner. Whether
ladies’ boarding schools will agree is a vexed
question; their essays on Hamlet might also
throw light on the subject of Hamlet’s madness.
I noticed that the seminaries watched the play a
little languidly. But, then, who wouldn’t be
languid when taken out to be improved in batches!
For .the sake of the boarding-schools, let us not be
too riotously glad that Shakespeare is back again.
Maud Jeffries dresses Ophelia sane in pink
brocade, with pearls in her long hair, and brings
out her brightest beauty for the occasion. In the
mad scene, she wears the white that all Ophelias
wear, and plays with a little spark that is danger-
ously near genius once or twice x like her mad
voice much better than her sane. The monoton-
ous American cadences of the latter are boring,
and, as Miss M‘Carthy has taken a loan of Miss
Jeffries’ tones, the double dose of drawl makes
you move in your chair. Ada Ferrar is a third
actress whose voice is modelled on the same lines,
and Maxine Elliot makes a large fourth. Blind-
fold, I defy myself to distinguish the quartette
by their speech.
As the Queen, Lillah M'Carthy was beautiful, but
young —too young. The half-grey hair failed to
tone down the fresh tints of her face, and she was
one of those mothers who would be mistaken for
her son’s sister. This, by the way, is the most
popular mistake in feminine annals, and one that
must never be cleared up, or out. Miss M'Carthy’s
red plush robes were heavily embroidered with
stage-gold, but I couldn’t give them much atten-
tion, so fascinating were the rings on the King’s
first fingers. Only a poisoner could have worn
such enormous glitters. Women are justified now
in avoiding, like plague, men with rings on the
first finger of each hand.
Miss Morley, who is young and pretty, and
Cartwright’s only daughter, is slowly recovering.
Her unpleasant accident recalls a memory of Essie
Jenyns, who would never use a cart if she could
help it, and no one could persuade her to go out
riding. She used to say her face was so important
that she dared not risk its injury, and her legion
of admirers had to lead back to the stables the
fancy horses she refused to mount.
The latest wear among smart London men is the
bottle-green coat, reminiscent of Dickens. One or
two have made their appearance already in these
“benighted kawlinies.” At a smart function
recently, darkly ignorant folk were for a time
staggered at the shabby and faded appearance of
a distinguished visitor’s “ beaufort ” when it sud-
denly dawned on them that the garment was com-
posed of bona-fide bottle-green.
At the Lyceum “ Phantoms ” came and went
last week, as phantoms will, and another white
thing took the boards on Saturday. This time it
was a “Squadron,” with Walner Gregory in
brass buttons as Commander. One grand tableau
revealed the Congress of Navies, and the audience
showed which side they were going to die for
when the American admiral’s goatee-beard came
into view. In fact, the gods smelt powder all
along the play, and were as happy as if they were
falling in the front ranks of the war—or happier.
Other happy things in this play were Miss Ben-
susan’s French frocks. A pale yellow silk dinner-
dress was made with long sleeves of tucked
ohiffon, and little tucked revers, and you don’t
know how to wear a Spanish hat until you have
seen Inez balancing a jaunty Toreador over her
left ear. Maud Williamson dresses the heroine
in black velvet, sacred to heroines, and a yellow
satin with a scarcity of shoulder.
Have you ever been to a party where the guests
got up to go in the middle of a fantasia the host
was executing on the piano ? These things seldom
happen now outside comic papers. But I went to
a concert last week where everyone was present
by invitation only, and half-way through the pro-
gramme the invited guests turned round for their
wraps and prepared to go out. It was quite
rousing to see Laurence Phillip, who was the host
of the evening, rush out on the platform and pour
his musicianly soul, mingled with a lot of fire and
brimstone, on the back hair of the departing ones.
A volley of hand-claps replied to his fusillade.
Then the guests went on towards the door, and I
couldn’t see where courtesy got to. Before the
last two items on the programme, the hall was
empty. So you won’t wonder if you are asked to
pay next time Laurence Phillip gives a concert.
Courtesy is a decoration that is never out of
place, but do you know that the floral decorations
were very much over-done at a recent dinner-
party ? Every dish had flowers in it, or round it,
and the tiniest patch of white tablecloth was
promptly squelched by a rose or a chrysanthemum.
When the guests were saying good-night, an old
gentleman gave the hostess an electric shock by
shouting into her ear, “ The only thing I thank
God for to-night is that there were no flowers in
your soup, madam.”
Florence Schmidt has come brilliantly through
three of her 15 concerts. The other 12 are
still before her. Enthusiasm was hot enough to
roast an ox when she appeared in Brisbane, and
£lOO of the <£Boo she hoped to bring back from
Queensland is already in her pocket. The end of
the year will probably see her launching herself on
a London public.
And yet another Voice is about to add itself to
the long roll of voices that are going to raise the
world’s hair some day. This time the singer is a
young man, with an Israelite mamma, and much
cash in the background, as well as the foreground.
At an at-home the other night someone invited
him to favour the company with a song. There-
upon began one of the longest, most florid arias
Donizetti ever perpetrated for a soprano voice.
Bravely the young man attacked it, and when the
note was out of his reach, he lifted it down an
octave, and shook and played with it there. I
hear that the other people present are still more
dead than alive, and one old vocalist, who hasn’t
smiled since his own voice failed him 15 years ago,
broke into a joke. “Look here, young man, what
is your voice ? ” “Tenor.’’ “Well, I’d have given
you a fiver on Donizetti’s account, if you had
stopped half-an-hour ago.”
It seems that there are a great many things you
shouldn’t do if the stage claims you. A little
party of theatrical people took a trip up the
Hawkesbury lately, journeying by the cheap ex-
cursion train, that meets the General Gordon at
the river. By the way, this trip costs ss. fid., all
told, and takes you miles and miles up the waters
of the Australian Rhine, as Trollope calls it. On
a clear day, the loveliness of the river is indis-
cribable, and an overworked woman couldn’t do
better than sit on the top-deck of that steamer,
and have her lunch brought to her, and drift for
an hour or two among the beauty. Ten miles up
the river the boat makes half-an-hour’s halt, then
you can get out and walk under the trees, and look
over orchard fences, and forget that there is such
a place as town.
Well, that is what this party did. And when
the half-hour was up they strolled back to the
boat, went down the river again to the train, and
so to town, all but one. He lay down under a
gum-tree for a minute, and went to sleep, and the
rest of his co. were in the train when he woke up.
That night the piece had to go on without him, no
understudy being available. And it went so much
more quickly with the whole of his scene cut out,
that next day he was politely requested to remain
out as long as that piece was being played.
Albani’s allegedly five-thousand-guinea diamond
belt is by no means the most valuable gaud that
has been exhibited in public here. Maud Hob-
son’s necklace cost more than double the figure of
the singer’s ceinture. Rumor says that Maud’s
splendid necklace was laid at her feet by an
adoring English youngster who worshipped the
statuesque Gaiety girl and who turned a stone-
deaf ear to the entreaties of his parents when they
besought him to marry some fair damsel in his own
rank and settle down ; the infatuated youth
insisted that his life was devoted wholly to Maud
and he would look at no other woman. The un-
happy mother at length had an interview with her
son’s enslaver and stated the case plainly to her.
Maud was most reasonable, and said that if it
would facilitate matters she would take a travel-
ling engagement and leave England and her
adorer for a year. The delighted mother’s thanks
were profuse and made substantial (so goes the
story) by a handsome cheque and the promise of
£lO a week as long as Maud remained out of Eng-
land. This led to the acceptance of the American-
cum-Australian engagement which gave Austra-
lians the chance of gazing nightly for some months
on their fascinating fellow-country worn an.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The most unconsciously funny speech that our
late 4 uncle Tom ’ ever perpetrated within the Mel-
bourne radius was given to the Mayor, councillors
and other nobility of Essendon —a suburb famous
for its footballers and boiling-down establishments.
Shortly before leaving for England, the solemn old
gentleman paid a state visit to Essendon, and,
having duly partaken of fatted calf and wine from
the gold-topped bottle, expressed his complete
satisfaction with all things thereabouts. He drank
prosperity to the city (laughter) of Essendon, re-
marking that he had often admired the suburb
whilst driving through it, and, in conclusion, he
said : ‘ If the visit of myself and Lady Brassey can
gi ve a certain lustre and fashion to the district, we
will not regret the time so pleasantly spent among
you. ’ As an instance of vice-regal faith in its own
radiance and ‘ tone ’-giving properties, this will
bear a lot of thinking over. It reminds one of the
first chapter of Genesis— 4 and the Lord said, Let
there be light, and there was Light. ’
“The dancing season was opened with aloud
flourish by the Newbigin ball given at South
Yarra in honor of the coming of age of the eldest
son last week. It promises to be a lively winter,
and a high-toned drapery firm is playing to the
gallery by having an electric-lighted window
display after dark, wherein several waxen beauties
slowly revolve and exhibit the latest styles of war-
paint to the gazing Amelia and Polly who
enviously sigh, 4 Oh my, ain’t that green just
lovely ? ” The arsenic-tinted robe is garnished
with edgings like curled parsley, and a pink poem
has the same idea in a blushing rose hue. Long
sleeves of spotty gauze and many jangling chains
of colored beads or sham pearls are the latest
vanities, while balloon sleeves have received a fatal
puncture and collapsed altogether. You can go
without sleeves entirely now if you like, and
hitch your bodice up with two narrow shoulder-
straps.
“ Among the best-written screeds appearing in
Melbourne papers just now are a series of articles
by Dr. Neild on ‘Notable Women,’ in a little
semi-amateur journal, The Court, run in the
interests of an anti-seandal-among-women society.
He does the articles for love, so they’re worth a free
advt. It’s amusing to note how gracefully the
astute doc. glides over thin ice in treating some of
his subjects for young persons. A recent article
on Helen Faucit, giving personal impressions of
the great tragedienne’s acting, is especially in-
teresting.
“ Rumors come and go of a big law-suit re the
money-matters of a famous churchy old Victorian
clan. Two moneyed dames and a well-gilt spinster
sister are expected to call upon their trustees, one
of them a relative, for the why and wherefore of
one thing and another. The spinster in question,
by the way, is locally cited as one of the few exist-
ing exemplars of that almost extinct quality—
constancy. In times agone, she plighted her
troth to a short-lived somebody, and no subsequent
suitor can boast of as much encouragement as
would lie on a thrippenybit. It’s not even as if
there had been lack of wooers.
“ Estelle Gowan, a Melb. girl, went ’Ome not
long ago for a trip, met a cousin of the same sur-
name, became engaged to him, went across to
Paris and bought a wedding outfit, brought it out
in several trunks as big as Noah’s Ark, made all
her friends here sit up in admiring envy — such
lingerie, such blouses ! —took her luggage back per
China, was wrecked on Perim Island, went on to
fiance per Carthage, had to leave her trunks
behind—and was married without her trousseau
after all. Selah !
“The Shakespearian Society met and kept up
William’s natal day on Saturday in the Town Hall
supper-room. Waxellency Madden presented
prizes tied in blue ribbon to successful exam.-
passers, also kindly patted the Swan of Avon on
the back in a neat speech and referred to the
efforts of the president (Dr. Neild) in spreading
abroad a knowledge of Shakesbacon. From the tone
of his speech, I should say the Society for Being
Rather Tired of Shakespeare won’t get Vie. ’s deputy
Governor for a life-member. A scene from the
Merchant of Venice was given, and the Portia
was Ethel Clarke, second daughter of Marcus,
robed, in white with a purple-velvet jacket and
hanging tresses. She spoke her lines with fervor
and hugged her Bassanio with the same. But
Bassanio Deegan has been there before at Shake-
speare meetings, and is quite used to it by now.
Her sister Rosa is more practical in business, and
teaches cooking at the gas co. as a profesh. Sophie
Osmond, one of the few women who know how
to .speak in public, used her voice effectually in
reading a clever paper. Everybody was dolled up
and had a festive air. On the whole, W.S. must
have been pleased with the way his friends kept
up his 334th birthday, though, unfortunately, he
wasn’t able to be present at his party himself.
“The new Westralian Agent-General is a grand-
son of parson Wittenoom, first ‘colonial chap-
lain ’ for W.A., while his maternal grand-dad was
one of the pristine harbor-masters at Fremantle.
The mother of the Agent-General of to-day kept
house for her widowed father in her maiden days,
and when she married it was arranged that her
ten-year-old motherless sister was to share her
new home. The bridal pair started to their up-
country place first, followed in a day or two by
the small bridesmaid and an old aunt in a spring-
cart. On the way to York the restless little
traveller fell out of the vehicle and was killed.
“The old W.A. settlers, you know, prided them-
selves on their ‘roughing’ capacities, and spring-
carts and bullock-drays were favorite conveyances.
In those times, too, more than one pioneer who
bad sunk his patrimony in that land of sand, lived
contentedly enough with his delicately-reared wife
on kangaroo and turnip-tops. Nowadays very,
very old W.A. residents shake their heads dis-
approvingly at the mention of young housekeepers
unprovided with tried and trusted recipes for
marsupial cookery. The tail, of course, was the
tit-bit. The forelegs went to the dogs, while
hind-quarter meat, camp-ovened in slices, with
alternate layers of salt pork and no water, was
staple fare. The same straight-backed ancients
love to tell how their elder children, many of
them the magnates of these times, were born in
tents or out-back humpies, while grand pianos,
farm machinery, and the rest of the parental
outfit lay half buried in the sand on the shore at
Fremantle.

requesting the recipient in return to send back the
heart that he gave her. Male cousins were called
in, both mothers had their say, and last of all each
lovey-dove tearfully twittered its troubles in the
ear of a sympathetic attorney.

A well-known Murrumbidgee squatter
takes the “saxpence” whenever he is asked to
drink. He also takes it when he himself
shouts. At local pubs, his little fad is
numored, and the waitress brings in a glass con-
taining a sixpence, which the old fellow solemnly
takes and pays for with the drinks ordered. The
coins eventually reach the charities.

H. 11. Champion, The Bulletin regrets
to hear, doesn’t get a fortune after all. But he
has had a glorious week’s enjoyment, which to his
cynical nature should have been as balm and oint-
ment. He suddenly discovered that he was one
of the most beloved of men in the eyes of persons
whom he had not previously suspected of any par-
ticular affection for him.

Artist Harry Furniss lias been telling his
friends in London a story of how, when he was in
Australia, he came across, near Melbourne, a
little hotel, kept by a Frenchman. The French-
man showed him, with great pride, the name
“John Millais” in his visitors’ book. “Ze
president of your Royal Academy, monsieur ! ”
Furness smiled. But the Gallic publican was
quite serious. Afterwards, F. discovered that Sir
John had a half-brother, also named John Millais,
in Australia. Has anyone else heard of this half-
brother ?
An ancient deadbeat was once brought
before old Johnny Fawkner in Collingwood court
(Vic.). After the police' had testified to the de-
graded character of the ruffian at the bar, the
Father of Victoria began as usual : “It is dis-
graceful to see a man, on the verge of the grave ”
“Garo!” interrupted the delinquent, “wotare
yer givin’ us, Johnny Fawkner ? Why, I was in
the same regiment with yer ole dad, I was. An’l
remember helpin’ to flog ” “ Take him away !”
screamed Fawkner,clutching his skull-cap, “Three
months—take him away.” And they rushed the
bad man out with his undesirable reminiscence
unfinished.

Reseigh Martin, the journalist who came
into prominence some years ago through his
libel on the late N.B. \V. Railway Commissioner
Eddy, died on April 8, at Little'Bay Hospital,
Sydney, after a painful illness. He had but lately
returned from Westralia, whither bad luck fol-
lowed him. Reseigh Martin was a man of con-
siderable capacity, but seemed ever pursued by the
demon of misfortune.

One effect of the Spanish-American trouble
should be to differentiate real American sentiment
from the Irish-Amerioan opinion which, though
rabidly antagonistic to England, will look side-
ways at an attack upon the Catholic nation, par
excellence. Not a few Hibernian families, by the
way, claim Spanish descent, the classic appellation
of Moran, amongst others, being said to be of
Peninsular origin.
Strange how often bigotry gets the upper
hand of com in on sense. The two daughters of a
N.S. W. M. P. not long deceased (who was converted
to the R.C. faith shortly before his marriage), fell
in love with two Protestants, hut were forbidden
by him to marry. These four fond lovers pined in
solitude for about 30 years. Now that the paternal
obstacle is at last removed, two of them have
recently been united and the other pair are about
to unite.
Joaquin Miller, tho poet of the Sierras,
went to Klondyke to get material for a volume of
Arctic verse. But the muse does not dwell in
those frozen lands. Joaquin—who is an old mail
now, and should know enough to write his winter
poetry in front of a good coal fire in his own
house—forced his way to Dawson City in the
teeth of a blizzard, and lost his ears, several toes
and a finger by frost-bite. A poet without ears !
But, as Joaquin still wears his hair long, his lack
of ears will probably not be noticed.
