The document under the hand and seal <>f
George 111., appointing Rev. Sam. Marsden to
the chaplaincy of N.S. Wales, which Rev. J. 8.
Hassall, Marsden’s grandson, is reported to lie
exhibiting as a curiosity in Brisbane, is not the
only extant Australian souvenir of the grotesque! y-
pious George. The communion plate now stored
in St. Phillip’s, Church Hill, Sydney, was speci-
ally presented by the monarch to the budding
settlement, in the hope, as he fervently expressed
it, that his Australian subjects would “ ever
remember God.” There is a similar memento of
the same king in a Hobart church. George 111.
ordered his name to be carved on the plate, so
that he and God should be indissolubly united in
the minds of the worshippers.
M.B.S. : “Surely The Bulletin is ‘a
bitout’ in saying that H. 11. Champion ‘was a
respectable failure in London, where well-dressed
and polished professors of Socialism excite sus-
picions by their uncommonness.’ It is rather the
other way about. The London Socialist leaders
are nearly all ‘ toffs.’ IT. M. Hyndman, leader of
the Social Democratic Federation, is a Cambridge
M. A., a West End society man, and nobody in
London wears a shinier hat or a more fashion-
ably-cut frock-coat. Sidney Webb, G. Bernard
Shaw, and the Fabian group, are well-to-do
middle-class men ; and nearly all the well-known
men in the movement are of the professional
class. Champion’s unpopularity lay rather in his
utter indifference as to whether he pleased his
friends by what he said and did. or otherwise.
He is too diabolically candid for ordinary humans’
endurance.”
What do Austra’i m statesmen read ? A
colleague of Premier Kingston’s, during the Con-
vention-time in Melbmrne, told a friend that
Kingston never reads a book. He reads Thb
Bulletin and the Australasian and the daily
palters, but has no fondness for literature. Pre-
mier Turner is said, by those who know him well,
to have never read a book through in his life.
He is what actors call a “quick study ” in getting
up a subject, but then the authorities are brought
to him. He has never made a quotation or liter-
ary allusion in his life. Premier Reid is said to
read the trashiest of light novels—Parkes used to
charge him with never having read a book at all.
The two greatest readers in Australian politics
are Alfred Deakin and Dr. Coekburn. Deakin
simply reads everything. His acquaintance with
French literature is really remarkable. Cock-
burn's speciallity is Aim rican literature, and
philosophy of the t"an cendental order. Braddon
has written excellent books of travel, and is an
ardent lover of letters. Attorney-General Isaacs
is an omnivorous reader—history and poetry for
choice.

the lately deceas'd Maori chief, Haki Whanga-
whanga, of Ngunguru. One day when riding out
with a pakelia friend they came to a steep hill.
The pakeha got off and led his prad to the top,

Plenty more horse, only one Haki Whanga-

whilst trying to drown a cat—which, after all, duly

The British Treasury prosecuted Lord
William Nevill ; but in N.S. Wales, when even
a public institution—a municipality, for instance
wants to prosecute a person for, say, embezzle-
ment, it has first, if the person has gone to
another province, to either pay or guarantee pay-
ment of the cost of bringing accused back. More
than one man is blossoming in Westrilia against
whom warrants have been issued, which warrants,
through the poverty of the person directly
aggrieved, have not heen served.

Dear Bulletin.— Re that Q. Divisional
Board clerk’s salary of .£l3O a year. On the lower
Diamantina, where nips are Is. apiece and flour
and sugar 7d. per lb., tho clerk’s salary , for years
had been only £lOO a year. At tho end of ’5)7
the Board had an economical fit, and, after
removing the headquarters to an obscure cattle-
station, offered the clerk £5O a year and find him-
self. Of course, he threw up the billet, \Vhich was
thankfully accepted by the storekeeper of said
station. The latter is now clerk of the Rabbit
Board, ditto Marsupial, ditto Divisional, and a
station storekeeper at tho munificent salary of
<£l7s a year. But note—the fat men members of
these Boards get paid Is. per mile for attending,
and, as some of them travel over 100 miles, the
amount received much augments their member’s
screw.— Ytmpah.

Damper: “Re kangaroo springing off his

with each spring, and heard the ‘whop.’ But on
examining the silt I could not find any impression
of the tail.”

are tho Vermont, leather-necked, concertina-

and cook are good, because Sam is a good em-

Adolphus ana Frederick wero working to
clear adjoining sections of bush, and, as their con-
tracts drew to a close, their work brought them
within hailing distance. ’Dolph’us, a tremendous
worker, had a funny little habit of knocking off at
any time after 4.30, if he heard a “ mo-poke ” cry.
He would call out to Fred, “ Thero is tho mo-poke ;
it is time to knock off," and not another chop
would he give. Fred, not so enthusiastic a
toiler, used sometimes to take advantage of this
peculiarity, and get his neighbor to stop work a
bit early by giving an imitation of tho bird’s cry.
One evening, feeling a little languid, he gave a
call : but, tho humor of the thing striking him
suddenly, his “mo-poke” broke in the middle into
a gurgle of laughter. Of course, that gave away
the show. Next evening, however, a real mo poko
went and perched in a tree not a chain from where
Adolphus was chopping. “ Mo-poke ! ” “ No, you
don’t, Fred ; you had me last night! ” (Whack,
whack, whack !) “ Mo-poke / ” (Whack, whack !)
“ Mo-poke t” (Whack, whack, whack!) “Mo-
poke! “No, I’m d dif you do.” (Whack,
whack!) Moral: Australia can create her own
/Esop’s fables when she wants them.

named. He goes

Short-Stager: “Re swaggies’ day jour-
neys. It’s only when a man has an object in view
that he walks a long distance in a. day. There is
generally nothing gained, when looking for work,
by rushing things. A man I know walked 154
miles in four days carrying a’good-sized swag,
tucker, water, and two parrots which he was
taking home for pets. I myself once walked
47 miles in seven daps, and came to the conclusion,
during that mad rush across country, that horses
aro a better mode of locomotion than shanks’
ponies.”
THE DANIELS FAMILY, OF QUEENSLAND.
“There has been no death in the family for over 42 years. There is not a cripple in the family. Not one member of the family ia tongue-tied. Not one
has ever beem insolvent. Not one has ever been summoned for anything-exeept to act as juryman.” This is the member for Cambooya's proud boast as he points
to the above group. Inqu iry discove rs that “Old” Mr. Danie ls is the son of an English man-o'-warsman, and was born in London in 1821. “Old” Mrs.
Daniels was bor n in London in the year 1819. They were married in 1844. The issue of the marriage was eight sons, the oldest and the fourt of
whom died in london at the age of two. Daniels, senr., and family arrived in Brisbane in 1861, and his youngest son was born in Toowoomba in 1863. The
last return of the Daniels family showed that the re were 52 of them, and that there was a markes tendency to increasc. Until recently, as Mr. H. Daniels,
M.L.A., points out, no member of the family was over 5ft. 7in. in height, but now the younger ones seem to look down on their elders. Mr. Daniels,
senr., and his six sons all had forma adjoing each other on the Darling Downs, but they found it neeessary to seek fresh ficlds and pastures new, and settled on
the resumed portion of Feralees run between Emerald and Springsure, where they śelectes 22,000 acres. It was on this selection the above picture (all Daniels)
was taken on December 20tb, 1897.-WORKER. After this, who woudn't dare to be a Daniels?

“ Compton ” on pollard as a food :
Re the alleged appearance ot pollard on the table
among German settlers. I lived for days on an almost
identical preparation myself, while travelling, some years
back. I was under the impression, until reading a recent
Bulletin par., that my experience was unique, as I have
never met an Australian yet who could be got to regard
pollard as anything more than fowl-feed. Being reduced
to some pollard and treacle through an accident at
an inland river, and being unable to replace lost flourand
meat and sugar without serious loss of time, I decided to
pull through on mash and molasses. Found it go down
extremely well. It wanted making scientifically by boil-
lngup the billy first, dropping in the meal a little at a
time and stirring much. The resulting mixture, fixed up
with syrup, was most palatable. I can say, too, truth-
fully, that I was never in better health either of brain or
body than during those few days. Pollard is a mild, natural
cathartic, and, judging from my own experience of it, a
particularly wholesome food. One week’s bush travelling
on white-flour damper and salt-horse has always been
enough to lay me out stiff and dead, with every derange-
ment of the digestive apparatus known to medical
science. The lack of fruit, vegetables, and light food
generally is usually quoted in the bush as the cause of
this. But all these things can, apparently, be made up
for by simply making damper of flour mixed with
pollard —about half-and-half. Whilst living on this,
in the bush, I have always had perfect health, despite
traditional influence of baking-powder. In civil-
isation, too, I have found that bread made in the
same way comes out with a peculiarly granular
structure, most conducive to digestibility. You
can’t make professional bakers believe this. They
yearn inexpressibly, when they do make brown bread, to
build it up on a bran-and-flour basis—a mixture pre-
eminently' calculated to stick in a dyspeptic’s gizzard.
An irritating series of experiences in this connection
have forced the perverseness of the tribe upon me.
Shearers’cooks, it is true, will, as a rule, profit by new
ideas. But the town-baker is the slave of inherited tra-
dition. And as the tradition says bran, it is bran, and
the insides of the race must stand it. They have no
bowels themselves, and no regard for those of others. I
have even gone and bought the stuff and taken it to
them so that there should be no question about it, and
been received, when calling for the bread, with the
pitying smile due to a man saved from destruc-
tion only by the superior penetration of his baker-
friend. “Oh ! you made a mistake. You brought us
pollard. But ice fixed it up all right. We got some
BRAN, and here is the result!”—a chaffy and indigestible
mass outraging dietetic principles much more violently
than the plain flour-and-water “sinker” of swagdom.
The “ brown bread ’’ of commerce is indeed a ghastly
fraud, but the virtues of the pollard mixture demand
universal proclamation in these days of the Almignty
Liver.

Essay by a Poverty Bay Maori boy on
Sheep:—
Dear Sir lam going to write you about the sheep. The
sheep is a very useful animal to us and in the time before
there was a flocks of sheeep they like it very much and it
say in the Holy Bible there was the only animal in the
worjd because it got nothing to do whit anybody and I
see in the Holy Bible it says this. The sheep is the same
as Jesus Christ because when Christ was kill and taken to
the cross he dosent cry, well when we kill a sheep he
dont’t cry nothing at all. The wool of the sheep is sent
to England to made cloth and blancket and many other
cloth we use, and his mutton we use them to eat this all
I can tell you about God blessed you.

Apropos Q. plague of ‘mosquitoes, one
farmer tells how ho lost nine pigs in two days
from the pest, and how ho saved the lives of the
others only by using mosquito - curtain. Just
fancy a pig enveloped in mosquito-curtain ! Just
fancy also the venomous vigor of mosquitoes that
could actually kill a pig. Why, he’s proof against
snake-bite !
Yon K. : “In the backyard
next door to me a playful kid
contracted an admiration that
amounted to hero-worship for the
pet kangaroo. For hours the kid
would stand and gaze rapturously
upon the marsupial, or beguile
that calmly - superior animal's
leisure with wondronsly-exeeuted
gambados. At last, after much
consideration of its august patron’s
mode of progression, it one day
butted the kangaroo into hurried
locomotion, and proudly followed,
balancing itself more or less suc-

approved marsupial fashion, till
the kangaroo turned and regarded
its base imitator with disgust.
How far this strong sense of elec-
tive affinity might have influenced
the kid’s future walk in life can’t
be known, because the want of a
caudal balancing-] ole ended in a
broken leg and the experimenters
destruction.”
A. : I struck a curious team
once on a Tas. potato-farm. It
consisted of a tired old frame of
a horse, a worn-out cow, and a
woman. The team was unicorn,
with the human female in the
lead. They were hauling logs on
to the boundary-line, and the
front moke did most of the pulling.
The owner of the combination
was a fine, strong-looking fellow,
with a long iron-grey beard
colored a beautiful tawny by
tobacco-juice and bad language.
The last I saw of the woman she
was resting in the yoke with her
tongue out, her hair down, and
her stockings hanging over her
boots. The on a stump
smoking and damning the Govern-
ment surveyors.

B E. • “ Let one molecule, at least, uplift itself in pro-
test that Wilson Barrett has been let down very lightly.
‘ Claudian’ confirmed (for this writer, at any rate) an
opinion w'hich ‘The Manxman initiated. Barrett is
essentially a licnd-actor. He acts with his brains, scarcely
ever with his heart and blood. His art is cold and
mathematical. On the stage he gives abundant impres-
sion that every ‘ position,’ almost every gesture, has been
‘ studied ; ’ maybe looking-glassed. The last ounce of
‘effect’ is extracted from each situation in which the
central character figures, hut it is as though a column of
insensate eights and nines had been rigidly added up;
the total is correct, hut. figures don’t thrill outside a
treasurer’s surplus.. One spectator may sadly record that
his heart has never jumped for anything Barrett has said
or done. He is too commercial. And his voice ! Pathos
consists in a semi-raucous rumbling ; a ventriloquial pro-
jection of the vocal organ into the recesses of the dia-
phragm ! The trick of the preacher ! Naturally, Wilson
B. appears to elocute with a sort of hybrid brogue which
might pass for Manx with Bete, but, ye gods ' when did a
Roman prefect sav ‘ Ye ca-an’t do it ? ’ Physically, H .B.
is what the late Charles Keen called ‘ stodgy,’ and, con-
sequentlv, dignity is principally obtained by taking the
longest t ime to cross a stage that has, probably, ever been
exploited in the history of acting.”
Holdar M. : “ Bassed the entrance to a metropolitan
theatre on a recent pav-morning, with edifying effects.
The pavement buzzed with artistic talent wearing a look
of pleasurable patience, as they exchanged ‘ financial
statements’ re the past and foretold a future ‘ sui plus.
The ‘stars’ mostly monopolised the gaudy portico, while
the rank-and-file stood slightly apart, as was only right
and proper. 1 detected an uneasiness, however, in then-
close converse as a glance w-as furtively taken across the
street. On the opposite kerb was edged a further
hatch of muddvrchioned mummers, these latter evidently
‘ resting ’; the while they kept strict watch on the
Thespian temple. The 8.0. clock presently heralded
developments, and both bodies of the ‘ perfesh girded
up their loins for the fray. The one party duly invaded
the treasurer’s sanctum, and, jingling the cash, turned
to find their retreat cut off by an advance movement of
their ‘resting’ pals. Some valiant efforts were made
to escape unscathed, but the enemy was not to be
lightly denied, and the salaried ones mostly succumbed
to the extent of a few shillings or a drink at the corner.
Which remarks may shed some light on the mystery con-
nected with the fitful appearance of that handbill with
its galaxy of talent w-ho contrive to survive pending the
next holiday.”

“ I was in the stalls of the Tht&tre Gymnase ; Paris,
with Dumas the elder, looking at the play of the Denn-
Monde,’ bv Dumas the younger. The incomparable Rose
Cheri acted the evil heroine. I whispered to my com-

S.S. : “ Why does a man in a theatre laugh uproari-
ously at a remark from the party on the stage, which
same remark, if originated at his dinner-table or in his
office, would either be ignored or treated with contempt.
Why is ‘damn ’ irresistibly comic on the stage, and only
a blankv commonplace in the street? It would seem to
be the open defiance of convention, the convention which
bids us not to swear publicly -in the presence of our
womankind, that tickles in the latter case. But why
laugh at stage puerilities that would not evoke the
faintest snigger in the parlor or the bar? Tnere
is no slush in literature, not even in the dread-
fullest of the penny dreadfuls or the cheap senti-
mentals, like the slush of the theatres, and yet
people with a decent, mediocre taste, in literature
swallow the stage slush, and are delighted with it.
I have seen a critic of liooks, who sets quite an Alpine
standard, roaring like a vahoo at the poor humor of an
illiterate comedian. But there, how many men revere
their ideals in a crowd? I believe the desire Of the
philosopher to seek seclusion in which to do his thinking
arises not, as we imagine, from the need of quiet so much
as from the instinctive knowledge that human association
corrupts good thought. There is an intellectual up-and-
down levelling process goes on in a large audience which,
if an un-aver **re man be not repelled altogether, or if he
do not hold himself aloft by constant effort, will surely
-impair his judgment by, in a measure, absorbing his mind
into the mass, till the latter has found its level. So the
superior man laughs with the foolsin the crowded theatre
because, for the time being, they have split their mental
difference. True individuality is only possible in a desert.
Mankind consists of two classes—inspec-
tors and inspected.
The first “tost” crop of tobacco from Vic.
Government plantation, at Wangaratta, is a blue-
mouldy failure, but “excellent crops of maize,
pumpkins, melons, Ac.,” have been raised on the
plantation. This doesn’t sound very promising.
The Turner Govt, didn’t import a tobacco-expert,
at a large salary, to grow vegetablei. He was to
settle the question as to whether the tobacco-plant
can possibly arrive at perfection in A ic., and now,
after two years of learned enquiry, he has ap-
parently only succeeded in proving that Wangs-
ratta is a fine pumpkin district.

J.D. : Could fill a book with yarns
(mostly true) re the mean little tricks of the strug-
gling Australian “cocky ” to get the value of the
paltry wages out of his employes. During the
ploughing season on some alleged farms fires are
lit on the headlands to enable the ploughman to
“ strike out ” without waiting for daylight. The
average pouud-a-week man in the winter seldom
sees the homestead in the light of day. Apropos:
One night, at the usual 9 p.m. supper, the plough-
man casually asked the boss what breed of fowls
he kept. “I dunno,” grunted the “farmer” ;
“p’r’aps the missus does. What sort are they,
Mary Jane?” he called out. “ Oh, just the ordinary
sort," replied the missus from the next room.
“What for!” “ Oh, nothin’,” said the plough
man; “only, as I’ve never seen ’em off the roost,^
I thought they must bo some new-fangled breed !
Harvesting on the Terricks (V.) a while
ago ; long days and hot; and the “ min ” used to
sleep in the cart-shed —the cart-shed and stable
were both under the same roof —said roof a last
year’s Btraw-stack. The stars were always shining
long before we had supper. Then a brief smoke
and turn in. We never seemed to have properly
got to sleep before we were awakened by the boss
hustling about the horses alongside us. If, after
a moderate amount of bumping of feed-boxes, we.
still gave no signs of uprising, the boss would in-
dulge in some gruff admonitions to Punch to
“ Woa, lad !” Then he would pick a quarrel with
some of the horses and vociferously yell at Kate
tj “Stand over there, you brute ! ’’ These objur-
gations were often accompanied by a sounding
thwack along the poor brute’s ribs with a broad
paling. Sleepily some of us would curse, and the
boss would apologise with—“Liestill, boys ; don’t
let me disturb yez ; breakfast won’t be ready for
ten minutes yet.” Ten minutes ! Lord, we’ve
only just had supper ! But we try to sleep for at
least five minutes of the precious ten. No good.
No sooner does the boss leave the stable
than one of the female cockies from the house
beyant comes out into the yard and rattles a
bucket. The rattle is accompanied by shrill
shrieks of "Pig, pig, pig, pig- P*/7, P>K, P'fL
pig!” We mentally anathematise the pigs, and
every live animal on the place. Hands sore and
swollen with cock spur-thorns, eyes bunged with
sleep and from the attacks of yesterday's flios ; to
get up and start work would be torture ! O! fora
few minutes sleep ! If the “pig, pig 1 invitation
doesn’t rouse us out at once, there will be a yell
from one of the cooky’s sons of “Father ! father !
let loose the dog; there’s a cow in the crop!
Tiny ! Tiny ! 1100 l ’em, Tiny ! Hoorool ’em ! ”
It’s fearful. Through a gap in the slab wall we
catch the first faint glimpse of the coming dawn.
We must get up now. Our morning prayer is :
“May all the ” ; but it is distinctly unprint-
able. Besides, the boss is now throwing the
harness on the horse within hearing, and there are
plenty more “min” on the track looking for
binding.
In threshiup-time the men are paid by the hour
—generally sixpence an hour “ an’ Biddy'll ate
yez.” Wlien an accident has happened to the
threshing-machine the cocky hits been known to
rush off to the house with—“ Arrah Biddy, take
off the mate, the Machine's broke ! ” No thresh-
ing, no tucker ! Once a crowd of us were paid off
on Saturday night and re-engaged on the Monday
morning. Sunday’s tucker we had to pay for at
the pub. four miles away. And so on, and so
forth. Goon the land? Goto ! Same thing.
‘ THE BULLETIN'S" PORK SUPPER.—A
PLEASANT DREAM .
The Westralian inspector who is out
determining the limit of the rabbits’ encroach-
ments across the South Australian border, was
paralysed, the other day, by an inquiry from
I lead-quarters whether a couple of fox-terriers
should be sent out to him to keep the pest back !
Considering that thereabouts a million acres is a
small patch, this is quite as delightful as any plan
imported from Europe, and published
(mis-spelling and all) m the “ great papers. Ihe
inspector, by the way, reports that if the natives
would only leave the iguanas alone the latter would
effectively deal with the rabbits.

Sketch in Sydney Domain.
A Dark River correspondent writes to a
Vic. country paper that a packer named Davis,
whose dead body was recently found in an out-of-
the-way gully in the Omeo district, had the
peculiar distinction of occupying a second-hand
coffin made by himself. He also helped to dig
his own grave. Three months previously the
coffin was made by Davis, and occupied for a
time by the body of another man, found dead in
the same locality. A bowelFss Govt, refused to
pay more than 6/6 for making it, but with ghastly
generosity informed Davis that he could retain
the coffin, as the department was of opinion that
he had the best right to it. Copy of memo, re-
ceived by Davis, from ‘Constable Strain :-r-‘‘ Mr.
Davis,—As you appear dissatisfied with amount
allowed you for making a coffin for Alex. Beal,
the department has no objection to your retaining
the coffin, as they are of opinion you have the best
right to it.”

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of o >al in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
“ Ron ” to The Bulletin :
I suggest a press-cutting agency to people looking out
for a business. No Australian opposition and not much
brainwork necessary. London and New York agencies find
their customers in publishers, authors, actors, M.’sP.,
scientists, lawyers, doctors, society entertainers, inven-
tors, traders, and the manufacturing, literary and pro-
fessional classes generally. The rates charged average
about £1 per 100 clippings, postages included. In Aus-
tralia the mainstay should be checking of advts. for large
advertisers, local and foreign. To commence satis-
factorily it would be necessary that at least six copies of
every Australasian newspaper, journal and magazine
should be forwarded regularly and free to the Press-
Cuttings office. Once convince their proprietors that it
is to their advantage to supply such free copies and all
would be plain sailing. Their returns would be derived
from orders for duplicate back numbers and the adver-
tising their publications would obtain among the P.O,
office’s clients.
“ N.” ; Herbert Booth gives himself away.
He says, “I am the Salvation Barnum.” Plain
English, it is, “ I’m a complaisant cuss, giving the
public what it wants in wooden nutmegs. My
sacred mermaid is made of a monkey stuck on to a
fish’s tail.” Booth’s theological giraffe is dead;
but he gammons it is alive, with wires to move
the head and open and shut its eyes. Even the
terrific pale horse is only Barnum’s manufactured
hairless horse. For surely Booth admits it, with
“Don’t be afraid of Hell. I’m only Barnum.”

A letter (genuine) :
Ed. Bulletin.— Dear Sir, —I have submitted for your
examination a sketch, on which I hope to receive your
impartial opinion, and ask you to please bear in mind the
fact that I am only 19 years of age and have yet to burst
the hard, petrified crust of indigent circumstances, being
only a butcher boy ; but my position only increases my
determination. I fear not when I reflect upon the terrible
odds I am compelled to face, and that I must fight the
battle alone and unaided, without even the sad, doubtful
smile of a loving mother or the gross fallacies of an
ambitious father. I will not give up cowardly; shamefully
sacrifice and relinquish all hope and crucify ambition and
self-confidence. But each breath will be spent in en-
deavoring to reach the goal, and if my attempts are
ineffectual and my successive efforts repelled, .hen I will
have the private knowledge that I have died game.
Literature among the sausages ! Stanzas echoing
the plunkety-plunk of the basket-horse ! And in
such a spirit conceived and achieved. Hooray,
kangaroo 1
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8. Senior, chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
Alcohol, the king of medicines—some-
times :
Capt. W , a skipper of old-time sort, was staying at
Barrett’s Hotel, Wellington (M.L.), when the writer first
met him—in the ’so’s. He had been there for six months,
and living chiefly on “stone-fence” —brandy and ginger-
beer. This so worked on the old man’s nervous system
that, as he developed an overpowering desire to stick his
fork into his neighbor’s eye at the dinner-table, or fling a
tumbler at the waiter, Brown, the landlord, had him
carted up to the hospital—a very small affair, under sole
control of a rigid teetotal medico. Poor W was at
once placed under temperance treatment, and the writer,
hearing that he was sinking rapidly, put a small bottle
of brandy in his pocket, and went up to see the old
sinner. The fatal screen had been drawn around his bed.
He lay almost pulseless, and with closed eyes. But when
the mouth of the bottle touched his lips, he sucked away
like a babe at the breast. Then he opened his wicked old
eyes and winked. “ Good 1” he gasped, “ we’ll cheat the
d—-d quac-k yet.” And, two days afterwards, he re-
turned to his old quarters a cured man. The doctor
declared he had never known of such a wonderful rally of
vital force. But, when he knew how the miracle had been
wrought, he wanted to prosecute the exhibitor of the
brandy.
Cripps-Clark to The Bulletin :
Bishop Stone-Wigg seems to doubt the existence of his
reverend black-brudder in Samoa who wears his white-
choker amidships, as it were. I can assure him, by the
long arm of coincidence, that your picture was taken
from an original photo. As 1 write, the splay-footed
gentleman gazes at me with the eye of faith (cabinet size),
and this is how I became possessed of him : On 15th Dec.,
’95, I was turning over my Bulletin on the deck of the
s.s. Oonah when I came upon that missionary picture
with an explanatory foot-note. It delighted me so much
that my companion asked, “ What's the joke ? I passed
it to him and, although I knew he had been down to the
islands recently, I was rather surprised when he replied,
“Yes, it’s very funny; but you should have seen the
original 1 ” “Did you?" I asked. “ Yes—l took the
photo, myself and sent, a copy to The Bulletin, knowing
it is interested in missionary work.” I could scarcely
believe him, but later he gave me a print from the
original negative—and seeing is believing.

The Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited
Tli© TENTH REPORT of tli© Directors
OF
The Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited-
To be Presented to the Shareholders at the Tenth ORDINARY GENERAL MEETING, to be held at the Bank,
126 Elizabeth-street, at noon on FRIDAY, 29th APRIL, 1898.
REPORT.
The directors beg to submit to the shareholders their Tenth Report, with a Balance-sheet and Statement of Profit
and Loss for the half-year ended 31st March, duly audited. After providing for expenses of management, interest
accrued on deposits, rebate on bills current, and tax on note circulation, the net profit amounted to £3,397 19s. 3d., as
shown in the balance-sheet. It will be observed from the figures in the balance-sheet that, notwithstanding the severe
drought that has been experienced in most parts of the colony, the business of the bank continues to be well main-
tained. In consequence of the low rates ruling for money, there has not been a large increase in the profits of the
bank ; but the directors are hopeful of an improvement in this respect during the current half-year. The adjustment
of the amount paid-up on ordinary shares referred to in previous reports, which has to be completed before an applica-
tion can be made to the Court for a reduction of the bank’s capital, has not yet been finally concluded, but the appli-
cation will be made at the earliest possible moment. The first instalment of the deferred deposit receipts, amounting
to £93,345 9s. 7d., will begin to mature on the 30th inst., and due provision has been made for payment of same as
they fall due. During the half-year a branch of the bank has been opened at Sunbury. The Tenth Ordinary General
Meeting of shareholders will be held at the head office of the company, 126 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, on Friday, the

Public Notice :.
Public Service Board,
50, Young-street,
Sydney, 2lst April, 1898.
A COMPETITIVE Examination of Candidates for
Junior Clerkships in the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney, and at various Country
centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to suit
the .convenience of candidates, on the 27th June, and
four following days. The estimated number of appoint-
ments to be made is 50. Candidates must not be less than
16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day of exam-
ination, of good moral character, and in sound health.
Persons already employed in the General Divisions of the
Public Service will be admitted to the examination, pro-
vided their age does not exceed thirty years. The sub-
jects in which Candidates will be examined are
writing, English (Dictation, Composition, Prbcis writing,
Parsing, Analysis, and derivation of words), Arithmetic
(full course), Geometry (Euclid, first Book), Geography
(outlines of physical and political geography, and the
geography of the Australasian Colonies in moderate
detail), English History from the Middle of the Eighteen
Century to the present time, especially with reference to
the Colonies and Dependencies. Candidates will also be
allowed to take any two, but not more than two, of the
following subjects :—Mathematics, English, a prescribed
Author, viz.:—Tennyson’s “Gareth and Lynette” (Mac-
Millan), Latin, French, German, Elementary Chemisty,
and Elementary Geology. Application to attend the
examination must be made on a form for the purpose,
which can be obtained at the office of the Board, and
must reach the undersigned not later than the Ist June.
Candidates will be required to pay an examination fee of
10s., which must be lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions
or the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to
the Secretary of the Board with the form of application.
A list of registered candidates with centres of examination
will be published in the Government Gazette, after the
time for the receipt of applications has expired, and can-
didates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined.
The Regulations and copies of the papers set at the
last examination can be obtained-at the Government
Printing Office, and the principal booksellers.
Bv order of the Board,
J. W. HOLLIMAN. Secretary.

The Apotheosis of the Referendum : The
Kondoparinga (S.A.) district council has resolved
to rider to the ratepayers the question of the
appointment of a ranger—a partially-paid road-
bailiff whose duty it is to see that too many cows
don’t wander round.
On his obtaining -the coveted place, a
certain politician potentate got a pile of congratu-
latory telegrams, mostly from people with a little
axe to grind. Next day lie answered something
like 200 of them. But the State got no div. from
those felicitations. “ Free ” was marked on each
wire.
N.S.W. Land-Minister Carruthers has been
spreading himself about the danger to Australia
from possible future foreign occupation of “those
islands in the Pacific which are now no man’s
land.” Unoccupied Pacific islands are about as
scarce as unoccupied fat political billets in Aus-
tralia.
Dr. Quick’s enthusiasm and patient plod-
ding have leavened Bendigo with Federation, and
the old goldfields city .s * stronghold of the Bill.
Local Labor-member Hamilton sides determinedly
with Trenwith, and carried the quarrel so far as
to publicly tell Tillett that he was talking flap-
doodle.
As the drainngo system slowly extends,
Melbourne stinks with a less deafening bouquet,
and grows reasonably healthy. In 1889 there
were 10,412 deaths in the Yarra-bank city ; in
1807, with a larger population, there were only
6838. The 1897 death-list was the smallest in the
last 14 years.
Federation nas temporarily caused a
curious re-shuffle of the political cards. One
wildly humorous element is the spectacle of the
wealthy National Ass. magnate, Chaffey Baker,
as an advocate of the Federal one-rtian-one-vote
franchise. Baker’s enthusiastic recommendation
of the Senate, in view of the fact that there is no
restricting property qualification, has also caused
acute indigestion in the Fat-man ranks.
Premier Reid, who says that he approves
of and will vote for the Federal Bil', still skulks
silently in the background and refuses to lend any
assistance; and Young, Jake Garrard (lately
blown up by “atmospheric gas”), Gould, S.
binith, Joseph Cook, and Sunday Brunker say
nothing. The Ministry of Seven Silent Shufflers 1
What has N.S.W. Public Service Board
to say to the printed allegation that, besides
promptly dismissing an officer, after 18 years’
unimpeachable conduct, for one solitary “squiff,”
it practically fined him, in addition, AT5O—
of his contributions to a superannuation
fund by which he will not now be allowed to
benefit ?
One of the most touching features of recent
politics in Bananaland was the public declaration
made by Nelson, and re-echoed by To/.er, that
each of the aforesaid statesmen would stand by
his country, at the political h**lni, as long as his
services were required— to the bitter end, if need
be. . . And the very moment a decent billet was
open, they both promptly rushed it.
N.S. W. Colonial Sec. Brunker is a long
making up his mind about the Convention
Bin. Probably his trouble is absent-mindedness
hasn t got t mind. When interrogated on
the subject late./, St. Brunker replied in a copy-
book-heading voice: “Depend upon it, I shall
do 'vhat is right. Since childhood it has been my
constant endeavor to do what was right.”
Query : Is the Coghlan whose federal
financial figures Sydney D.T. is now booming as
so indisputable the same Coghlan whose published
figures keep on showing that Premier Reid has
had a continual deficit ever since he assumed
office? If lie is the same, why does the D.T. talk
so persistently about the alleged Reid surplus? If
he isn't—well, where is the other Coghlan ?
Even slow-going Westralia has a Closer
Settlement system, and Govt, is steadily buying
back (at large prices) the lands which it joyfully
chucked away in the early days, and cutting them
up into small farms.. A lot of the Government’s
friends are making a good thing out of the trans-
action—still it facilitates settlement, and the pro-
cess is reasonably honest as honesty goes in the
Red Dust Land.
Maoriland Governor Ranfurly, in his
famous address to the local Orangemen, told them
that he was ijlqd to know they had humped
their old traditions all the way from Ireland to

"f Constitution Bill current outback in

The formal manifesto of the N.S.W. Pro-
vinci dist party, published last Monday, is a loud
wail about the disproportionate representation of
the small provinces in the Federal Senate, and a
t •emendous appeal to the public to vote against the
Bill and thus preserve the sacred principle of
majority rule. If the Provincial crowd had any
sense of decency it would crawl away and hide
behind a heap of bonedust whenever majority rule
is mentioned. According to the famous amending
bill which it jolted through the N.S.W. Legisla-
ture, if 79,009 Cornstalk electors vote for Federa-
tion and only one is against it, the 70,000 lose and
the one man wins. And the crowd which stacked
the cards like that has the brutal insolence to issue
a manifesto with a heading in big capital letters—
“ Majority rule must be maintained.”

Dear Bulletin. --The Vic. railway rule
that no person in the service shall enforce a patent
against the Commissioner, is probably based on
the result of an action fought years ago between
M.L. Railway Dept, and one of its foremen, who
had invented an improved ballast-waggon. The
Inventor patented his waggon and resigned to
push biz, whereupon the Dept, ordered one truck,
and, having tested it, proceeded to build similar
ones wholesale. The inventor then went to law,
with the result that the Department established its
right to use the patent, without royalty, on lines
in existence at the date of said patent, but not
otherwise. Toe invention, it was argued, was
the result of observations made during the
patentee’s rail way service, the inference apparently
being that a Govt, servant has no right to call his
very soul his own.—H.

Item from the N.S. W. Provincialist mani-
festo—the document that is signed by Want,
M'Court, and Millen :
THE FEDERAL CAPITAL.
In view of the fact that, the Convention definitely re-
fused to provide that, the Federal capital should he in
New South Wales territory, it can now he hardlv doubted
that the Federal city would, under the Bill, he in the
extreme south of the continent. This, therefore, must
be added to the long list of sacrifices that we are’called
upon to make.
Let’s see. The Convention refused to fix the
capital anywhere. Then a definite proposal that
the Federal city he fixed in N.S. W. was defeated
by 33 votes against 5. A proposal that it should
he in Victoria was defeated by 3(5 votes against 3.
Anri the fact that the proposal to fix the capital
in Victoria was thrown out by the larger majority
proves that the capital is to lie in Victoria. Surely
somebody has been drinking.

Loss of the Ship Atacama and
Thirteen Lives.
N.S.W. Marine Board on the 25th inst. de-
livered its decision in the matter of the loss of the
Atacama (in connection with which The Bulletin
has been served by the owner, Mr. M. G. Cowli-
shaw, with a libel-writ claiming £2OOO damages).
» • • •
The President read the finding, as follows :
“ Whereas the Marine Board have instituted an
inquiry respecting this catastrophe, whereby 13 lives were
lost, the board now report that the abandonment was
justifiable, and that the master, Henry Charles Spruitt,
is exonerated from blame.
« * » *
“In connection with this report, the following
remarks are appended :
“ The abandonment of the Atacama and lament-
able loss of life in connection therewith was a peril of the
sea, which might have been averted if the vessel had been
stripped, thereby affording the opportunity of thoroughly
overhauling her bottom when she was docked for
examination, prior to leaving Sydney on the 21st
January, 1898.
• • • •
“The evidence taken in the case is most
voluriiinous and contradictory, rendering it difficult to
decide many of the points in dispute. This is especially
manifest in regard to the actual condition of the
timber in the ship. The preponderance of the evidence
is, - however, in favor of her being sound and fit for
repairing. The following brief statement may be
accepted as actual facts in connection with this unfor-
tunate case. The ship put into Sydney in distress, flying
the Chilian flag, under the command of Captain Henry
Charles Spruitt., on October 14, 1897. She was surveyed
by Captains Vine Hall and Grainger, marine surveyors ;
also, by Mr. Pollock, Lloyd’s surveyor for the port of
Sydney. These gentlemen reported that the vessel was
in a very bad condition, and chat the cost of repairs would
probably be more than she was w*orth. They, however,
drew up a specification, which included the stripping of
the copper, and tenders were sent in by Mort’s Dock
Company to perform the work for £350(1. This offer was
not accepted, and the vessel was sold by auction, on the
order of the Chilian Consul, for £7OO. She was purchased
bv Messrs. Cowlishaw Brothers, and put into the hands
of Mr. J. Young, master shipwright, and Captain Spruitt,
who was retained in command of the vessel, to be
thoroughly' repaired, without regard to cost, under the
superintendence of Mr. J. C. Ellis, who was interested in
a venture upon which the ship was to be sent.
* » • •
V It is admitted on all sides that the repairs
abo.vejdie waterline were thoroughly effective, but when
the vessel was in dock, after stopping a leak, and examin-
ing her bottom, it was not considered necessary by the
owners’ representatives, and the carpenters entrusted
with th.e repairs, to strip all the copper off. Whilst the
abovement.foned repairs were going on, the vessel was
frequently visited by Mr. Ellis, also by Captain Laidman,
marine surveyor to the Underwriters’ Association ; also
by Mr. Bentley, shipwright-surveyor to the same institu-
tion, and there were about 2000 new bolts driven into her,
and other extensive repairs effected. Messrs. Laidman
and Bentley made the abovementioned visits because it
was understood that the vessel’s cargo was to be insured,
although, in accordance with the rule in Messrs. Cowli-
shaw's office, it was not intended to cover the hull of the
vessel.
* » * »
“ On the application made to Mr. Gibb, chair-
man of the National Insurance Company, for insuring the
cargo of the Atacama, he says he gave instructions to
Messrs. Laidman and Bentley to put down in black and
white, and supply Mr. Ellis with a list of everything they
required in the shape of further repairs to make the vessel
acceptable to the insurance office, and it was understood
that a meeting had been arranged between Messrs. Laid-
man and Bentley with Mr. Ellis, on board the vessel whilst
she was in dock, to discuss the matter. Mr. Ellis did not
attend this meeting, not having been notified, he states,
nor did he receive the list of the surveyors’ require-
ments. The surveyors, however, sent in a confidential
report to the underwriters’ office, stating that the vessel’s
copper must be stripped before an insurance should be
effected in connection with her, but they don’t appear to
have done anything further in the matter at the time.
When Mr. Ellis applied to have his policy completed, he
was confronted with the statement that the Atacama was
not a safe risk, but at an interview with Mr. Gibb, when
he pointed out the circumstances in connection with this
particular affair, the insurance risk was accepted for
£6OO on the cargo. By this time the vessel had proceeded
on her voyage from Sydney via Newcastle to San Diego,
and, after encountering very bad weather for 24 hours,
commenced to leak, and had finally to be abandoned on
the seventh day from Newcastle, in a position about 300
miles from Sydney.
* • * *
“ The crew left the ship in three boats, but only
one of these, which contained Captain Spruitt and four
seamen (the fifth man having been drowned), after won-
derful adventures and undergoing very severe privations,
reached Sydney in the tug Leveret. The two remaining
boats and 12 men are still missing. Very bad weather
was experienced in the captain’s boat after she left the
Atacama, and he feels certain that the vessel soon foun-
dered, and that the remainder of the crew perished.
• * * *
“ Appendix.—The Navigation Law Amend-
ment Act of 1881 renders both the owner and master
guilty of a misdemeanor if they should knowingly send
or take an unseaworthy ship to sea from any port or
place in New South Wales. The question, therefore, arises
(but this is outside the immediate scope of the inquiry
instituted under the provisions of the 87th clause of the
Navigation Act of 1871) as to whether there is a case
against Messrs. Cowlishaw or Captain Spruitt in this
respect, or whether all reasonable precaution for the
safety of the lives of those who sailed away in the ship
was taken. Mr. Mahlon Cowlishaw, the member of the
firm who managed the business in connection with the
Atacama, was absent from Sydney most of the time that
the vessel was being equipped, and he, therefore, saw
very little of her. He, however, gave directions to
Messrs. Ellis and Young, the master-shipwright, also to
Captain Spruitt, that she was to be put thoroughly to
rights, without reference to cost, and over £lBOO was
spent on her. The interest of the firm was most certainly
in the direction of the vessel performing her voyage suc-
cessfully, when it is considered that she was valued at
£5OOO with the venture she was on, and was not insured.
Captain Spruitt, who is an intelligent and experienced
master-mariner, had been in command of the Atacama a
considerable time. He was, therefore, as well, if not
better, able to judge of her seaworthiness before she
sailed than many of those who have given evidence
against her, but the captain distinctly pronounced the
vessel to have been in thorough good order when she
was floated out of dock, and he had no hesitation in going
to sea in her. Having these circumstances in view, the
Marine Board is of opinion that a charge of knowingly
sending or taking an unsea worthy ship to sea under the
penalties contained in the second and third sections of
the Navigation Law Amendment Act of 1881, could not
be sustained against either the Messrs. Cowlishaw or
Captain Spruitt in connection with this melancholy
disaster."

W.A.H. on the eternal subject of
“ Ranji ” :
Personally as sick of the Ranji worshippers and the
press thatsooled them on as anyone, let me protest
against “ Mummer’s ” recent steam-roller display. Under
the pressure of constant newspaper stimulation and a
run of disaster Ranji developed a tendency to chatter
which was trying in the extreme. But please let him
have a show. It’s not his fault. He’s responsible
neither for being dark and a “ prince ” nor for the
cackling adoration of the young women of
Sassietv and the old women of the Australian press.
Ranji, “Mummer” says, was ungrateful to the press —
the press which started the whole thing. Well, why
should he have been otherwise ? There is no evidence to
show that Ranji wanted any press deification at all. The
kicks it got when his Illustriousness responded to its in-
vitation to wipe his feet on it were high poetic justice.
What Ranji’s uncle is doing in India doesn’t seem to
affect the point. Nor is the fact —or the alleged fact —
that Ranji is proud of him surprisingly relevant. To
have uncles engaged in something or other is not the sole
prerogative of the cheerful youth in th& “ Private Secre-
tary,” and most people, when their uncles are not
purely professional and monetary, hesitate at enthu-
siastic repudiation of them in public. That Ranji
has something which serves him quite as satisfactorily
as most men’s brains can hardly be denied after
his book, and even after his Stead contributions. They
are not brilliant, but they are written with fairness and
modesty. It is easy for the pressman to poke borac at
Ranji’s references to his own doings. But the pressman
has never been there. He doesn’t know what it is to
write on things afte. taking a leading part in them with-
out dropping into unconscious self-glorification or exag-
gerated self-suppression. “ Mummer’s ” grip of the historic
past is paralytic. Has ever, he asks, a sporting combina-
tion been raised to such importance? He cannot imagine
greater importance than press-notices can bestow. To
him, apparently, to be discussed in “ whole columns” is
to have the sun and moon stand still, witnessing con-
quest, and the universe pausing to worship. The news-
paper is the universe—space the only measure
of earthly glory. Otherwise he might have stopped
to think of lively governments that have been
upset and of boss monarchs that have been obliged
to make themselves uncommon scarce over chariot races
—which turned on quite as “insignificant” a combina-
tion as a latter-day cricket match. And “ Mummer’s ”
modern instances are quite as unreliable as his history.
His large, off-handed statement that one man on his own
side and “ two or three on ours ” outshone “ the nigger
with the stick” calls for exposition. Who are they? Hill
alone outclassed him. Who else? Such bludgeoning of
the black man is a fine sample of typical bull-headed in-
capacity for discrimination. The frenzy of goat-acting
that the Australian public chose to plunge itself into, re-
acted unquestionably to some extent upon its to-be-
coinmiserated victim. But surely it is not to be justly
visited upon him now. The fact that the press and the
women of the continent combined to make a little copper-
plated idol of the prince is no intelligible reason for flat-
tening him out under the weight of a violently prejudiced
dislike. The spirit which prompts the N.S.W. police to
make a rule of arresting the objects of assaults for
riotous behaviour, and which in the last century stirred
up critics of ecclesiasticism into making indecent attacks
upon the Virgin, inspires “ Mummer ” now. He cannot
distinguish between the object of a cult and its professors.
Bismarck is of Jewish descent on the
female side. His mother was a daughter of Anas-
tasius Menken, a Hebrew bureaucrat of Frederick
the Great.
Melbourne has discovered something new
in lepers. He is a Russian, and when run down
was boarding at a local hotel. He was given the op-
tion of life at the lazarette or being shipped abroad,
and is now bound for Germany. Melbourne
medicos say the man has been 20 years a leper,
but there is no ulceration, and the disease would
only be detected by an expert. The leper’s name
is kept dark, ditto all particulars about the ship he
sailed in.
Two cases were tried at last Goulburn
(N.S.W.) Assizes, before Chief Justice Harley—
one, of alleged rape, the other an even-less-men-
tionable matter ; and they both shewed on what
a slender thread an accused’s life hangs. Had it
not been for the astuteness of the policeman, in
the former case, in taking the prosecutrix, an ap-
parently half-witted girl of 16, to the doctor on
the day the alleged offence was committed, the
case would have borne a different hue, but the
doctor could find no trace of any assault. He
found, however, the traces of several previous
“ assaults.” The Chief Justice took a level-headed
view of the case and the jury acquitted the man.
In the second case, prosecutor, a State youth of
14, calmly swore that his statement in the lower
court was a tissue of lies—and this, mind you,
after the Chief Justice had threatened him with
prosecution for perjury.
“Collins Streeter”
The rise and fall of David Munro—the died the other
day—principal engineering contractor of Smellbourne’s
boom-time, was to me an always interesting illustration
of the blatant folly which passed for bold enterprise dur-
ing that period of Bank-rule. I remember strolling
across the old Falls Bridge with a casual acquaintance
who spoke of the new bridge about to be constructed by
D. Munro and Co. I knew of Munro only in his character
of a noisy twaddler at public gatherings, and I referied to
his recent run of big contracts as a surprising sue vest
Whereupon my companion—a practical engineer—told
me that Munro was, in truth, a hollow, assertive
delusion, that he knew very little about his business, and
was taking the contracts at ruinously low prices. Then
we began a-wondering how such things could be. and
how long the greatness of David Munro would last.
It lasted for some years. The old Falls Bridge must have
been gone for ever when The Bulletin* poked borak at
the mutual admiration society of Tom Bent and David
Munro. Speechifying down at Brighton (Melb.), the
bridge-builder pointed to Bent as the George Washington
of Victoria, and Bent paid his tribute to the Napoleonic
intellect of friend David. The two burly blatherskites
were giants in those days. But Melbourne's money-
supuly started to shrink, and shrink ; the business of
David Munro and Co., Ltd.—it had been turned into a
joint stock company—became a matter for indigna-
tion-meetings of shareholders, and the enterprising
bridge-builder was hardly recognisable, of late years,
in poor, friendless, shabby, broken-down David.
The explanation of his bygone bogus success duly ap-
peared among the liabilities of a busted bank. Munro,
as my engineering acquaintance had stated, undertook
his big contracts at a loss, and the bank supported him in
his follies. He was a notable instance of boom-time
enterprise, though not at all an uncommon one. Very
few of the Melbourne speculators and traders who sprang
to the fore and loomed large on overdrafts were clever
business men. They seemed to have commended them-
selves to the bank managers by their impudent capacity
for losing money on a large scale. They wore an air of
importance and talked a financial jargon that deceived
outsiders, but, for the life of me, I can’t think how they
came to get thousands of pounds from banks where their
brainlessness was being recorded all the time in the
ledgers.

The efficiency of thirst as a promoter of
ingenuity was regretfully acknowledged by the
particularly close-fisted captain of a passenger
steamer which, a couple of years ago, reached
Maoriland. Four days before arrival a saloon
passenger, whose drouth was out of all proportion
to his means, secretly exhibited to the skipper the
rough copy of a testimonial which he alleged was
to be presented to him (“ the old man ”) the day
before arrival, and which ended with “ kindly
accept the accompanying £lOO as a slight testi-
mony of our appreciation of the skilful way in
which -the ship has been navigated, and of our
appreciation of your generosity to everyone on
board.” The thirsty one not only got a heavy
wet on the spot, but the captain astounded his pas-
sengers that night by opening two cases of fizz for
them “on his own.” But the only testimonial —in
due time forthcoming—finished up: “We thank
God that we are near enough land to swim
ashore. You have been out of your course three
times during the voyage (on one occasion 480
miles), and we suspected that it was then your
intention to invite our capture by pirates, with
whom we believed you were in partnership.”
Alleged head of household, having care-
fully absorbed the hydatid items in gazetted
N.S.W. Board of Health ukase, button-holes his
friends to enquire what they think of that for a
colored alien in our midst, and emphatically main-
tains that no right-thinking person should hence-
forth tolerate a dog on his premises. But, des-
pite laws, taxes, boards of health, war-scares, and
Federation-fights, Dog, having once been allowed
to leave the Ark, refuses to be abolished. The
crusade against him has as much chance of success
as the war against wine and women, which has
been waged since the days of Noah. Up-to-date,
mundane affairs consist largely of tfyese two big
W's and a Dawg.

• * • *
At a recent Southern Coast race-meeting one of
the heaviest layers against the winner of the second raoe
was missing at the “ weighing-in ” ceremony. Although
several other events were on the card, the bucolic backers
transferred all their attention to the capture of the
absent one. About a dozen punters formed a cordon
around the steamer lying at the wharf, in anticipation of
the welsher shipping for the metropolis. The welsher,
having lain “ perdu ” for half-an-hour to “ cool-off,” as
he expressed it, reached the shore end of the pier, and
noticing the crowd at the far end of it, skilfully worked
his way under the pier to the ship’s side and slipped into
the coal-bunker through the open port. After the races a
thorough search of the vessel was made by the backers,
without result, hut as the welsher could not get to the
city otherwise, the crowd deoided to go on with the boat.
Just before the steamer touched the city wharf a ory of
“ man-overboard ’’ was raised. A rope was thrown from
the wharf, the welsher grabbed it, swarmed up the side
of the wharf, and before any of the backers could land he
blew them a kiss, wished them a “ pleasant return-
journey,” and vanished.
* * * •
The Gunbearer case was finally closed on Mon-
day, when, after taking evidence, the A. J.O. committee
decided that “ Messrs. Hordern and Earnshaw be ex-
onerated from any complicity in' the suspicious running
of Gunbearer in the First Nursery Handicap.”
When the horses were coming up the straight in
the First Nursery, a well-known punter shouted continu-
ously, “ Look at Gunbearer, gentlemen ! Keep your
eyes on Gunbearer, gentlemen!” And the gentlemen
did keep their eyes on Gunbearer, with the result of a
“sixer’’for jockey Cleal. It would be interesting to
know exactly the motive which actuated deal in this
business.

A landmark of Australian racing disappeared on
Wednesday last, when the Messrs. Eiles dispersed the
Duckenfield Park stu 1. The patriarch, imported Grand-
master, as might have been supposed, was not parted
with, an ' the other stallions went for a song. New Hol-
land bro i cht only 15 guineas whilst. Russley was secured
for 165 guineas bv a west coast breeder, who also went in
for Ja number of the blood mare 9. The Australian Peer
went to Mr. John F.ales at 120 guineas. The highest-
priced mare was Wildfire, with colt-foal by Medallion, to
Mr. J. M‘Donald for 350 guineas. Some rare bargains
were picked up in the thoroughbred mares with foals at
foot by Commercial, which went at prices ranging from
7 to li guineas, as the youngsters, failing trotting-track
form, are bound to turn out good harness horses.

should go right with him up to the day, Bobadil will pro-
bably be backable at even money.

“ Ebor, in Kempton Stand Steeplechase, showed
a remarkable horse-sense that, alas ! proved his downfall,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent. “ He was
giving Maroh Hare—his only opponent—a stone over two
miles (which no horse in England can do), for 200 sovs.
Betting 11 to 10 on the mad ’un. Hickey led all the way
with the public money on until the second-last nhstacle
obtruded, whan his mount’s intelligent adaptability let
list. 111 b. finish alone. For Ehor, recognising that
English steepletops have no ‘ guts’ like unto Flemington’s
rails and logs, now rushes through the furzewood in a
stride as a hound bounds through growing grain. Un-
fortunately the top rail, hidden from sight, was higher
up than he thought, and Ebor was knocked down for the
second t ime that day. The first was to Capt. Lambton,
for £l2OO guineas, after being passed in at £llBo—and I
think Gollan did wisely and well.”
• « • •
Compton : “ Recent Bulletin paragraphs on
all-round men in cricket seem right insofar as they urge
that all the bowlers in a team should be hats. Every man
in an eleven has to take his strike in turn ; the team in
which each si rike adds to the total is in a better way than
the team with an ineffective fraction. But there is no
earthly reason why the bats should be all bowlers. Nine
change men are rarely wanted to line up at the crease,
and when a fair number are provided for a balance of
• crack ’ bats is the best that can be got. The all-round-
ness essential to this part of the team is ability to field
as well as to score—a phase of the matter not
dealt with in vour pars. From this point of
view the English bats, taken on their ’Ome
form, were more all-round in their qualifications than
ours. Ranji and Mae.Laren, their two exceptional
bats, were exceptional in the field as well. Hill and
Darling, the two corresponding Australian batting
wonders, are respectable as fields—nothing more. Gregory
and Iredale are bats and fielders both, but so were (in
England) Mason and Druce. Druce was included as a
hat and as a phenomenal man in the long field. Mason
ditto ditto in the slips. Stoddart’s old reputation was
built up by good work in both departments. All amateurs,
in fact, were supposed to be double headers. The real
weakness of the English team on this head lay with
Hearne and Richardson, who can both do nothing but
bowl—neither field nor bat. They are truly specialists—
men of one department—not to eftmpare with the Aus-
tra’ian howlers in the last test match, all of whom were
brilliant fields, and four out of six of them good bats as
well.”

Record-making bicycle firms and riders have
apparently a deep love for one another. Virgin started
off lately to knock-out the time for the ride from Broken
Hill to Adelaide ; and, later on, it was found that the
track had been carefully strewn with wire nails and
other favors. But he negotiated these, only to break
down further on, and do a few bush-miles on his rim.
• * * •
From the detailed account of the Jackson-Jef-
fries fight, it looks as if Peter badly wanted a sound ad-
viser In hisjiorner. From the outset, Jackson, instead of
keeping sledgehammer left playing upon Jeffries’
frontispiece until an opening appeared for the right,
began to land his right under Jeffries’ heart, a blow
which left him open to the white’s most dangerous re-
turn —a round-and-upward swing with the left. Jackson
ducked under several of them with old-time agility, but a
couple whizzed dangerously close. The end came in the
second round, when Jackson landed a right under the
heart, and, ere he could disengage, Jeffries had placed
his fateful left hook" on Jackson’s chin.
* * • *
“ Scribo ” : Re Gerald Quealey — the smug props,
of Bendigo Independent should have been the last, to
cavil at their minion’s amateur pugilism, as it more than
once stood that paper in good stead. Once Quealey was
at a public meeting of miners, and when a motion for the
exclusion of the press was carried, the Independent man
told the meeting it would have to exclude him by force.
No one accepted the long man’s defl., and next day the
Independent had a full account.
* • * •
Dear Bulletin.—Your serious acceptance of
M. Henri Gilbert, the French pedestrian whose portrait
didn’t adorn a recent issue of The Bulletin, has almost
moved me to tears. Said Gilbert professes to be engaged
upon a five years’ walk round the world for a wager of
£lO,OOO. Who checks his walk to see that he doesn’t get
“lifts” by the way? Who laid the wager against this
idiotic achievement, and, if anybody did lay it, was the
money posted ? Also, who is holding the stakes—£2o,ooo
—and drawing a comfortable income for five years as
interest on the money? Moreover, if the wager was laid,
without any posting of the money, is it likely that the
layer will cash up a fortune when the absurd Gilbert
comes to claim it ? Will he not slap the pedestrian on the
back and congratulate him on having duly carried out -a
good joke? Gilbert's wager isn’t worth much, I fancy.
As for his expectation of making “ another £lO,OOO by a
book about his journey”—well, what sort of a book
would the brain of a five-years’ walker be likely to
produce ?—St. Thomas.

In Sydney senior football, the Wentworth Club
has disbanded, and the Sydney and Burwood are two new
clubs, ambitious for senior honors. On Saturday last the
Sydneys showed themselves a strong lot by running over
Pirates by 26 points to nil. Wallaroo beat Burwood in
their maiden game by 11 points to 3.

Australian Steve O’Donnell had still another go
recently with Peter Maher, when he received the usual
drubbing. Strange that O’D. cannot drop on to some-
thing softer than the clever, hard-hitting Irishman. This
is the third time Maher has knocked him out.

Dear B. — A friend of mine at Albert Nyanza
writes re a big Congo man-ape that plays cricket
amongst the iocal nigs. His bowling pace is so
terrific that batsman and wicket-keeper sometimes
funk and hide themselves behind a neighboring
gu n-tree. He fields with his tail and feet, and the way
he gathers in the ball is one more proof of the ape’s
supremacy in games like cricket. . . . Will try and get
him over here for next season. He is a most gentlemanly
monkey, and never chyaks the umpire. He is also im-
pervious to flies and newspaper-talk, and his throat never
gets out of order. His intelligence is slightly above the
average chimpanzee's, and his articles and cricket chatter
should bring crusts for himself and me.-
ALBA D.

It has often been remarked in Bananaland
that the Chow is very generous to hospitals and
charities. T’other day, in a Bakblox (Q.) town,
a Chinese shopkeeper was solicited for a subscrip-
tion to some benevolent object. John pleaded
poverty, but opened his money-box all the same
and donated the contents—haif-a-crown in small
coins. ... It turned out afterwards to be the
whole of the Celestial’s takings for the day.

Alas ! The average man never gets any flowers

Interesting correspondence :
To the Editor op the “Cathodic Press.”
Sir,—Kindly let me know the amount of my subscrip-
tion to date, as I wish to pay it, and discontinue the
paper.
A journal which gives Mr. B. R. Wise a free hand in its
leading columns is not one for which I am prepared to
pay much.—Yours, &c. t L. F. HEYDON.
[Mr. Wise has never written, or inspired, a line in the
leading columns of the Catholic Press. Comment on
Mr. Heydon’s impudence and on his insult to Mr. Wise is
unnecessary. It is fortunate that men of this stamp are
no longer regarded by anyone as representative Catholics.
Mr. Heydon’s support of this paper has been worth less
than a penny a week. Had he withdrawn twelve months
ago, a collapse might have been inevitable, hut we are so
firmly established now that we may be able to survive the
shock.—Ed. C.P.I
“ Cosmos” sends to The Bulletin a fore-
cast on the Yanko-Spanish war :
If discipline 'and experience mean anything at all, a
pitched battle between equal forces on Cuban soil should
result in an American defeat. What sort of officers are
turned out of West Point we don't really know, but the
arm) is largely recruited from emigrants, and is cer-
tainly not noted for uniformity, physique, or discipline.
Ex-Civil and Mexican war men are rare in the modern
army, and, barring a few Indian skirmishes, there is a
painful lack of practical field-work. So that the bel-
ligerents will practically consist of veterans against a semi-
disciplined mob. The Cubans have been systematically
aided by the big Am nican rings, which are now going to
grab the island for themselves. The “ humanity ” yarn is
obviously mere cant. lie the talk of “privateering”:
Spain would have the advantage, owing to her different
bases for such operations and the great number of Ameri-
can sailing vessels. Further, there is no real patriotism
in the States, and the commissariat and other work will
have to be carefully guarded against the desire to sacrifice
everything to profit. Also, it is alleged that certain
eastern regiments and arsenals are really in the hands
of monopolists who fitted and maintain them
as a guard against internal troubles. Spain is
a decayed nati" and will probably lose through
want of cash, but the struggle may hurst
the bubble of American power and show Russia and co.
that Britain's prospective ally is not greatly to be
feared. As for the navies, America possesses the heavier
tonnage and gunnage, but torpedoes are nasty things,
and modern battleships are as weak as they are powerful.
Anyhow, America’s action is merely a repetition of Bri-
tain's Egyptian affair—the international power being used
on behalf of private rings and trusts.
“ Never mind everybody cutting you when
down. When you’re up, you won’t want to know
anybody.”
Baked-banana is found in South America,
says the Journal of Horticulture, an excellent
substitute for meat for the weak-stomached and
dyspeptic. The two ends are cut off, and the
bananas.baked in their jackets and so served. The
banana is, by the guest, split lengthwise, and but-
tered so as to supply the food-fat necessary.
