“ Assume a truculency if you have it not.
Cultivate a fire-eating manner and a selfish,
haughty disregard for the comfort of others, and
you will command respect. In a railway compart-
ment the strong man, especially if he wear a silk
hat, always gets the choice corner, while the apolo-
getic person is shoved in the draughty seat, and is
cursed if he tries to put the window up.”—London
Clarion.
The Chinaman’s method of “ fossick-
ing ” on Australian goldfields is crude enough, but
it is mechanical perfection compared with the
aboriginal practices of his own country. At
Tjeshui goldfield, the miners dig narrow,
winding burrows from the surface to the veins,
the quartz being slowly loosened with a sort of
maul and wedge. The stone is carried in baskets
to local hot springs, where it is broken by hand,
and crushed in an ordinary (i.e., extraordinary)
Chinese mill, consisting of a flat millstone, with a
wooden rod sunk in its centre, around which turns
a heavy stone roller see in a frame, and drawn by
a blindfolded donkey. The result is washed on
inclined tables, and finally panned off in wooden
bowls, certain contents of magnetic ironsand being
extracted per loadstone.
Brisbane (Q.) whisky, on the average, is
the vilest liquor drunk by human beings. The
ordinary Brisbane poison styled whisky is a dirty,
low-grade, oily, stinking, fiery fluid, chock-full of
bite and grip and greasiness combined, that costs
the publican 6s. per case in bond. Six shillings
for bottles, corks, capsules, labels, case, packing,
for all the labor involved in production, for the
profit of the manufacturer, and —for the liquor !
Sixpence a bottle ! The bottle itself, with cork,
gum, label, capsule, and its contributing share of
expenses towards the case, must cost at least Id.
Fivepence for a pint of the Concentrated Lees of
Pandemonium ! And yet it is not much worse
than Sydney whisky, of which suoh hard things
are not being said just now. One case of real good
whisky, genuinely labelled, and of undoubted
bona-fides as to maker, capsule and cork, should
last a Brisbane publican who knows his business
a lifetime and-a-half —unless he drinks whisky
himself. The wise publican never parts with such
a bottle, except under special circumstances of
urgency or compulsion, and always pays the top
bottle price for well-preserved empties. Take of
kerosene, methylated spirit, axle-grease, spoilt
turpentine, and mixed boozers’ leavings, equal
parts ; dilute copiously with stagnant water ; add
cayenne-pepper and bilge-sweepings according to
taste; soak in the decoction for a few days brown
paper and semi-burnt linen and woollen-rags,
defunct lucifers, and any odds-and-ends suffi-
ciently dirty ; strain through a greasy rag ;
clarify with whito-of-egg, and color to various
tints with different strengths of water solutions of
burnt sugar—and there you have Brisbane imita-
tions of all the whisky brands on earth.
“ Ex-Dominie ” growls from Maoriland:—
English grammar is in need of radical reform. Local
children are taught an immense amount of rules and
definitions which I, who profess a knowledge of literature
and criticism, am wholly unable to understand. Ask a
school-teacher what an abstract noun is, and you will find
he has to confess he does not know. Make him write
down the definition he gives children and you will see at
once it is absurd. Then ask him to classify nouns and he
will give you a list, including, perhaps, common, abstract
concrete, individual, material, collective. This is not
classification, it is mere waste of time. It is as foolish as
if I classified animals as carnivorous, domestic, hairy,
cold-blooded, white, and noisy. He will define a pronoun
as a word that stands for a noun. Yet the first sentence
you take, such as “ Who killed Cock Robin ?” upsets his
definition. I wonder what noun “ Who” stands for ?
The writing taught is not writing at all, but a kind of
painting, and can only be done so slowly that it has to be
relearnt when the boy enters an office.
With regard to cleanliness, it is well-known, and you
can put my name at the end of this par. if you like, that
the younger children are extremely—only the Saxon
word will express it—lousy, and actually pick the insects
off and put them on the few clean ones. I have urged
this matter on the inspector, who says “ I have no sense
of decency.” I had charge of a school once myself, and
the first time the presence of vermin was noticed I went
through every head personally, and will guarantee they
were absolutely clean when they left me. The fury of the
parents passes description. However, I escaped with my
life, and the children brought no more vermin t.o the
school while I had it. I considered it m.v plain duty to
act as I did ; what sheep-farmer would retain the services
of an overseer who allowed the sheep to suffer in this
way ? And are not ye of more value than many jum-
bucks ?
A Perth (W.A.) advertisement puts the
thing straight, without any unnecessary blushful-
ness :
TJUILDER, 28, has nice home, wishes Christian Girl
Companion.
Most people make a thin pretence of having a
‘‘view to matrimony,” but the builder, aged 28,
simply wants a girl, and says so in as few words as
possible.
Lately there has been a considerable
migration from Broken Hill on account of the
removal of the smelting from the mines to the
S.A. seaboard. While in Broken Hill the work-
men, bossed by the Labor coterie in Sydney, were
opposed to Federation because, under the Bill,
the “little States bossed the show.” And
now, being in S.A. and more or less bossed by the
Labor coterie in Adelaide, they still “barrack”
against the Bill on the ground that the “smaller
States will be outvoted by the two big ones.” It
is hard to be an anti-Federalist when you have to
hustle for a living in two provinces during the one
campaign.
In deprecation of the candidature of Henry
Copeland (the sitting member) for Phillip division
in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly, John Norton (who has
been nominated by the local Protectionist Union
for the seat, despite Copeland’s long and able
services to the party) prints in Sydney Truth a
reference to “the high-minded but unnecessary
resignation of Mr. R. D. Meagher from the seat
he had triumphantly won.” The Bulletin pre-
sumes that Norton wasn’t looking when this got
into the paper. Also, should Norton stand for
Phillip he will be beaten, and so will Copeland;
the seat will go to a Freetrader.
Re Bulletin’s recent article about preva-
lent Australian false-labelling of food-goods, it is
refreshing to note the style in which a London
magistrate recently served it out to the Junior
Army and Navy Stores people—swell crowd
though they think themselves —for vending Ameri-
can hams as Irish. An “organised system ” was
testified to, by which “ suitable-looking American
hams were cut short in the shank, warmed over
the gas, rubbed with peameal and oil, and sold to
consumers as Irish, Wiltshire, or smoked York, at
10id. to Is. 2d. a pound, the price of Americans
being 7id. to Bd.” The employes concerned de-
posed that they would have been promptly sacked
had they breathed a word of such doings, and
that “they were paid a penny commission on
every ham so palmed off, but nothing on hams
sold for what they really were.” Fined £l2, and
£3l 16s. costs!
Federation poets may well despair of find-
ing anything fresh to say on that theme, when
they remember that there is scarcely a verse-
writer in Australia for the last 40 years who has
not expressed his emotions about it. Chance has
brought to notice a poem by Charles Bright, pub-
lished in the long-defunct Melb. publication, My
Note-Book, as far back as March, ’57. The poem
is called “The Whisperings of the Wire,” and it
rhapsodises prettily about the civilising and unify-
ing efficacy of telegraphs, then being erected.
Hence these two verses, addressed to Australia
generally :
Bind up your broken countries—bind,
And make your magic girdles meet; —
And when the lightning vein’s complete,
Let its electric pulses beat,
Obeying one Australian mind 1
I hear the whispering of the wire 2
A Federal union will be
The first-fruits of its agency;
The last, a nationality
Whose light shall make the world admire.
Lazarus : I have slept out, in winter. It
is ever worse to think of than to do. There is a
tempering to the shorn. It is a poor neophyte
who takes the benches in the park. Stranded in
a city, you must always go to an outlying suburb,
and are bound to find a doss. The other Sunday
night, as I was going to church, two wagsters ac-
costed me, judging me one who knew my way
round, and they wanted a recommendation.
“ Just here,’’said I. The Here was the municipal
sheds, where one could nick a lee with the wind
from any quarter. But, if lazy, I can fudge about
inside a city any night. One rather cold night I
put in part coiled under a door-portico, part in a
new half-finished shop, and part sitting over a jet
of steam that came out of a flour-mill. This beat
any fire, and warmed me all round. When a man
sticks me up on a fairly warm night, I give him a
supper, and recommend him without compunction
to the stars. Three hundred nights of the year, in
Melb., are clear starlight, two hundred cloudless.
Rain is the most depressing thing. The beauty of
sleeping out is that there is no getting out of bed.
You are really introduced to the world of nature.
You have leisure for Arcturus, Orion, Pleiades,
and the Chambers of the South. How beautiful
is the study of noises, all the hum and tinkle of a
great city. Almost equally delicious with the in-
extricable, mysterious noises of the bush forest,
made up of “ tweets,” tappings, sighings, groan-
ings, flaps, grunts, and what not in eternal concert.
And how the dosser (some dossers) loves a sunrise !
He is like Goethe’s Faust, awakened on the bed of
roses by the myriad of little ’skeeter fairies flutter-
ing around.

Scene : Early morning on spick-and-span
saloon-deck of ocean steamer. Frst officer comes
along suddenly and finds common sailor-man
sauntering carelessly about chewing tobacco.
“What the do you mean? There you go
walking up and down with your hands in your
pockets spitting on my clean deck like a blanky
passenger ! ”
South Australia will accept the Constitu-
tion Bill; but if there were a 20,000 minimum
vote required the result would be very doubtful.
Not even the enthusiastic and, at times, eloquent
Josh Symon can get a big meeting, and the Labor-
party has abandoned hope of rousing active
opposition. Meantime a steady canvass is pro-
ceeding, especially in the north of the province
where the great lever is the Broken Hill trade,
which, without Federation, is at any time liable
to be spoiled for S.A. farmers, &c., by border
duties.
The latest Sinellbourne alleged embezzler,
at time of writing, is a clerk who seems to have
sloped from the employ of the Orient S.N. Co.
with Klondike in view. It is now about time
that some careful enquirer collated the statistics
of Victorian and N.S.W. embezzlements for the
past four years or so, in order that the effect of
the “ hush-it-up ” policy (adopted by “ representa-
tive ” persons when the Melbourne banks began to
go) may be fully understood. The offenders now
are mostly sons of the fatuous middle - aged
citizens who found excuses for the unpunished
criminals of a few years ago.
The Melbourne Jewish Literary Society
pegs away with its meetings in the Buxton Art
Gallery. At last meeting, a Mr. Robert Jones
(very un-Jewish name, Bob Jones !) gave them a
smartish lecture on “ The Humor of Error ” ; and,
next month, Messrs. Sevenson and Brennan will
debate the question: “ Whether the Victorian
Newspaper Press is failing to fulfil its true func-
tion.” Prizes have been offered for senior and
junior oratory competitions; also, the society will
hold its third annual ball, at the Masonic Hall, on
the Glorious Fourth of July.
Dear Bulletin.—A P. and O. officer has
been telling a Sydney paper that if his ocean
greyhound were surprised at sea by a hostile
cruiser he would keep her bow-on to the enemy so
as not to offer so good a mark to his guns, and try
to ram him. Any officer ought to have known
that modern guns miss more owing to errors of
elevation than those of direction. A ship re-
ceiving fire broadside-on only offers the height of
the side and the width of the deck as a target.
Bow-on she offers the whole length of her deck,
perhaps 500 feet. A shot that would fly harmlessly
overhead broadside-on might, bow-on, fall on
deck about the middle or stern of the ship. No
blizzard of death so destructive as a raking fire.
“ 0.E.H.” to The Bulletin :
Thackeray, in “ Roundabout Papers,” after speaking of
the pluck shown on board the sinking Birkenhead, says
“ read the story of the Sarah Sands.” Your recent cor-
respondent “ C.P.” has read it to small purpose ; hardly
an item in his aocount being correct. In the first place,
the s.s. was not commanded by “ Capt. Martip, of the ill-
fated London,” but by Capt. John Castle, still alive, at 76,
and a nautical assessor in England. The Sarah Sands, a
screw s.s. of 1330 tons, was chartered by the East India
co., in ’57, to convey to India a portion of the 55th Regt.,
numbering upwards of 350, besides the wives and children
of some of the men and of the officers. On Nov. 11, when
the ship was about 400 miles from Mauritius, and when
Capt. Castle was presiding at dinner, it was reported to
him that the vessel was on fire aft. He so quietly
requested the colonel to take his place that no
other person in the cabin suspected what was
amiss. The fire was in the after-hold, and burning
fiercely, but there was no confusion. Every order was
obeyed as coolly as given. The engines were stopped,
and the ship brought to the wind so as to drive the flames
astern. Attention was then directed to the powder maga-
zines, and the starboard one was soon cleared, but in
attempting to empty the port one the men became over-
powered with smoke and heat, and had to be dragged on
deck, senseless. Meantime the women and children
were put into the boats, and the boats’ crews
ordered to keep away from the ship until recalled.
Presently the powder left in the port-magazine blew up ;
but the work of fighting both the fire and a gale went on
till the crew, about midnight, got the upper hand,
though by the time the fire had been fought-out the
ship was in a desperate plight, with the port-quarter
blown out by the explosion, and 15ft. of water in the
hold. Spare sails and blankets were used to stop the
leaks, two hawsers were passed round the stern and set
up, and the troops employed baling and pumping.
Next day the boats were recalled, and the ship bore
away for Mauritius, arriving there on the 23rd. Capt.
Castle was afterwards personally' complimented by the
Queen upon his skill and courage.

That clever Australian painter and charm-
ing woman, Mrs. Ellis Rowan, was approached the
other day in Yankeeland by a well-barbered
person who, on the promise of getting her into a
“leal good tiling,” secured from the lady her
stock of pictures, valued at .£3000, for the purpose

Reform—the thought’s absurd —

“The big naval vote taken in conjunction
with the delay of the Queen’s visit to Cimiez,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“is understood here to mean nothing less than
a bluff to Hanotaux and France in general.
One of the ‘Govt, pilots’ told me today that
U .R.I. was in the best of health, for he had just
seen her, and the plea that rough weather prevents
the channel crossing is even less valid than the
illness excuse. Britain has got her hands full up
to her elbows just now, and West Africa may yet
unloose the hounds of war upon her whilst she is
badly prepared to grapple with them. Strange to
say, and evidencing how ‘ dicky ’ the position
must be, there is a rapidly growing good feeling
—amongst British naval officials, especially—for
the United States; and an Anglo-Saxon alliance
to sweep the world’s waters was earnestly discussed
by two of the ‘ Navy League’ mainmasts (if there
can be two) in my hearing. It fairly ‘bush-
whiskied ’ me ; knowing how these Anglo-
Yankees hate the other tag of the hyphen.”

How to excel at Billiards.
Only a few days ago a wonderful game of
billiards was witnessed in one of the principal
billiard saloons in this city, which fairly astonished
everyone present, inasmuch as one of the players
was known to suffer from a long-standing nervous
affection, so much so that he could scarcely hold
the cue in his hand ; but notwithstanding this he
offered his opponent (who was a professional) 50
in 100, and beat him by 20. And he gave Herr
Rassmussen’s Alfaline Vitality i'ills the
credit for his wonderful improvement.

S.S. : “ The complaint of a poor woman
with a small family in a Melbourne suburb, de-
barred by a bad surgical trouble from earning the
usual honest crust ; ‘ I cannot get assistance
from the Local Committee because my house is
clean and tidy. The ladies visited us, and,
although I was hungry at the time, and there was
no food in the house, they would give me nothing.
They looked through the rooms, and said: “Oh !
you are very comfortable here. There are many
worse off than you,” and I was refused assistance.
If my house had been untidy and my children
neglected and dirty, mine would have been con-
sidered a deserving case.’ How like the grey
tabby cat of organised charity, the purring, in-
quisitive creature who lo es to paw over a nasti-
ness and poke her thin whiskers into family
scandals. She recognises povertyonlyinonegui.se,
and it must be dirty to be really deserving ! ”
Jack Want, of N.S.W., as reported in
Telegraph of 15th April:—
This cast-iron Constitution for ever and aye which you
can never change.
Same Want (Telegraph of 23rd April):—
One of the first amendments made in the Bill would
be to chop off from - N.S. Wales. Broken Hill and the
Riverina.
It doesn’t matter in the least to Jack Want that
he yells one day that the Bill is cast-iron, and the
next that it is so flexible that it is going to be
altered immediately.
The only party in Victoria which has de-
clared bitter war against Federation is the Han-
cock end of the Labor body—the Trenwith end
being distinctly favorable. The Hancock end is
hostile because there is no mass Referendum in
the Federal Constitution. Yet a Federal Parlia-
ment with both Houses consisting of paid mem-
bers elected on' the one-man-one-vote principle is
almost certain to adopt the Referendum later on.
And if Federation is r-jected, when does Victoria
expect to extract any Referendum from its House
of 48 Rich Landlords ?
S. A. Parliament has been further prorogued
till June 23. The period during which the States
can grant bounties to local industries expires on
June 30. So far as S.A. is concerned, this effec-
tually falsifies “Jack” Want’s gruesome warning
that the other provinces were going to enact
bounty laws wholesale, and thus make interpro-
vincial Freetrade a mpre mockery. When S.A.
has opened its Parliament, and the Governor has
trailed his official rags on to the scene and
back again, and the Address has been got through,
and the Saturday and Sunday adjournment are
over, there will be no time left for passing bounty
laws.
At the frugal Eight Hours Day luncheon
in Melbourne Friendly Society’s Gardens, the
anti-Federation group of Labor-members sat back
to back with their “ chief ” Trenwith. It was
Trenwith whom the chairman called upon to
speak up for Parliament, and Trenwith had the.
support of that ancient worker in his refusal to
talk Federation! Also, Ben Tillett avoided the
bone of contention, and when it was quite evident
that nobody intended to call upon Hancock for a
wild address Hancock and his following made a
depressed departure from the tent.
The Melbourne representatives of Labor
who gathered together for a ham-sandwich
struggle on Fight Hours Day were fearfully and
wonderfully “ loyal.” They drank to the health
of the Queen, not in casual silence, but with in-
dividual shouts of the old lady’s official designa-
tion. After which a weak-minded individual, who
doubtless thought he was only doing his dooty to
the Constitooshon, struck up the Hanthem, and
found a lot of sons of labor to join him in his
foolishness.

a native of Tendooly, County Donegal, Ireland,
who left Ireland for Australia or New Zealand in the
year 1856 or 1858. Her father's name was Dennis
McCready ; mother’s maiden name was Rose Carr. They
died in the year 1854 or 1855. Nancy McCready’s brother
Janies died in Fort Smith, Arkansas, U.S.A., on the
30th day of August, 1806, leaving some property, which
Nancy or her descendants, as next of kin, are entitled to,
if they can be found.

THE TENTH REPORT
of the DIRECTORS of
The National Bank of Australasia Limited.
The Directors now present to the shareholders their Report on the transactions of the bank for the half-year,
which terminated on 31st March last. • . .
Acting on the announcement made at the general meeting in November, 1897, they have had to declare forieitea
for the non-payment of calls 10,531 shares, representing a capital value of £45,456 45., and in terms of the provision in
the Companies Act 1896 to that effect, have transferred this sum to a reserve fund.
The net profit for the six months just closed, after deducting expenses of manage-
ment and the other items enumerated in the profit and loss account amounted to £18,469 16 2
Balance from previous half-year .. .. .. .. .. •<r 34,196 13 11

In terms of the powers conferred on them in the Articles of Association (No. 113), the directors nave set aside out
of the profits the sum of £4,543 16s. as an addition to the reserve fund now instituted, making the same Fifty
Thousand Pounds. • .
The following will be the distribution of the amount left at their disposal after that appropriation, viz. :
Dividend at 5 per cent, on preference shares .. .. .. •• .. £7,644 10 0

£45,124 2 6
The paid-up capital Of the bank will now amount to £1,973,677 14s. Bd., and the reserve fund to £50,000. _
The directors have the satisfaction of pointing out that of the deferred fixed deposits, amounting originally to
£6,844,884, the sum of £5,187,044 has been released and dealt with up to date. , , ,
The Ordinary General Annual Meeting will be held at the Athenaeum, Collins-street, Melbourne, on Wednesday,
4th May, at noon. . . ._ . _ . „ , . ~
The shareholders will be called on to elect two directors on that occasion, in lieu of John Grice, Esq., and Arthur
Septimus King, Esq., who retire by rotation. There are no other candidates. They will also be required to appoint
two auditors.
By order of the Board of Directors,
The National Bank of Australasia Limited,
Head Office, Melbourne, 22nd April, 1898.
F. G. SMITH,
Chief Manager.

IUEES OF THE CONVICT SYSTEM, by

“The pompous 1 respectability of the
Bankers’ Association,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, “ has been a good deal
shaken-up of late, and all through Lord B .
But it is his generosity, and not his liquor, that
is to blame. One of the smartest of sassiety dames
called on him recently, and unfolded a piteous
tale about debts of <£2ooo, which she knew not how
to pay. Would dear Lord B help her with
a potent slip of paper? The generous-hearted
B was touched, and did the needful. But
when the document was presented by the lady
at the bank, a ‘mere nothing,’ added on the
right, had pushed the ‘2 ’ one place to the left.
The clerk queried at once, the. lady put up her
monocle and said there must be a mistake, and she
would take the cheque back, but the clerk was
obdurate. The ten-fold increased document was
submitted to the drawer, who agreed with the
mistake theory, and desired to cancel the cheque
and write another. But no, the matter was too
serious for such off-hand treatment, and the assoc,
must be called in, for had the bank paid the 20
* thou.’ it would have been held liable for the loss.”
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.

Is. bottles. Made in London *
A S.A. lady recently arrived at Coolgardie
after fighting two steamship co’s and a railway
department all the way from Adelaide, and leav-
ing them in ruins along the road. She had a hus-
band in the West, but his remittances were
irregular, and, when there came a longer interval
than usual, her money ran out, and her landlord
fired her into the street. So she stepped peace-
fully on board a steamer, and, when her ticket
was demanded which didn’t happen till they
were well out at sea—she simply explained that
she had none—also she had no assets whatever.
She was hauled up before a very grim and indig-
nant captain, and requested to explain, which she
did in the presence of an interested crowd. She
told him that he might put the vessel back if he
pleased, and send her ashore ; or he might throw
her overboard ; or he might make her work her
passage ; or he could take her clothes in payment,
though she warned him that the only clothes she
had were those she was wearing, and his conduct
wouldn’t be respectable; or he could give her
in custody at Albany, or put her in irons,
or he might hit; her with a rope-end. She
was perfectly resigned, and was willing to
take things as they came. The infuriated officer
did the only thing he well could do ; he let her be
a steerage passenger to Albany and there he gave
her ss. and chased her ashore. At Albany she
jumped another boat, and when another indignant
captain found her she offered him the same alter-
natives. Her fired her ashore at Fremantle with
an objurgation and 2s. 6d. Then she began to be
heard of on trains along the long line from Perth
to Coolgardie. Her explanation was always the
same. She had no money and no ticket; she
wouldn’t get out, but if the railway officials could
lift her out they might. When they tried to lift
her she hung on to the seat and explained that if
they liked to hit her knuckles till they made her
let go they could do so, but otherwise she meant
to hold on. Being threatened with the police she
explained that she would as soon be locked up as
not; she didn’t propose to walk to the police-station,
but if any officer was prepared to grasp her
round the waist she would hang in a perfectly
resigned condition over his shoulder with her
head and feet dangling on either side of him, and
let him do the best he could with her. Sometimes
she was thrown out; then she spent her time till
the next train was due in cleaning her boots with
grass and washing her stockings in a creek. More
often she wasn’t, and the train would depart amid
the cheers of the passengers. Anyhow, no one
ever arrested her, and she reached Coolgardie in
triumph only ten days behind schedule time—
which, for a grass-widow with no money and no
spare clothes, was not bad.

Death Was Near. She Was
Simply Fading Away.
SHE IS NOW BLOOMING WITH BEAUTY
AND RADIANT WITH PERFECT
HEALTH.
“ I am 19 years of age,” said Miss Petrea Peter-
son, of Featherston, North Island, New Zealand,
to a press representative who interviewed her last
month, “ and since I was 15 I have been suffering
more or less. I had acute palpitation of the heart,
which was brought about by anaemia or bloodless-
ness, to which I was long a martyr. Whenever I
ran upstairs my heart would thump violently
against my sides, and I would be quite speechless
for several moments, until I recovered my breath,
rsight after night I tossed, weary and worn-out,
on my bed, trying vainly to sleep. I had fearful
attacks of cramps in my legs, and so great was the
agony that frequently it caused me to scream out
in very desperation. I was always in a weak, low
state, and never felt fit for my work. My legs
would at times swell up to a considerable extent,
and this naturally occasioned myself and my
mother great suspense. Thus, as I grew older I
i>rew worse and worse, being subject also to
periods of giddiness which many a time caused
me to almost fall down. I tried denty of
medicines, and I had several doctors, but they
gave no relief. I also consulted three doctors re-
garding an affliction of the eye from which I suf-
fered. Havingalmost given up my case as hopeless,
I one day noticed an advertisement for Dr. Wil-
liams Pink Pills for Pale People in the Evening
Post. Gathering hope from this, in June, 1897,
I commenced taking them strictly according to
directions. After the third dose I found a change
for the better. I continued with them until I, bit
by bit, improved so much that four months ago I
left them off altogether. 1 have taken a total of
linn' boxes, and am now in every way in the most
perfect health. Insomnia, weakness, giddiness,
heart palpitation, .cramps, and swelling in the
legs have all disapr>eared, and the affection in my
eye has likewise ceased. As there has been no re-
currence of these ailments at all during the past
four months, it is evident that my cure is a per-
manent one. The pills have absolutely cured me,
and I will always keep some of them' by me. I
feel fit for my work and for anything. My mother
thinks the. world of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, and she has tried several boxes her-
self. lam awfully thankful to them, and I have
recommended them to all my friends. Four of
them speak exactly as I do of the Pills, and can-
not praise them too highly. I am well-known
here, and numbers of people have been watching
my case with interest. You can use this testi-
monial in any way you desire.”
Afflicted with Rheumatism at
the Age of 80.
HE IS NOW THOROUGHLY CURED AND
FREE FROM PAIN.
When a gentleman has reached the age of 80
years and is then attacked by acute rheumatism
so severely that at times he is only able to get out
of bed with the utmost difficulty, the case would
to most people seem incurable, and so the gentle-
man himself thought. But a course of Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People has thoroughly
changed his ideas. Last September twelvemonths
Mr. David Robertson, of Byron-street, Kew, Vic-
toria, who is over 80 years of age, was attacked
most virulently by rheumatism, first in the right
knee, next in the left hip, and then in the shoul-
ders, wrists, hands, knees, and legs. He was
rendered entirely helpless. Every friend he had
recommended different remedies in turn—sulphur,
kerosene, outwardly and inwardly, citric acid)
marsh mallow, fomentations, hot sea baths)
several bottles of a largely-advertised cure for
rheumatism, and other articles were all tried
without the slightest benefit accruing. Ultimately
Mr. Robertson came to the conclusion that his ail-
ment was incurable. A little while later, however a
friend induced him to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
He followed the directions carefully, and patiently)
in about three weeks or a month after first taking
them, he noticed a considerable improvement in
his condition ; he had then taken two boxes. He
persevered with the remedy, and, little by little
his ailment gave him less and less trouble. He
continued in this manner until he had taken about
10 or 11 boxes, the time occupied being from six
to seven months. At this stage, every trace of
rheumatism had left him, and he was absolutely
and perfectly cured in every particular. He is
now quite free from pain. Mr. Robertson, in con-
elusion, said he had recommended Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills to many of his friends, and they had
received most beneficial results from their use.
He was well known in the locality, and certainly
continues to recommend them.
This case is one which amply demonstrated the
marvellous curative properties of this universal
medicine, under the most adverse circumstances,
ff an old gentleman of 80 years of age can be cured
cf rheumatism, what may not be effected in those
cases in which the sufferer is a much younger man
cr woman ? The answer is too obvious to need
insertion.
Liver Complaint, Weak Stomach, and General Debility.
ANOTHER CURE COMES FROM MELBOURNE.
Mrs. Elizabeth Duggan, of “ Lochiel House,” 525 Latrobe-street, West Melbourne, is a lady who
has been most marvellously restored to health by a course of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People
after she had suffered most acutely for years from chronic indigestion, liver complaint, and general
debility. Seen recently by a reporter, she chatted as follows : —“ lam 36 years old, and have been a
resident of West Melbourne all my life. About two years ago my health broke down, my stomach
wouldn t contain its food, and I sank into a terrrtfiy low condition. Even brandy and egg would not
stay on my stomach for more than five minutes. Sleep deserted me, and I often wished I were dead,
as I was a nuisance to myself and a burden to others. I was admitted to both St. Vincent’s and the
Alfred Hospitals, and, although I received the greatest kindness and attention at both, I was really a
wreck when discharged, and quite unfit to carry on my duties. Several doctors also attended me, but
did me no good at all. I took a large number of patent medicines, and these likewise had no
beneficial effect. The pains in the back of my neck, the excessive weakness of my sight, severe
headaches, giddy sensations, and general weakness never deserted me. About nine months ago I was
reluctantly persuaded by a young lady to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. Now I look
back to the day I started taking them as the luckiest in my life,” declared Mrs. Duggan enthusiasti-
cally. “ Altogether I took ten boxes. The second box produced a wonderful effect, and this became
more and more pronounced as I continued with them. My appetite has returned ; my indigestion,
pains, dizziness, sleeplessness, and weakness have vanished. I am up with the lark. My work is a
pleasure instead of a distress, and the longest day is never too long. I start work sometimes at five in
the morning, and keep on till late at night; yet I have no feeling of tiredness or langour, and for this
lam indebted to Dr. Williams Pink Pills. A number of my friends are taking them upon my recom-
mendation, with good results, and as my little girl is not well I intend giving her some ; X am sure
they will cure her. One of my doctors told me I was suffering from indigestion and liver complaint,
but he did nothing for me. Make whatever use you like of this testimonial.”
A Prostrated Farmer Who was Unable to Walk.
HE CAN NOW WALK 30 MILES A DAY.
“ I hear, Mr. Batchelor,” said a reporter recently to that gentleman at his farm at Horton
River. N.S.W., “ that you have just experienced a remarkable recovery from a very long illness.”
“ I have, indeed.”
“ Would you mind narrating the particulars of your case to us for publication ?”
“ Certainly I will, for I think the case is one which should have all the publicity possible. lam
sixty-five years of age, and have been resident in this district for about twelve years. Prior to my
coming here, I had always enjoyed the best of health, and I had been accustomed to work very hard.
About six years ago, however, all my energy seemed to gradually desert me. I suffered acutely and
constantly from pains in my limbs, lost my appetite, and for months at a stretch I did not know
r

what it was to get a good night’s sleep. Work of the very lightest
nature at last became beyond the question with me, and I could
do nothing but sit by the window and content myself by watching
the others work the farm. I gave up all hope of ever being able to
do any more work, and almost believed that every week which
commenced would be my last. In my endeavors to stay the
ravages of the ailment which had overtaken me, and was gradually
drawing me into the grave, I tried innumerable so-called cures, and
swallowed an unconscionable quantity of medical compounds, but
beyond giving me temporary relief occasionally, they, none of
them, did me any good ; in fact, I honestly believe that the
majority of them only aggravated my sufferings. I was just on
the point of giving up in despair a few months back when I hap-
pened to read in a newspaper of some wonderful cures effected by
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and I determined to give
them a trial. After taking the first box, I felt considerably re-
lieved, and so marked was the improvement I noticed in my
strength that I desired to continue their use. I did so, and up to
the present time I have taken five boxes. My inability has all dis-
appeared, and I am pleased to say that to-day I am as strong and
hearty as I ever was in my life. It is no trouble to me to do a
day’s farm work now, and I feel quite equal to the task of walking
at least thirty miles a day. Everyone who knows me can testify
to the great cure these Pills have effected in me, and in recomend-
ing them for cases similar to mine I can honestly say that they are
. ._, , worth more than their weight in gold. If everybody knew their
true value, there would be no other medicines sold.
TOO LITTLE BLOOD.
Too little blood ! That is what makes men and women look
pale, sallow, and languid. That is what makes them drag along,
always tired, never hungry, unable
to digest their food, breathless
and palpitating at the heart after
slight exertion, so that it is a
trouble to go upstairs. They are
“Anaemic,” doctors tell them ; and
that is Greek for having “ too
little blood.”
Are you like that? Have you
too little blood ? Pull down your
lower lip—are your gums pale in-
stead of being scarlet? Pull down
your lower eyelid—is the lining of
it bloodshot and pale ? That i?
where “too little blood” shows.
More anaemic people have been
made strong, hungry, energetic,
cheerful men and women by
Dr. Williams’

Fifteen Years of Violent
Indigestion.
A WOMAN’S MARVELLOUS RELIEF.
Whether Mrs. Boyes, now residing at 162
Dowhng-street, Sydney, merited the 15 years
torture she has just completed, we will leave an*
unbiased public to say. Certain it is that but
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People her
sufferings would have been even more prolonged.
“For fifteen
years I suffered
from indiges-
tion,” said Mrs.
Boyes to a re-
porter from the
Leichhardt
Standard, and
continued she,
“a long, weary
time to be ill,
and I might
have been suf-
fering still if I
had not the good
fortune to drop
on a paragraph
in the papers
describing a cure
I
by Dr. Williams' Pink Pills for Pale People in a
case very similar to mine. Though I always had
indigestion vvith me, it came on much more
violently at times than was usual. The attacks
were at first comparatively light, but as years
went on they became more and more severe.
They commenced with a feeling of sickness and
a disinclination to eat, and when I did manage
to eat some light food it would lie on my chest in
a heavy, immovable lump. The sensation was
most oppressive and distressing, and sometimes
I was in such pain that I could hardly sleep,' and
often I would wake up in the middle of the night
with a, start and not get any more sleep till the
morning, and when I did get out of bed I would
feel depressed in spirits, and quite overcome by a
feeling of lassitude and weariness. I had violent
headaches, and a most awkward giddiness when I
got up suddenly from a sitting posture; I also
suffered from a continual bad breath and an un-
pleasant taste in the mouth. I also had bad
bilious turns. I was not as you see me now, but
was much thinner, with a pale, unhealthy com-
plexion. During all these long years, in addition
to the attention of doctors, I tried a large number
of other remedies, but none of them did me an
atom of good. At length I tried Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. The effect was astonishing: after
taking them for only a short time the attack I was
then suffering from was completely got rid of. I
had a sound, healthy appetite, which I hadn’t had
for years, and my sleep was no longer disturbed.
I took altogether about 12 boxes, and the Pills
completely cured me of both my indigestion and
biliousness, and I have never had the least return
of these complaints, or, in fact, of any illness
since.”
Mrs. Boyes is at present quite a picture of
health and strength.
Pale and Sallow Girls.
HOW THEY MAY FADE AWAY.
What can be more distressing than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth.
Instead of bright eyes, glowing, rosy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, yellow, or
greenish complexion, and a languidness of step
that bespeaks disease and an early death if proper
treatment is not promptly resorted to and per-
sisted in until the impoverished blood is enriched,
and the functions of life become regular.
Upon parents rests a great responsibility at
the time their daughters are budding into
womanhood. If your daughter is pale, com-
plains of weakness, is “tired out” upon the
slightest exertion; if she is troubled with head-
ache or backache, pain in the side, if her temper is
fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition
of extreme peril, a fit subject for the development
for the most dreaded of all diseases—Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly ; they will enrich the blood and restore
health s roses to the cheeks; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and
all danger of consumption and premature death
wdl be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pills upon the approach of the period of woman-
hood, and thus avoid the risks of disease or early
decay.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are
not a patent medicine, but are a thoroughly
scientific preparation, the result of years of care-
ful study on the part of an eminent Edinburgh
University physician, and they were successfully
used by him in his everyday practice for years
before being offered for general sale. They are
a perfect blood builder and a nerve restorer,
curing such diseases as rheumatism, neuralgia,
partial paralysis, locomotor ataxia, St. Vitus’
dance, nervous headache, general debility, ner-
vous prostration, the after effects of la grippe,
influenza, and severe colds, diseases depending
on humours in the blood, such as scrofula,
chronic erysipelas, etc. Pink Pills give a healthy
glow to pale and sallow complexions, and are a
specific for all troubles peculiar to the female sys-
tem, such as anaemia, poor and watery blood,
female irregularities, etc. In the case of men they
effect a radical cure in all cases arising from men-
tal worry, over-.vnrk, or excesses of any nature.
These Pills are not a purgative. They are
genuine only with the full name, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People, and are sold by all
chemists and by the Dr. Williams’ Med'icine
Company, Sydney, who will forward (post paid)
on receipt of stamps or post order, one box for
2s. fid., or half-a-dozen for 15s. 3d. They are
unrivalled as a tonic for both sexes.

The Ways of Many Waters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Because of a painted fancy,
That is neither old nor new,
The path of the further distance
It seemeth for aye more true ;
For this have the Dreamers wandered
Forlorn, on a golden quest,
Their sails, in the sunset dipping
Aslant to the reddened West
For this have the Rovers journeyed,
Subtle and strange though it seem,
Spelled by the shade of a shadow,
Lured by the loot of a dream ;
And so doth the Great Fleet gather.
The fleet of a thousand sail,
With a long-oared galley leading,
And a liner at the tail.
They sweep with a song from Sidon
The song of an old desire,
They come with a crash of trumpets
Out from the quays of Tyre.
Along on the open waters
Do their leaping galleys line,
To trade with our tattooed fathers
The trinkets of Palestine.
Evoe! and a cup to Bacchus
The Lycian seaman pours,
Then kisses his dark-haired Phryne,
And springs to the straining oars.
Hard down, by the mole at Pharos
The Rhodian Ketos bides
The hour of the sacred augur,
The time of the wheeling tides.
They swing from the yellow Tiber
Into the laughing seas,
With gifts to the gods in passing
The pillars of Hercules ;
The gleam of Imperial purple
On Imperial ocean falls,
The flag of the legion flutters,
The stern centurion calls.
Now, loud is the shout of wassail,
And the Northern Eagle shrieks
As the Viking’s men come crowding
Out from the bays and creeks—
bons of the snows and the forests,
High in the forehead and bold,
Strong with the love of strong women,
Sturdy to take and to hold.
They glide, with a chant of lovers,
Into the sleeping lagune ;
I he sails of the Great Doge gleaming
Silver and silk in the moon—
While far in the east she glimmers
On Indian argosies,
Gallantly bearing the Mogul
Rich gifts from the Genoese.
But now ’tis a rowdy rabble
That chatters on Palos pier,
As up from the Unknown Ocean
A tom sail l ist’s clear,
And a calm world-finder cometh
Not as the Conquerors came—
Loud with the blaze of pennons,
Clamoring favor of Fame.
And lo ! from an English harbor,
In his jerkin brown a rose,
With a broad sword in his scabbard.
The sturdy John Cabot goes
Westward, and westward for ever,
But ever of stout intent,
To claim for his burly monarch
His share of a continent.
And now ’tis a white-haired Spaniard,
Seeking in travail and ruth
The place of the fabled waters,
The fount of enduring youth.
The gallants of gay De Soto
Bear out on the seas again,
And Cortez, with banners trailing,
Heels down for the Western main.
The shout of Balbao echoes
Across the Pacific waste ;
And free from St. Malo harbor
Brave Cartier wears in haste ;
The sun on their mail to glisten,
The sun on their swords to glance—
A kiss for the mistress weeping,
Then, hey for the lilies of France 1
They waddle away together,
Round-bellied, from Rotterdam,
To trade in the Eastern Islands
Or barter at Surinam ;
Or, far to the south’ard driven,
They see with a startled gaze
The hills of the mystic Java
Loom up from the tropic haze.
Now low on the Southern oceans
The gleam of their lonely sails,
Where Tasman, undaunted, has weathered
The Cape of a Thousand Gales;
Where Hartog is boldly sailing
Into Australian seas,
One eye on the chance of plunder,
And one on the Portuguese.
They dart from the nooks and the crannies,
White eagles athirst for prey,
Room for a little adventure,
And plenty of room to play;
With letters-of-marque that cover
A slip if it endeth so,
And back to their friendly harbors
Full tilt, with a prize in tow.
They stand with their port-fires lighted
To rake him over and through,
For the sake of his golden ingots
And the sake of derring-do ;
They riddle his timbers gaily,
And up on his high decks spring—
One thrust for the English lasses,
And one for their English king 1
They reel, with a drunken chanty.
Loading their swivels amain,
Be-ribanded robbers cheering,
Their black flag up to the main ;
The pick of their ocean plunder
The loot of a half-score loads,
To scatter among the ladies—
Of pleasure—in Whydah Roads.
And a low, black hull still crosses
The face of the moon away,
And again the night re-echoes
The shout of the turbaned Dey;
And the night-wind moans and shivers,
But the Dago seaman swears
’Tis a ghostly Rover chiding
His Barbary Corsairs !
The Company’s fleet is booming
Along on the sou’-east trade,
And the brave East-India clipper
On her outward course is laid;
She cheers to the passing troopship
That buckles into the gale,
A reef in her straining topsails,
The red rag over the rail.
They roll from the docks of Lunnou ;
And out of Cork Harbor go
The immigrant tubs full-listed,
“God bless ye ! ” and “South’md ho !”
Ay, south’ard and south’ard ever,
The gallant old ships of teak,
That lie at the banks o’ Yarra,
With their spreading yards apeak.
Ay, south’ard and westward bravely,
Since ever the years were born,
They battle the wild Atlantic,
They battle around the Horn ;
With the California clipper
Dainty and deep in the beam,
And the Austral clipper racing
Ahead of the days of steam !
’Tis a lordly, long convention,
Foregathering day by day,
From the Mayflower bravely beating
Her passage to Cape Cod Bay,
From the trim old wooden traders
Who smuggled their silks and Lee,
To the steel-built Cunard packet
In the pride of her peerless pac e.
They sleep in the deep, dark places,
The fleets of the days gone by;
But oft when the flaked sea-fires
To the churning screw-beats fly,
With the sound of a faint, sad music,
The lilt of an old-time tune,
They rise from their grave of waters,
To ride ’neath the quiet moon.
The ships of the Dreamers gather,
They gather at dead of night,
Till the face of the deep, dark places
Y ith their crowding sails grows white ;
And then, in a grand procession,
Away to the West they sail,
With a long-oared galley leading
And a liner at the tail.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
The president of Melb. Alfred Hospital
lately reminded young doctors, who might be in-
tending to apply for appointments as “ residents,”
that they were not expected to make a permanent
position of the office. “It was only for the pur-
pose of gaining experience on the bodies of the
patients, of course. “ Trying it on the dog.”

of The Art of Living in Australia ” and kindred

mending it.
In Arcady,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For miles along the Hunter (NS.W.) are to be
seen the galvauised-iron and slab habitations of
easy-going cockies who have evidently not yet
learned the use of rainwater. No spouting adorns
their roofs, and such a thing as a tank is un-
known. Water for the household is obtained
from the river per medium of a more or less
dilapidated horse and a sledged water-cask. A
farmer’s wife may occasionally be seen heaving a
chunk of wood at a mangy cattle-dog as he is en-
deavoring to disentangle his head from the hole in
the cask, after he has had his fill; and the farmer
himself generally has to curse his thirsty fowls 17
times a day, as they try, by various conjuring tricks,
to reach the water in the cask at low tide. Some-
times an unfortunate “ chick ” falls into the
family reservoir and remains there till his
effluvium makes an approach to his watery grave
anything but a pleasure-trip. What remains of
him is then fished out and the cask is given a
brush-up for appearance sake.
Small wonder that a typhoid epidemic every
now and then gives the agricultural portion of the
Hunter districts a look-up, and sends a few con-
signments over the border. But typhoid doesn’t
trouble a Hunter cockie, because on that part of
the river it is customary to wake a defunct—and
the more wakes the merrier !
A recent Hunter wake took the cake. After all
and sundry had expressed their sympathy and
called the corpse “fine fellow,” “good-natured
soul,” etc., a large quantity of muddy red wine
and snaky whisky was introduced to drown the
general sorrow, and when the talking power of the
mourners had got into full blast, a large, hairy
dairyman began to tell how many pounds of butter
his little “ yaller ” heifer, that was out of Bill
King’s prize cow, yielded in a week. The wake
proper then began, and for ten minutes nothing
could be heard but “pounds,” “quarts,”
“brindle cows,” “South-coasters,” “churns,”
“separators,” “green feed,” and “Ayrshire.”
Then, after every man on the premises had called
every other man twenty-seven kinds of variegated
liar, about a dozen of the arguers hung their Sun-
day coats on the fence r and stepped out into the
milking-yard.
The corpse was forgotten, and the rest of the
company came out and “ sooled ” on the twelve. It
was a glorious scrimmage, with fists, feet, sticks,
and even half-bricks ; and, when the fighters were
quite played-out, the proceedings were declared to
be over, and everybody was invited to the funeral,
next afternoon.
These Hunter cockies would wake a man f
he had died of leprosy, as long as they could make
“ a good night ”of it. If typhoid raged all the
year round they would never look to the state of
their drinking-water; and if you explained to
them the connection between the two matters
they would inform you that when the Lord calls
you, you’ve got to go—water or no water.
A. A. A.

A Marriage Examining-Board.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A reformer in the Ohio Legislature has introduced a
Bill requiring persons applying for marriage-licences to
pass a medical examination. The Bill forbids the issue of
a licence to any person suffering from dipsomania, in-
sanity or tuberculosis. It provides for a Marriage Ex-
amining-Board of three physicians in each county. A
measure of this kind has been advocated by many re-
formers of society from Sir Thomas More down to our
own own day, but if science is strong human nature is
stronger. It is pretty certain that love, which laughs at
locksmiths, will not show more respect for medical certifi-
cates.— British Medical Journal, Feb. 26. 1898.
They are up-to-date in Ohio State,
For they deal out laws from the shoulder straight;
They’re passing a Bill to preserve the use
Of the marriage-license against abuse ;
And you can’t get married, whoever you be,
Without the consent of a board of three
Grave medical men. It isn’t enough
The consent of your girl and her father gruff—
You both must confront this board irate
Or you can’t get spliced in Ohio State.
’Tis hard indeed that the heart should bleed
For the sins of the flesh the disease-germs breed :
The hectic flush on your true love’s cheek
Is the sweet blush rose that ever we seek,
And the clear, white skin of her brow and neck,
The silken locks that her shoulders deck,
Bespeak her a fairy—the daintiest girl—
With long, curving lashes, red lips set on pearl,
And a brilliant vivacity lightens the whole
As her pupils dilate with the depth of her soul,
A charm so resistless her witchery proves
Man looks and is dazzled, she smiles, and he loves.
The blight’s in the bud, the moth’s in the fruit,
The germ’s in the cell, the worm’s at the root;
Remorse stings the soul, grief gnaws at the heart,
Or matter or mind is diseased in each part.
Our noblest endeavours soon falter and flag,
To-day we are zealots, to-morrow we lag ;
A world of old “ chronics ” in body and mind,
Each built for each other, blind leading the blind,
So fish in Life’s Ocean young maidens and lads,
In spite of reformers brain-addled with fads;
’Tis a lottery-net that you cast in the deep,
And the fish that you catch, you can play with—
or keep;
But seek not Ohio, that science-struck State,
Where your soul’s not your own, where you can’t
take your mate
To the altar of Hymen, unless you both pass
A naked exam.: and obtain a first-class.
We lean to old fashions, the unions we guess
Where your bride has conferred the degree M.R.S.,
Are better than those who are shifty and free,
Where your mistress, yourself, and the world can’t
agree ;
And these are the things that they legislate for
In the State of Ohio ; they bar the front doo-
Of legalised unions, Love slips out the back
And fondles his Psyche unlicensed, alack ;
So let their strong Science do all that it may,
Twill only boom licence and free-love that way ;
Where the drunkards will mate with the dribbling
loons,
The phthisical pair with the cancerous coons,
Where healthier mortals like ship-rats will clear
From the tottering State and its wreckage so near.
Vic.
Banool.
The Rush to Klondyke.—A Voice
from Vancouver.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Been four days here, scraping up all the informa-
tion I can. Been introduced to miners of all
classes, from all parts—mining agents, experts,
practical miners, &c. Some come from Dawson
City and other parts of the Yukon. Met an old
schoolfellow here, who started from Vancouver
last August, with a party of 8, and 4 tons of
stores. Killed 21 horses in crossing the White
Pass. Reached Dawson City in three months;
staked out claims, and after two months’ work
had to leave for want of tucker. Got no gold out
in that time, and paid .£lOO for tucker on way
back, arriving in Seattle after nine months’ hard
toil and an expenditure of 8000 dollars. The
papers announced that they had returned with
7500 dollars of gold-dust, whereas they hadn’t a
color. This man is trying to sell his claim, but
has little hopes of doing so. The boom here is at
fever-heat; people rushing about madly, buying
stacks of stuff to take with them. Thousands
coming in every week from all parts of the world.
Worth being here to see the life ; reminds one of
an ant-hill when the ants have been stirred up.
Local people are very calm over it, but raking in
the dollars in hundreds of thousands. None of
them dream of going out—got their mines at their
own doors! and laughing in their sleeves
at the rest of us. A mining - engineer has
come back here with a report from Dawson,
but his account is so sober and truthful that the
newspapers would not publish it for fear of check-
ing the boom. He said there was work, so far,
for 4000 people, but that the remaining 2000 had
nothing to do, and that 50 per cent, of the people
were poorer than when they arrived. He says the
transportation-companies are doing their best to
get in enough tucker to keep alive the big mob
that are going in; but he cannot see what the
people will do when they get there. I do not
think there will be less than 150,000. Everything
here is in the hands of syndicates—even the press.
They are all working together to keep the boom
alive and bring as many people as possible to
spend their money here. On every street you are
stopped by someone who wants to drag you into
his store and force you to buy a Klondyke outfit.
No doubt that there is plenty of gold in British
Columbia, but it is a losing game to get it. There
is a man here who came from Klondyke a week
ago. Been working hard for three years, and only
made 50 dollars. I can hear of no one from here
who has done any good there. There will be a
great deal of gold from Klondyke this summer,
but only from the few good claims that were
taken up nearly two years ago. It is quite un-
true about the high wages on the Yukon. Difficult
to get work at any price. A party of my
shipmates are off to-morrow to the White Pass.
Going by an old ocean tramp converted into a
passenger-boat. Six of such have been wrecked
within the last few weeks. My mates spent
7500d01. on supplies, and have only money left to
pay their fare to Skagway, so I don’t know how
they will get back, or live there when their food is
done. They can only do surface-prospecting in
the summer, and have no chance of proving their
claims till this time next year. No one can know
if he has got anything in less than a year, except
in a few cases.
I don t think the danger to life on the journey
from Skagway to the Yukon is serious. 'The news
that hundreds of people were dying is quite un-
true. I don’t see the need of hurrying away at
once, in any case, as no one can travel past Lake
Bennett till-the ice breaks, which will be about
June. Ice on rivers very rough, with hummocks ;
cannot drag more than 1501 b. on a sledge. Thou-
sands camped at Lake Bennett, waiting for ice to
break; meanwhile, eating up their provisions.
Many thousands also camped at the Pass ; some
sitting on their luggage, crying in their helpless-
ness. No money to come back, and no money to
have their loads taken up the mountains by the
transportation-companies, who charge 200dol. a
ton. The rest of our fellows think they are going
to drag theirs up themselves, a little at a time.
This might possibly be done ; but, as it is 45 miles
to the lake, and same back, and this would have
to be travelled over at least six times, it means 540
miles, or two months’ work. The tucker they
would eat would be worth more than the cost of
having it packed over by the companies.
The much-talked-of Stewart and Pehhv Rivers
are not staked-out, and no one can get up them, as
the stream is too strong in summer, and in winter
the surface cannot be prospected because of the
snow and ice. Some men went a little way up
the Stewart and found rich gold, but their journey
was so slow (one mile per day) that their tucker
failed and they had to return after two days
there. Nothing can be done till the railway is
made and steam-launches built. The Stewart is
about 400 miles long, and cannot be reached across
the mountains. The opinion amongst candid
people is that this will be the most disastrous gold-
rush ever known.
KENNETH STEWART HUNGERFORD.
Vancouver, 9th March, 1898.
The Rescue.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were sitting on the lower deck, near the
stern of the little ferry steamer Kirribilli, chatting,
delighted at the coincidence of their meeting.
“ They two would wed to-morrow morn ” —or
rather to-morrow six months, say; and were in
the first delicious newness of it all. Huggable she
looked too: fresh as early peach-blossom, in her
summer muslin and big kiss-me-quick hat. And
manly he : broad-shouldered and strong-haired.
Her eyes wandered, after many minutes.
“Oh, Walter, what a dear little boat ! ” She
jumped up quickly to see better the little
“dingy” which, with white flashing sails and a
bright red crescent, was bent on cutting across the
ferry-boat’s bows in the very nick of time. Half
the passengers rose, too, and, manlike, obscured
the view.
“ Those stupid men! ” Standing on the seat, she
leaned far out, clutching a stanchion; her breeze-
blown skirts fluttering behind her, and affording
just a glimpse of a saucy bit of lace and a pair of
pretty ankles.
Did the steamer lurch ? She was in the water,
many feet astern ; her cry still in the air. There
was a shout from excited passengers, and Walter
Murray, in his shirt-sleeves, stood for a moment
on the bulwarks—
“ Mr. Murray had his coat off and was over the
side in an instant; with a few vigorous strokes he
reached the unfortunate lady, and supported her
till ...”
A roar of execration, a jarring, churning shudder
that shook the boat from stern to stem ; and a
dark rushing body from the upper deck came fly-
ing, clothes and all, through the air past Walter,
who still stood staring on the bulwark.
‘ ‘ Mr. Murray had his coat off and was over the
side in an instant.” His brain had over-shot
itself —was hours ahead.
“ Oh, Walter.” Her last sob reached him. His
brain cleared with a bound. He realised his dry
clothes; the green, white-flecked waves, the black
shoulders striving their utmost, but still so miser-
ably far from their goal; the sail that flew like
lightning past the backing steamer. He realised
the sudden stillness where the splashing hands
had been ; then long minutes afterwards the white-
wrapt, dripping figure handed up from the thwart
of the Sea Gull.
As, all in vain, the knowing ones pumped her
arms about, the crowd gave way in silence to a
man in shirt-sleeves with a frozen face, who
knelt down on the deck and watched an
even stiller one. When they reached the wharf
someone took his arm and led him to a seat; then
somebody came, he thought, and seemed to bother
—somebody he knew, named Murray—about his
name and address; and then he was left alone,
and sat on there on the hard wooden seat, while
ferry-boats came and went, and passengers eyed
him curiously.
He gave his evidence quite clearly at the inquest
offered no explanation. His best friends rather
ostentatiously didn’t cut him, but soon found a
wall more interesting. A wall will echo, anyway.
A fortnight after the accident he suddenly told
his father he had decided to give up the Law.
At the University he had been correctly lazy;
now he re-opened his books. When he’d mastered
the Integral Calculus fie took up Spherical Har-
monics. After two years’ study he sat for his
M.A. degree and won first-class honors and a gold
medal for mathematics. They say he'll be a Pro-
fessor some day, but I don’t think so. I’m medical
officer for a life-assurance society.
N.3.W.
S.K.
Aaron : “A clue to the true theory of
baldness. A horse that was to he exhibited at a
show had his tail braided, turned up on itself, and
secured with an india-rubber band Gin. from the
root for two or three days. In a few weeks almost
all the hair came out of the tail. This was due to
constriction of the blood vessels and starvation of
the hair follicles. The arteries supplying the
scalp are so situated that a tight hat compresses
them and causes slow starvation of the hair fol-
licles. Women escape baldness because they don’t
wear tight hats. In man the hat pressure is in
front on the frontal arteries, and it is in tho
region supplied by these arteries that baldness
generally appears.”
